Allah's Existence
beginner
4 min
The Impossibility of Spontaneous Generation Proves a Wise Creator
Maturidi argues that things cannot come from nothing; their origin shows a wise Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the origin of complex beings, like humans, cannot be explained by natural processes alone, and therefore proves a wise Creator.

Core Idea
Natural processes alone cannot produce humans; a wise Creator is necessary.

Maturidi presents a powerful argument against spontaneous generation. He shows that the development of a human from a sperm, the movements of stars, and even the mixture of light and darkness all require a wise decree beyond the materials involved. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Understand the opening premise
Maturidi begins by noting that creating something from nothing is beyond human capacity, yet everyone affirms it for God. This sets up the problem: if humans cannot create from nothing, how do things come to be?
3
Examine the examples
He lists several phenomena: embryological development, stellar influences, the mixture of light and darkness. Each is claimed to occur by nature, but Maturidi argues they are beyond mere materials.
For instance, in sperm, food, and womb there is no trace of human qualities like reason or sight.
4
Reflect on the final analogy
Maturidi argues that if natures and substances of good and evil were left to themselves, no creation would result. Therefore, claiming existence arises spontaneously is even more inconceivable.
This reinforces the need for a wise, knowing decree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن لم يكن في خلق الشيء من غير شيء إلا خروجه من وسع الخلق وارتفاعه عن التصور، فلا أحد امتنع عن القول بتحقيق مثله؛ لأن نشوء الجسم وكونه في الأرحام بالطبائع، وحدوثه بحركات النجوم، أو خروج العالم عن هذا الطبع، وامتزاج النور والظلمة ثم التباين، فهذا كله خارج عن الوجه الذي ذكر. على أن حقيقة كل شيء من تأمله كذلك يجده؛ لأنه ليس في النطفة ولا في جميع الأغذية ولا في الأرحام شيء من معاني البشر، ثم مما له من العقل والسمع والنظر؛ فإنما ذلك خارج عن ذلك بتقدير عليم حكيم. وكذلك جميع الطبائع المختلفة أو جواهر الخير والشر لو خُلّى بينها وبين عملها ما ظهر بها جوهر ولا يمكن بها خلق فالقول بالكون بمثله أبعد عن التصور في العقل. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then if there is not in the creation of a thing from nothing except its being beyond the capacity of creation and elevated above conception, then no one refrains from affirming the realization of the like of it; because the arising of the body and its being in the wombs is by natures, and its occurrence by movements of the stars, or the departure of the world from this nature, and the mixture of light and darkness then separation—all this is outside the aspect mentioned. Indeed, the reality of everything upon reflection finds it thus; because there is not in the sperm-drop nor in all foods nor in the wombs anything of the meanings of mankind, nor also of what it has of reason, hearing, and sight; rather that is outside that by the decree of a Knowing, Wise One. And likewise all the different natures or substances of good and evil—if left between them and their action, no substance would appear through them, nor would creation be possible by them; so the statement of existence by the like of it is even further from conception in the intellect. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is an argument from the impossibility of spontaneous generation and the need for a creator. Maturidi begins by noting that creating something from nothing is beyond human capacity and conception, yet everyone affirms its possibility for God. He then gives examples: the development of a body in the womb via natures, the occurrence of events by stellar movements, the mixture of light and darkness leading to separation—all these processes are beyond the mere materials involved. For instance, in the sperm, food, and womb, there is no trace of human qualities like reason, hearing, or sight; these come from a Knowing, Wise Decree (تقدير عليم حكيم). Similarly, if different natures or substances of good and evil were left to themselves, no substance or creation would result. Therefore, claiming existence arises spontaneously is even more inconceivable.

Key Terms
خلق من غير شيء
طبائع
تقدير
عليم حكيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think natural processes alone suffice to generate complex beings.
Correction: Maturidi shows that materials lack the qualities of the final product, so an external wise agent is required.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi give as an example of something that cannot come into existence without a wise creator?
A) The movement of stars
B) The formation of a human from sperm
C) The mixture of light and darkness
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of spontaneous generation demonstrates that all existence depends on a wise, powerful Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that your own reason, hearing, and sight are not products of the sperm or your mother's womb but a direct decree from Alim Hakeem, how does that challenge your self-perception of being self-sufficient? What change does this demand in your daily gratitude to God?
?
If you truly believed that the world and everything in it cannot arise from natural processes alone, would you still act as if your successes are due to your own effort? How can you align your heart with this truth in your next decision?
?
Reflect on a time when you attributed something good in your life to 'luck' or 'nature'. How does Maturidi's argument expose the emptiness of that attribution? What will you do to correct that belief from now on?

The Embryo: A Proof of a Wise Creator
Maturidi presents two arguments: from embryonic development and from the impossibility of substances creating themselves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the development of the human embryo from simple components demonstrates the necessity of a wise, knowing Creator.

Core Idea
The embryo's emergence from lifeless matter proves creation requires a wise designer.

Imam Maturidi begins with an observation everyone can make: the human being comes from a sperm-drop that contains no trace of intellect, hearing, or sight. Yet these complex faculties appear. He challenges the view that nature alone could produce them, arguing that without a determining power, such order would be impossible. This passage lays the foundation for a rational proof of God's existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style and the two distinct arguments.
2
Identify the two arguments
The passage contains two proofs: one from the embryo and one from the impossibility of substances creating themselves. Identify where each begins and ends.
3
Reflect on the logic
Ask yourself why Maturidi considers the absence of human attributes in the sperm and womb as evidence for a Creator. How does this refute purely naturalistic explanations?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن حقيقة كل شيء من تأمله كذلك يجده؛ لأنه ليس في النطفة ولا في جميع الأغذية ولا في الأرحام شيء من معاني البشر، ثم مما له من العقل والسمع والنظر؛ فإنما ذلك خارج عن ذلك بتقدير عليم حكيم. وكذلك جميع الطبائع المختلفة أو جواهر الخير والشر لو خُلّى بينها وبين عملها ما ظهر بها جوهر ولا يمكن بها خلق فالقول بالكون بمثله أبعد عن التصور في العقل. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقد بينا وجه الحكمة في خلق الجواهر المختلفة، وأن فعل الله لا يوصف بذلك، وأن إنشاءها على ما عليه من قبح القبيح وحسن الحسن هو معنى الحكمة ووضع كل شيء موضعه، وأن الله تعالى إذ لم يخلق لحاجات نفسه وإنما خلق بذاته. إنه خالق ليكون الخلق الذي ركب فيهم العقول وجعلهم أهل المعرفة بالنعم والبلايا يُمتحنون بوضع كل شيء موضعه والقيام بالشكر لما أنعم عليهم؛ بأن جعل لهم جميع الخلائق على اختلاف جواهرهم أدلة وعبرا ومحنة وابتلاء بمعاداة جواهر وموالاة أخرى؛ وليعرفوا كيفية الاتقاء ووجه الحذر وما فيه الرعب ووجوه المبادرة في ذلك للعواقب المحمودة في العقول واتقاء الآخرين لمكروهة فيها؛ بما عاينوا من مختلف الجواهر والأحوال في حق الترغيب والترهيب؛ ليكون الوعد والوعيد مقدرًا عن الحس والعيان، إذ ذلك طريق المعارف، وبه يوصل إلى درك النهايات. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Indeed, the reality of every thing, whoever reflects on it, finds it so; because there is not in the sperm-drop nor in all the foods nor in the wombs anything of the meanings of the human being, then what he has of intellect, hearing, and sight—that is only beyond that by the determination of a knowing, wise (God). And likewise all the different natures or the substances of good and evil, if left between themselves and their action, no substance would appear from them, nor would creation be possible by them. So the statement that things come to be by something like that is farther from conceptualization in the intellect. And there is no power except with God. And we have explained the aspect of wisdom in creating the different substances, and that the action of God is not described by that, and that His bringing them into being in the state they are of the ugliness of the ugly and the beauty of the beautiful is the meaning of wisdom and placing everything in its place, and that God—exalted—did not create for the needs of His self but rather created by His essence. He is the Creator so that the creation, whom He has composed intellects in them and made them knowers of blessings and trials, are tested by placing everything in its place and performing gratitude for what He has bestowed upon them; by making for them all creatures, despite the difference of their substances, as proofs, lessons, trial, and test by befriending some substances and opposing others; and so that they know the manner of piety, the aspect of caution, what contains fear, and the aspects of promptness in that for praiseworthy consequences in intellects, and avoidance of others' disliked (consequences) in them; by what they have witnessed of the different substances and states regarding encouragement and warning; so that the promise and threat are based on sense and observation, for that is the way of knowledges, and by it one reaches the comprehension of ends. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part proof and explanation. First, Maturidi presents an argument from the development of the human embryo: in the sperm, food, and womb, there is nothing of human meaning (intellect, hearing, sight), yet these emerge by the determination of a wise, knowing Creator. This shows that creation cannot be from the natural elements themselves. Second, he argues that if different natures or substances of good and evil were left to themselves, they would not produce any substance or creation. Therefore, claiming that things come into being by such natural causes is even more inconceivable. Third, he reaffirms that the wisdom in creating diverse substances lies in placing each thing in its proper place, and that God did not create out of need but by His essence. Fourth, he explains the purpose of creation: to test humans who are given intellect and knowledge of blessings and trials, so that they are tested by placing things in their proper order and showing gratitude. God made all creatures as proofs and tests, so that humans learn piety, caution, and promptness for good outcomes, and avoid disliked outcomes, through witnessing different substances and states. This establishes that divine promise and threat are based on sense and observation, which is the path to knowledge and the comprehension of ends.

Key Terms
نطفة
تقدير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The embryo argument is just an argument from ignorance.
Correction: Maturidi is not saying 'we don't know how it happens, so God did it.' He is saying that the observed components contain none of the final characteristics, so the transformation requires an external intelligent agent — a rational inference based on causality.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the development of the human embryo from a sperm-drop demonstrate?
A) That the embryo grows by itself.
B) That a wise Creator is necessary because human faculties like intellect and sight are not present in the sperm.
C) That the mother's womb provides all necessary qualities.
D) That the embryo is a product of chance.

Key Takeaway
The embryo's development from a simple drop to a complex human proves that a wise, knowing Creator is responsible for bringing about qualities that were not present in the original matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the faculties you take for granted—hearing, sight, intellect. When you reflect that they were not present in the sperm-drop or food, do you truly acknowledge that they are gifts from a wise God, not products of random matter? How does this acknowledgment change your gratitude today?
?
If left to yourself, your own nature cannot produce any good or virtue. When you see good deeds or noble qualities in yourself, do you attribute them to your own effort or to the determination of the Wise? Which attribution makes you more humble?
?
The passage ends with 'there is no power except with God.' In what area of your life are you still acting as if your own power alone is sufficient? What would it mean to truly rely on God's power in that area?

The Wisdom Behind Creating Diverse Substances
Maturidi explains the wisdom of creating diverse substances for testing and grounding promise and threat in sensory experience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the purpose behind God's creation of different substances: to test humans and make divine promise and threat perceptible.

Core Idea
God created diverse substances as a test, grounding reward and punishment in sensory experience.

After establishing that a Creator is necessary, Maturidi now explains why the Creator makes substances different in nature, including good and evil. He shows that this diversity is not arbitrary but serves a profound purpose: it allows humans to be tested, to learn piety, and to perceive divine promise and threat through actual observation. This passage reveals the rational basis for moral responsibility and eschatology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on wisdom and purpose
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice how Maturidi connects the creation of diverse substances to testing and sensory knowledge of promise and threat.
This passage is longer and more explanatory.
2
Identify the two purposes
First, Maturidi says God created so that humans are tested by placing everything in its place and showing gratitude. Second, He made different substances so that promise and threat are based on sense and observation. Find these two points.
3
Reflect on the role of senses
Why does Maturidi emphasize that divine promise and threat must be based on 'sense and observation'? How does this relate to the way humans acquire knowledge?
This connects to his epistemology: sensory experience is the foundation of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن حقيقة كل شيء من تأمله كذلك يجده؛ لأنه ليس في النطفة ولا في جميع الأغذية ولا في الأرحام شيء من معاني البشر، ثم مما له من العقل والسمع والنظر؛ فإنما ذلك خارج عن ذلك بتقدير عليم حكيم. وكذلك جميع الطبائع المختلفة أو جواهر الخير والشر لو خُلّى بينها وبين عملها ما ظهر بها جوهر ولا يمكن بها خلق فالقول بالكون بمثله أبعد عن التصور في العقل. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقد بينا وجه الحكمة في خلق الجواهر المختلفة، وأن فعل الله لا يوصف بذلك، وأن إنشاءها على ما عليه من قبح القبيح وحسن الحسن هو معنى الحكمة ووضع كل شيء موضعه، وأن الله تعالى إذ لم يخلق لحاجات نفسه وإنما خلق بذاته. إنه خالق ليكون الخلق الذي ركب فيهم العقول وجعلهم أهل المعرفة بالنعم والبلايا يُمتحنون بوضع كل شيء موضعه والقيام بالشكر لما أنعم عليهم؛ بأن جعل لهم جميع الخلائق على اختلاف جواهرهم أدلة وعبرا ومحنة وابتلاء بمعاداة جواهر وموالاة أخرى؛ وليعرفوا كيفية الاتقاء ووجه الحذر وما فيه الرعب ووجوه المبادرة في ذلك للعواقب المحمودة في العقول واتقاء الآخرين لمكروهة فيها؛ بما عاينوا من مختلف الجواهر والأحوال في حق الترغيب والترهيب؛ ليكون الوعد والوعيد مقدرًا عن الحس والعيان، إذ ذلك طريق المعارف، وبه يوصل إلى درك النهايات. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Indeed, the reality of every thing, whoever reflects on it, finds it so; because there is not in the sperm-drop nor in all the foods nor in the wombs anything of the meanings of the human being, then what he has of intellect, hearing, and sight—that is only beyond that by the determination of a knowing, wise (God). And likewise all the different natures or the substances of good and evil, if left between themselves and their action, no substance would appear from them, nor would creation be possible by them. So the statement that things come to be by something like that is farther from conceptualization in the intellect. And there is no power except with God. And we have explained the aspect of wisdom in creating the different substances, and that the action of God is not described by that, and that His bringing them into being in the state they are of the ugliness of the ugly and the beauty of the beautiful is the meaning of wisdom and placing everything in its place, and that God—exalted—did not create for the needs of His self but rather created by His essence. He is the Creator so that the creation, whom He has composed intellects in them and made them knowers of blessings and trials, are tested by placing everything in its place and performing gratitude for what He has bestowed upon them; by making for them all creatures, despite the difference of their substances, as proofs, lessons, trial, and test by befriending some substances and opposing others; and so that they know the manner of piety, the aspect of caution, what contains fear, and the aspects of promptness in that for praiseworthy consequences in intellects, and avoidance of others' disliked (consequences) in them; by what they have witnessed of the different substances and states regarding encouragement and warning; so that the promise and threat are based on sense and observation, for that is the way of knowledges, and by it one reaches the comprehension of ends. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part proof and explanation. First, Maturidi presents an argument from the development of the human embryo: in the sperm, food, and womb, there is nothing of human meaning (intellect, hearing, sight), yet these emerge by the determination of a wise, knowing Creator. This shows that creation cannot be from the natural elements themselves. Second, he argues that if different natures or substances of good and evil were left to themselves, they would not produce any substance or creation. Therefore, claiming that things come into being by such natural causes is even more inconceivable. Third, he reaffirms that the wisdom in creating diverse substances lies in placing each thing in its proper place, and that God did not create out of need but by His essence. Fourth, he explains the purpose of creation: to test humans who are given intellect and knowledge of blessings and trials, so that they are tested by placing things in their proper order and showing gratitude. God made all creatures as proofs and tests, so that humans learn piety, caution, and promptness for good outcomes, and avoid disliked outcomes, through witnessing different substances and states. This establishes that divine promise and threat are based on sense and observation, which is the path to knowledge and the comprehension of ends.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The existence of evil substances contradicts divine wisdom.
Correction: Maturidi explains that creating both good and evil substances is part of divine wisdom, as they serve purposes such as testing and teaching moral discernment.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the purpose of creating diverse substances (good and evil)?
A) To confuse humans.
B) To test humans and establish divine promise and threat based on sensory experience.
C) To entertain God.
D) To make the world beautiful.

Key Takeaway
God created diverse substances to test humanity and to ground the concepts of reward and punishment in observable reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter something in your life that appears ugly, painful, or unfair, do you truly believe it has been placed there by God's perfect wisdom? Or does your heart resist, questioning His purpose? What specific situation are you currently struggling to accept as part of the test He designed for you? And what would it mean for you to see it not as meaningless suffering but as a necessary means to learn piety and caution, and to draw you closer to His promise?
?
The passage says that God made all creatures—the ones you befriend and the ones you oppose—as proofs and tests. Consider the people or circumstances you instinctively dislike or avoid. How might they be precisely the tools God has chosen to refine your character and test your gratitude? Are you willingly engaging in that test, or are you running from it? What would change if you saw each difficult relationship as an 'opposing substance' given by God for your ultimate good?
?
The divine promise and threat, Maturidi argues, are grounded in what you see and feel. That means your everyday sensory experiences—whether ease or hardship—are not random; they are direct signs from God calling you to respond. When you experience pleasure, do you immediately turn it into gratitude and awareness of His promise? When you face fear or pain, do you let it warn you and drive you toward piety? Or do you numb yourself and miss the lesson? What one change will you make today to read the signs of His promise and threat in your daily life?

The Fallacy of Denying Creation from Nothing Using Imagination
Maturidi presents the absurd logical consequence of denying creation ex nihilo based on imagination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is logically invalid to deny the creation of something from nothing based on the inability to imagine it.

Core Idea
Denying creation because it's unimaginable leads to absurd consequences.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi refutes the claim that creation from nothing is impossible because it cannot be conceived in the imagination. He shows that this argument would also allow anyone with defective senses to deny sensory evidence, and would justify rejecting all unseen realities. This would refute not only rational thought but also the beliefs of other groups, such as the Magians, who blindly follow their predecessors.

Guided Path
1
Read the hypothetical argument
Read the passage and identify the conditional statement Maturidi presents: 'if it were permissible to deny a thing's coming from nothing by what is not conceivable in the imagination...'.
Pay attention to the logical structure: if P then Q.
2
Notice the absurd consequence
Maturidi shows that this would allow anyone with defective senses to deny sensory perception and deny all unseen things. This is an absurd outcome that no rational person would accept.
3
Understand the refutation of the Magians
Maturidi points out that this argument refutes the Magians and others who blindly follow their predecessors without rational basis.
The Magians are Zoroastrians who believed in dualism and rejected creation ex nihilo.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو جاز إنكار الشيء لا من شيء بما لا يتصور في الوهم لجاز لكل مؤوف الحاسة إنكار ما يدرك به إذ هو غير مدرك، إنكار كل غائب لم يبلغه الحاسة. وفي ذلك نقض المجوسية وغيرهم، إذ هم جميعا اتبعوا أوائلهم. ثم التصور في الوهم تقديره مما تقع عليه الحاسة إذا ارتفعت، فيتصور حال وقوع الحاسة في وهمه أو يقدّر مثله في الوهم. ثم الله سبحانه لم يُعرف من طريق الحواس ولا له مثال في المعروف، بطل التقدير به. ثم الأصل أن التصور في الوهم هو علم الحس، أو في علم الحس دليل لزوم العلم بما لم يُحِس، لأنه يعرفه. إذ كل ذي حس جاهل بمائية الحس وكيفيته، فلزم ذلك في كل من هو كذلك، فيجب كون الحواس بمن يعرف حقائقها وينشئها، على ما يرى أهل الحواس أن الذي أنشأها لا يُحتمل إدراكه بالحواس؛ إذ كل ذي حاسة جاهل بما عليه أحواله وعاجز عن احتمال وسعه ما فسد منه، فأوجب ذا أن وراء هذا عليم حكيم، لا يحتمل ما احتمل المحسوس؛ إذ لو جاز واحتمل لم يحتمل كون المحسوس به، كما لم يحتمل بأمثالنا. وبالله العصمة والنجاة.
And if it were permissible to deny a thing's coming from nothing by what is not conceivable in the imagination (wahm), then it would be permissible for every defective in sense to deny what is perceived by it, since it is not perceived, (and) the denial of every absent thing that has not reached his sense. And in that is the refutation of the Magians and others, for they all followed their predecessors. Then the conceptualization in the imagination is its estimation of what the sense falls upon when it is removed, so he conceptualizes the state of the sense's occurrence in his imagination or estimates its like in the imagination. Then since God—exalted—is not known by way of the senses and has no likeness in the known, the estimation by it (imagination) becomes invalid. Then the principle is that conceptualization in the imagination is knowledge of sense, or in the knowledge of sense there is a proof of the necessity of knowledge of what one has not sensed, because he knows it (by analogy). Indeed, every possessor of sense is ignorant of the essence (mahiyya) of sense and its modality (kayfiyya), so that is necessary for everyone who is like that. Therefore, it is necessary that the senses belong to one who knows their realities and creates them, according to what the people of sense see: that the one who created them cannot be perceived by the senses. For every possessor of sense is ignorant of his own states and incapable of bearing the capacity of what has corrupted from him. So this necessitates that behind this (world) is a knowing, wise (God), who cannot bear what the sensed bears; for if it were possible and he bore it, then the existence of the sensed by him would not be possible, just as it is not possible by our likes. And with God is protection and salvation.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the denial of creation from nothing using the limitations of imagination (wahm) and senses. Maturidi argues that if one could deny a thing's creation from nothing simply because it is not conceivable in the imagination, then it would also be permissible for someone with defective senses to deny sensory perceptions, and to deny all unseen things. This would refute the Magians and others who follow their forebears blindly. He then explains that imagination is based on sense perception: it estimates things that have been sensed. Since God is not known through the senses and has no likeness in the known world, it is invalid to use imagination to deny His creative act. The principle is that conceptualization in imagination is tied to sense knowledge, and sense knowledge actually guides one to knowledge of what is not sensed, because the senses themselves testify to a creator beyond them. Every person with senses is ignorant of the essence and modality of their own senses, so they cannot have created them. The senses themselves indicate a creator who knows their realities and brings them into existence. This creator cannot be perceived by the senses; in fact, He is beyond the limitations of sensory perception. The conclusion is that there is a wise, knowing being behind the universe, who is unlike created things. The passage ends with a prayer for protection.

Key Terms

حاسة
مؤوف الحاسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if something is unimaginable, it cannot be real.
Correction: Maturidi shows that this reasoning leads to absurdity and cannot be applied to matters beyond sensory experience like divine creation.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say would be permissible if denying creation based on lack of imagination were allowed?
A) Denying only the existence of God.
B) Denying what is perceived by someone with defective senses and denying all unseen things.
C) Denying the existence of the Magians.
D) Denying only abstract concepts.

Key Takeaway
Denying creation ex nihilo because it is unimaginable is a flawed argument that leads to absurd consequences.

?

?

The Nature and Limits of Imagination
Maturidi defines imagination and explains its dependency on sense perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how imagination functions and why it cannot be used to judge matters beyond sensory experience.

Core Idea
Imagination works by reconstructing sensory data; it cannot conceive what has no sensory basis.

After establishing the fallacy of denying creation based on imagination, Maturidi now explains what imagination actually is. He defines it as the estimation of what the senses have experienced, or the creation of mental images based on sense perceptions. Since God is not known through the senses and has no likeness in the known world, imagination cannot estimate or conceive Him. Thus, the argument that creation from nothing is unimaginable is invalid because it misapplies imagination beyond its proper domain.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of imagination
Read the passage where Maturidi defines conceptualization in imagination as an estimation based on sense experience.
Note the phrase 'تقديره مما تقع عليه الحاسة'.
2
Understand the dependency on senses
Imagination works by recalling or recombining sensory impressions. It cannot create something entirely novel without sensory basis.
3
Apply to God
Since God is not known through the senses and has no likeness, imagination cannot be used to deny His creative act.
This is the key conclusion: تقدير الوهم باطل في حق الله.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو جاز إنكار الشيء لا من شيء بما لا يتصور في الوهم لجاز لكل مؤوف الحاسة إنكار ما يدرك به إذ هو غير مدرك، إنكار كل غائب لم يبلغه الحاسة. وفي ذلك نقض المجوسية وغيرهم، إذ هم جميعا اتبعوا أوائلهم. ثم التصور في الوهم تقديره مما تقع عليه الحاسة إذا ارتفعت، فيتصور حال وقوع الحاسة في وهمه أو يقدّر مثله في الوهم. ثم الله سبحانه لم يُعرف من طريق الحواس ولا له مثال في المعروف، بطل التقدير به. ثم الأصل أن التصور في الوهم هو علم الحس، أو في علم الحس دليل لزوم العلم بما لم يُحِس، لأنه يعرفه. إذ كل ذي حس جاهل بمائية الحس وكيفيته، فلزم ذلك في كل من هو كذلك، فيجب كون الحواس بمن يعرف حقائقها وينشئها، على ما يرى أهل الحواس أن الذي أنشأها لا يُحتمل إدراكه بالحواس؛ إذ كل ذي حاسة جاهل بما عليه أحواله وعاجز عن احتمال وسعه ما فسد منه، فأوجب ذا أن وراء هذا عليم حكيم، لا يحتمل ما احتمل المحسوس؛ إذ لو جاز واحتمل لم يحتمل كون المحسوس به، كما لم يحتمل بأمثالنا. وبالله العصمة والنجاة.
And if it were permissible to deny a thing's coming from nothing by what is not conceivable in the imagination (wahm), then it would be permissible for every defective in sense to deny what is perceived by it, since it is not perceived, (and) the denial of every absent thing that has not reached his sense. And in that is the refutation of the Magians and others, for they all followed their predecessors. Then the conceptualization in the imagination is its estimation of what the sense falls upon when it is removed, so he conceptualizes the state of the sense's occurrence in his imagination or estimates its like in the imagination. Then since God—exalted—is not known by way of the senses and has no likeness in the known, the estimation by it (imagination) becomes invalid. Then the principle is that conceptualization in the imagination is knowledge of sense, or in the knowledge of sense there is a proof of the necessity of knowledge of what one has not sensed, because he knows it (by analogy). Indeed, every possessor of sense is ignorant of the essence (mahiyya) of sense and its modality (kayfiyya), so that is necessary for everyone who is like that. Therefore, it is necessary that the senses belong to one who knows their realities and creates them, according to what the people of sense see: that the one who created them cannot be perceived by the senses. For every possessor of sense is ignorant of his own states and incapable of bearing the capacity of what has corrupted from him. So this necessitates that behind this (world) is a knowing, wise (God), who cannot bear what the sensed bears; for if it were possible and he bore it, then the existence of the sensed by him would not be possible, just as it is not possible by our likes. And with God is protection and salvation.

The Argument from the Senses to a Transcendent Creator
Maturidi argues from the ignorance of the senses to the existence of a transcendent creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the limitations of sensory knowledge point to the existence of a wise creator beyond sensory perception.

Core Idea
Our ignorance of our own senses proves we did not create them; therefore, they must come from a transcendent creator.

Having established that imagination is limited to sensory experience, Maturidi now uses the senses themselves as evidence for God's existence. He argues that every person with senses is ignorant of the essence (māhiyya) and modality (kayfiyya) of their own senses. This ignorance shows that we did not create our senses, and they cannot be explained by our own capacities. Therefore, they must belong to a creator who knows their realities and brings them into existence. This creator cannot be perceived by the senses; indeed, He is beyond sensory limitations. The conclusion is that behind the universe is a knowing, wise God, unlike any created thing.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument about ignorance of senses
Read the passage stating that every possessor of sense is ignorant of the essence and modality of sense itself.
The Arabic terms: مائية الحس وكيفيته.
2
Understand the logical necessity
Since we are ignorant of our senses, we could not have created them. Therefore, they must be created by someone who knows them fully.
3
Note the creator's transcendence
Maturidi says that the creator cannot be perceived by the senses; He is beyond sensory limitations.
This aligns with divine transcendence.
4
Conclusion: a wise God exists
The argument concludes that behind the world is a knowing, wise God, unlike any created being.
The passage ends with a prayer.

Refuting Denial of the Unseen by Imagination
Maturidi refutes the argument that denies the unseen because it cannot be imagined.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying the unseen based on lack of sensory imagination is a flawed argument, according to Maturidi.

Core Idea
Denying the unseen because it cannot be imagined destroys all knowledge.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi addresses a skeptical argument: that we should not accept the existence of anything that cannot be imagined. He shows that this would lead to absurd consequences, such as denying clear sensory perceptions. He then explains how imagination itself is based on the senses, and since God is beyond senses, any attempt to imagine Him is invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice the logical structure: a hypothetical, its consequence, and a conclusion about imagination.
The passage uses technical terms like 'al-wahm' (imagination) and 'al-hawas' (senses).
2
Identify the absurd consequence
Maturidi sets up a conditional: if denying something just because it cannot be imagined were allowed, then any deficient sense could deny its own perceptions and all unseen things. This would lead to complete skepticism.
3
Understand the refutation of imagination-based denial
Maturidi then explains that imagination is always derived from sensory experience. Since God has no sensory example, imagining Him is invalid. This refutes the original premise.
Notice the key phrase: 'batala al-taqdir bihi' (estimation by Him is invalid).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو جاز إنكار الشيء لا من شيء بما لا يتصور في الوهم لجاز لكل مؤف " الحاسة إنكار ما يدرك بها إذ هو غير مدرك، إنكار كل غائب لم يبلغه الحاسة. وفي ذلك نقض المجوسية وغيرهم، إذ هم جميعا اتبعوا أوائلهم . ثم التصور في الوهم تقديره مما تقع عليه الحاسة إذا ارتفعت، فيتصور حال وقوع الحاسة في وهمه أو يقدّر مثله في الوهم. ثم الله سبحانه لم يُعرف من طريق الحواس ولا له مثال في المعروف، بطل التقدير به.
ثم الأصل أن التصور في الوهم هو علم الحس، أو في علم الحس دليل لزوم العلم بما [ ٩٠ظ] لم يُحِس، لأنه يعرفه . إذ / كل ذي حس جاهل بمائية الحس وكيفيته، فلزم ذلك في كل من هو كذلك، فيجب كون الحواس بمن يعرف حقائقها وينشئها، على ما يرى أهل الحواس أن الذي أنشأها لا يُحتمل إدراكه بالحواس ؛ إذ كل ذي حاسة جاهل بما عليه أحواله وعاجز عن احتمال وسعه ما فسد منه، فأوجب ذا أن وراء هذا عليم حكيم، لا يحتمل ما احتمل المحسوس ؛ إذ لو جاز واحتمل لم يحتمل كون المحسوس به ، كما لم يحتمل بأمثالنا . وبالله العصمة والنجاة.
And if it were permissible to deny something from nothing that cannot be imagined (al-wahm), then for every deficient [in] sense it would be permissible to deny what he perceives by it, since it is not perceived, [and] to deny every unseen that has not reached the sense. In that is the refutation of the Magians and others, since they all followed their predecessors. Then imagining (al-tasawwur) in the imagination (al-wahm) is its estimation from what the sense falls upon when it is removed, so he imagines the state of the sense's falling in his imagination or estimates its like in the imagination. Then Allah—glorified is He—is not known from the path of the senses, nor does He have an example in the known, so estimation by Him is invalid. Then the principle is that imagining in the imagination is the knowledge of sense, or in the knowledge of sense is the evidence of the necessity of knowledge of what [he] has not sensed, because he knows it. Since every possessor of sense is ignorant of the essence of sense and its modality, so that is necessary for everyone who is like that. Thus it is necessary that the senses [point to] one who knows their realities and creates them, according to what the people of sense see: that the one who created them cannot be perceived by the senses. Since every possessor of sense is ignorant of his own states and incapable of bearing the capacity of what has become corrupted from him, so this necessitates that behind this there is an All-Knowing, Wise One, who cannot bear what the sensible bears. For if it were permissible and possible, then the existence of the sensible by it would not be possible, just as it is not possible by our likes. And with Allah is protection and deliverance.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument that would deny the existence of anything not subject to sensory imagination. The author argues that if accepting such denial were permissible, then anyone could deny clear sensory perceptions, which is absurd. He then explains that imagination (al-wahm) is always derived from sensory experience: when a sense is removed, one imagines the state when it was present. Since God is not known through the senses and has no analogy in the created world, any attempt to imagine Him is invalid. The author then establishes a deeper point: all sensory beings are ignorant of the true nature of their own senses, which points to a Creator who knows and creates the senses. This Creator cannot be perceived by the senses, because every sensory being is limited and fallible. Therefore, reason necessitates that behind the sensory world is an All-Knowing, Wise Being who is beyond sensory attributes. The argument proceeds logically: if we accept that sensory beings cannot fully understand their own senses, we must posit a transcendent cause.

Key Terms
al-wahm (imagination)
al-hawas (senses)
al-gha'ib (unseen)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Something must be imaginable to be real.
Correction: Maturidi shows that imagination is based on the senses, so it cannot apply to God, but He is still real. Denying the unseen based on lack of imagination is invalid.

Key Takeaway

?
This passage warns against denying things simply because they cannot be imagined. Is there any truth in your faith that you have secretly rejected or downplayed because it seems 'unimaginable'? Confess that to Allah now and resolve to accept it fully based on His word.

The Sensory Argument for a Transcendent Creator
Maturidi argues from the limitation of senses to a transcendent Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the limitations of the senses point to a Creator beyond sensory perception, according to Maturidi.

Core Idea
Our ignorance of our own senses proves there is a Creator who knows them.

After refuting the denial of the unseen, Maturidi now builds a positive argument for God's existence. He establishes a principle: imagination and sense knowledge are linked. Because every sensory being is ignorant of the true nature of its own senses, this points to a Creator who knows and creates them. This Creator must be beyond sensory perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the logical steps: the principle about imagination, the ignorance of the senses, and the conclusion.
The passage contains the key phrase 'كل ذي حس جاهل بمائية الحس وكيفيته' (every possessor of sense is ignorant of the essence and modality of sense).
2
Understand the principle
Maturidi states that imagination is based on sense knowledge, or sense knowledge provides evidence for knowledge of the unseen. This connects sensory experience to what lies beyond.
3
Trace the argument to the Creator
Because we are ignorant of the nature of our senses, and the senses themselves are corruptible, there must be a Wise Creator who knows their realities and is not subject to sensory limitations. This Creator is beyond sensory perception.
Notice the term 'sani'' (Creator) and the conclusion 'وراء هذا عليم حكيم' (behind this is an All-Knowing, Wise One).

ثم الأصل أن التصور في الوهم هو علم الحس، أو في علم الحس دليل لزوم العلم بما [ ٩٠ظ] لم يُحِس، لأنه يعرفه . إذ / كل ذي حس جاهل بمائية الحس وكيفيته، فلزم ذلك في كل من هو كذلك، فيجب كون الحواس بمن يعرف حقائقها وينشئها، على ما يرى أهل الحواس أن الذي أنشأها لا يُحتمل إدراكه بالحواس ؛ إذ كل ذي حاسة جاهل بما عليه أحواله وعاجز عن احتمال وسعه ما فسد منه، فأوجب ذا أن وراء هذا عليم حكيم، لا يحتمل ما احتمل المحسوس ؛ إذ لو جاز واحتمل لم يحتمل كون المحسوس به ، كما لم يحتمل بأمثالنا . وبالله العصمة والنجاة.
And if it were permissible to deny something from nothing that cannot be imagined (al-wahm), then for every deficient [in] sense it would be permissible to deny what he perceives by it, since it is not perceived, [and] to deny every unseen that has not reached the sense. In that is the refutation of the Magians and others, since they all followed their predecessors. Then imagining (al-tasawwur) in the imagination (al-wahm) is its estimation from what the sense falls upon when it is removed, so he imagines the state of the sense's falling in his imagination or estimates its like in the imagination. Then Allah—glorified is He—is not known from the path of the senses, nor does He have an example in the known, so estimation by Him is invalid. Then the principle is that imagining in the imagination is the knowledge of sense, or in the knowledge of sense is the evidence of the necessity of knowledge of what [he] has not sensed, because he knows it. Since every possessor of sense is ignorant of the essence of sense and its modality, so that is necessary for everyone who is like that. Thus it is necessary that the senses [point to] one who knows their realities and creates them, according to what the people of sense see: that the one who created them cannot be perceived by the senses. Since every possessor of sense is ignorant of his own states and incapable of bearing the capacity of what has become corrupted from him, so this necessitates that behind this there is an All-Knowing, Wise One, who cannot bear what the sensible bears. For if it were permissible and possible, then the existence of the sensible by it would not be possible, just as it is not possible by our likes. And with Allah is protection and deliverance.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument that would deny the existence of anything not subject to sensory imagination. The author argues that if accepting such denial were permissible, then anyone could deny clear sensory perceptions, which is absurd. He then explains that imagination (al-wahm) is always derived from sensory experience: when a sense is removed, one imagines the state when it was present. Since God is not known through the senses and has no analogy in the created world, any attempt to imagine Him is invalid. The author then establishes a deeper point: all sensory beings are ignorant of the true nature of their own senses, which points to a Creator who knows and creates the senses. This Creator cannot be perceived by the senses, because every sensory being is limited and fallible. Therefore, reason necessitates that behind the sensory world is an All-Knowing, Wise Being who is beyond sensory attributes. The argument proceeds logically: if we accept that sensory beings cannot fully understand their own senses, we must posit a transcendent cause.

Key Terms
al-wahm (imagination)
al-hawas (senses)
al-sani' (Creator)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Senses give full knowledge of reality, so God should be perceivable if He exists.
Correction: Maturidi shows our senses are limited and we are ignorant of their true nature, so the Creator must be beyond sensory perception.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the ignorance of the essence of sense prove?
A) That senses are unreliable
B) That there is an all-knowing Creator beyond the senses
C) That we cannot know anything
D) That the senses are self-sufficient

Key Takeaway
The limitation and ignorance inherent in sensory beings demonstrate the necessity of a transcendent, all-knowing Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You trust your eyes, ears, and touch every moment. Yet this passage says you are ignorant of their very essence. How does that realization change the way you see the world—and the One who made it? Will you let this ignorance drive you to acknowledge the Creator today?
?
If you have ever doubted God's existence because you cannot see Him, Maturidi argues that your limitation is exactly the proof. What will you do with this argument? Will you allow it to reshape your certainty?
?
This passage says every sensory being is 'incapable of bearing the capacity of what has become corrupted from him.' When you look at your own failings and corruption, do you see yourself as a sign pointing to a perfect, uncorrupted Creator? Or do you look away? Commit to one specific way you will let this truth humble you this week.

God's Commands and Prohibitions Grounded in Benefit and Harm
Maturidi argues that God's creation of benefit and harm necessitates commands and prohibitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational argument that God's creation of benefit and harm implies the necessity of divine commands and prohibitions for the creatures' welfare.

Core Idea
Benefit and harm in creation point to divine guidance through commands and prohibitions.

Maturidi presents a deeply rational argument: since God created the world with a clear distinction between beneficial and harmful things, and made every substance capable of producing pleasure or pain, it cannot be without purpose. That purpose is to guide humans through threats and promises, and then through commands and prohibitions that align with the very nature of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal English translation. Notice the repetitive structure starting with 'وبعد' to mark the two parts of the argument.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the two stages of the argument
First, Maturidi argues that creation into harmful and beneficial, with susceptibility to pain/pleasure, implies consequences (threats and promises) to complete desire and fear. Second, he argues that God's creation of benefits and harms for others, despite His self-sufficiency, logically leads to commands and prohibitions.
These two stages are connected by the idea of purpose in creation.
3
Notice the role of God's self-sufficiency
Maturidi emphasizes that God has no need of anything (غنى). Therefore, His commands and prohibitions are not for His own benefit but for the benefit of the creatures.
This refutes the misconception that God commands for His own advantage.
4
Reflect on the rational grounding of morality
Consider how this argument ties moral obligation directly to the structure of creation. Right and wrong are not arbitrary but rooted in what genuinely harms or benefits humans.
This is a foundational principle in Maturidi theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإنه إذ جعل الخلق قسمين ضارًا ونافعا، وجعل كل جوهر محتملاً للألم واللذة، لم يحتمل أن يجعلهم كذلك إلا لعواقب، يُحذرهم بها ويُرغبهم فيها من الوعيد بالشدائد والوعد بالملاذ، وبذلك تتم الرغبة والرهبة. والله الموفق. وبعد، فإذ خلق الخلق، وجعل البعض منافع ببعض، وإن لم يكن له في ذلك نفع لغناه، وكذلك المضار، فمثله يأمر وينهى بمنافع بعض ببعض واتقاء المضار؛ مع ما يأمر بما ينفعهم، كما خلقهم وجعل لهم ذلك، وينهى عما يضرهم. والله الموفق.
And after, when He made creation two divisions: harmful and beneficial, and He made every substance susceptible to pain and pleasure, it is not plausible that He made them thus except for consequences, by which He warns them and encourages them through threat of hardships and promise of pleasures, and thereby desire and fear are perfected. And God is the grantor of success. And after, for He created creation and made some benefits by means of others, even though He has no need of that due to His self-sufficiency, and likewise the harms, so the like of Him commands and forbids regarding benefits of some by means of others and avoidance of harms; along with what He commands that benefits them, just as He created them and made that for them, and forbids what harms them. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage presents a rational argument for divine commands and prohibitions based on the nature of creation. Maturidi states that God created things as either harmful or beneficial, and every substance is susceptible to pain and pleasure. It is inconceivable that He would do this without purpose—namely, to provide consequences (threats and promises) that complete fear and desire in humans. Then Maturidi argues that since God created some beings as benefits for others, even though He is independent of any need, He therefore commands and forbids in relation to those benefits and harms. His commands enjoin what benefits and prohibit what harms, consistent with His creation of those things. The argument is teleological: the structure of creation implies a lawgiver who guides creatures toward their welfare.

Key Terms
ضار ونافع
رغبة ورهبة
أمر ونهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's commands and prohibitions are for His own benefit or need.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that God is independent (غني) and has no need; therefore His commands are solely for the benefit of the creatures.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does God command and forbid?
A) Because He needs obedience
B) Because creation is divided into benefit and harm, and humans need guidance to avoid harm and gain benefit
C) Because angels requested it
D) Because it is a test without purpose

Key Takeaway
God's commands and prohibitions are not arbitrary but rooted in the rational structure of creation as beneficial and harmful.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself reluctant to obey a divine command, do you pause to consider the underlying benefit God intends for you? How might this lesson reshape your attitude towards divine obligations?
?
This passage argues that God's commands are not for His own need but for your well-being. In what area of your life are you acting as if God's rules are burdensome restrictions rather than merciful guidance? What step will you take to change that perception today?
?
If you truly believed that every prohibition is to protect you from harm, what would you do differently in your daily choices? Commit to one specific change based on this understanding.

Wisdom Requires Knowledge of the Benefactor
Maturidi argues that wisdom entails command/prohibition and knowledge of the Benefactor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that wisdom itself demands that we recognize God's blessings and thereby know Him.

Core Idea
Reason demands that blessings be recognized; ignoring the Giver is injustice.

Maturidi makes a striking claim: wisdom itself requires commands and prohibitions. In this passage, he explains why. He argues that God's abundant, undeserved blessings make it irrational not to know the Benefactor. Let's see his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let Maturidi's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how he structures his argument with 'because' and 'therefore'.
2
Identify the key premise
Maturidi points out that God's blessings came without any prior merit from humans. Find this premise in the text and note why it matters.
This premise is crucial: it shows blessings are pure favor, not repayment.
3
Understand the rational conclusion
He concludes that it is rationally impermissible to give such blessings to someone who does not recognize them. Why? Because it would be squandering and injustice to the favors.
Think about how this conclusion leads to the necessity of knowing the Benefactor.
4
See the link to trial and incentive
Maturidi connects knowledge of the Benefactor to the necessity of trial (mihna) and attaches promise and threat to perfect desire and fear.
This shows that knowledge is not just intellectual but has practical consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن في الحكمة الأمر والنهي؛ لأن الله خلق البشر في أحسن تقويم، وسخر لهم جميع ما على وجه الأرض وبركاتها وبركات السماء، من غير أن سبق منهم ما خرج [به] ذلك مخرج المكافأة أو مخرج حق قضاه. فلا يجوز في العقل إسداء مثل هذه النعم إلى من لا يعرفها، لما فيه تضييع وظلم النعم، فلزمهم به معرفة المنعم ليعلموا من يستحق المحبة ويستوجب الشكر، وفي ذلك لزوم المحنة؛ ووَصل بذلك الوعد والوعيد لتتم الرغبة والرهبة. وبالله التوفيق.
And also, wisdom indeed requires command and prohibition; because God created human beings in the best stature, and subjected to them all that is on the face of the earth and its blessings and the blessings of the sky, without there having preceded from them anything that would make that [come] out as a compensation or as a due He decreed. So it is not rationally permissible to bestow such favors upon one who does not know them, because that would entail squandering and injustice to favors. Therefore, knowledge of the Benefactor became incumbent upon them, that they may know who deserves love and who deserves gratitude. And in that is the necessity of trial; and He attached to that promise and threat to perfect desire and fear. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage presents a wisdom-based argument for commands and prohibitions. Maturidi states that wisdom (al-hikma) itself entails command and prohibition. The reason is that God created humans in the best form and subjected all blessings of earth and sky to them, without any prior act from them that would make these blessings a reward or a due. Therefore, it is rationally impermissible (la yajuzu fi al-'aql) to bestow such blessings on someone who does not recognize them, as that would be squandering and injustice. Thus, humans must know the Benefactor (al-mun'im) to know who deserves love and gratitude. This knowledge leads to the necessity of a trial (al-mihna), and God joined promises and threats to perfect desire (raghba) and fear (rahba). The argument emphasizes that rational gratitude requires recognizing the giver, which is itself a kind of command.

Key Terms
حكمة
أمر ونهي
معرفة المنعم
محنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think blessings are automatically recognized, and there is no need to actively know the Benefactor.
Correction: Maturidi argues that ignoring the Benefactor is rational injustice; reason demands that we recognize the Giver to avoid squandering the blessings.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say it is rationally impermissible to give blessings to someone who does not know the Giver?
A) Because God is unjust
B) Because that would be squandering and injustice to the blessings
C) Because humans are naturally ungrateful
D) Because blessings are always a reward for prior action

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that rational reflection on God's blessings makes knowing Him not just recommended but rationally necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you enjoy a blessing—health, wealth, or a beautiful sunset—do you immediately turn to the Giver in gratitude, or do you often stop at the gift? What would change in your day if you paused to recognize the Benefactor before every blessing?
?
The passage says it is rational injustice to receive blessings without knowing the Giver. Look at the last 24 hours: where did you treat God's favors as if they were simply 'there' without acknowledging Him? What specific step can you take today to begin repaying that injustice?
?
If wisdom itself demands command and prohibition, then your very intellect holds you accountable to seek God and obey Him. Are you living as if your intellect is satisfied with ignoring the Commander? What would it cost you to submit to that demand starting now?

The First Rational Argument for Prophethood: The Vastness of Blessings
Maturidi's first rational proof for prophethood from gratitude

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the rational obligation of gratitude and the incomprehensible magnitude of blessings to prove the need for a divine informer.

Core Idea
The intellect cannot fully grasp how to thank for all blessings; a messenger is necessary.

Maturidi begins by affirming that reason itself recognizes the goodness of thanking the Benefactor and the evil of ingratitude. He then points out that every blessing we perceive through our senses is beyond our complete knowledge. From this, he draws his first argument: since the degrees of gratitude due to benefactors and the magnitudes of blessings are known only to the Creator, the intellect requires someone to inform us of the true source of those blessings.

Guided Path
1
Read the rational axiom
Read the first sentence. Notice that Maturidi takes as a starting point that reason knows the goodness of thanking the Benefactor and the ugliness of denying Him. This is a foundational moral axiom.
He does not prove it here; it is taken as self-evident.
2
Observe the vastness of blessings
The second sentence notes that every sense we have is itself a blessing from God, and that we cannot fully grasp all the blessings. This sets up the limitation of human knowledge.
3
Understand the first statement
The first of two statements argues that the differences in how benefactors deserve thanks and the varying magnitudes of blessings are beyond human knowledge. Only the Originator knows them fully.
4
Draw the conclusion
Therefore, the intellect concludes that it cannot perfect its thanks on its own. It necessitates an informer (someone who tells us about the true Benefactor). That informer is the prophet.
This is the core of the first argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم مما يلزم القول بالرسالة بضرورة العقل هو أنه قد ثبت حسن معرفة المنعم والشكر له في العقل، وقبح الجحود له والكفران بنعمته. ثم ما من شيء تقع عليه حاسة من حواسه إلا والله عليه في سلامة حاسته وما أدرك نعما يعجز عن الإحاطة بها. ثم بعد هذا له عبارتان؛ إحداهما تفاوت استحقاق المنعمين الشكر، وتفاضل أقدار النعم مما لا يبلغ علم أحد نهايتها إلا علم من أنشأها. فعلى هذا لا يبلغ عقل بما به تمام شكرها إلا هو، فيلزم العقل من يخبر عمن منه تلك النعم. والأخرى أن تلك النعم إذ هي تفرقت على الحواس وأصابت كل جارحة منها، فلزم استعمال كل جارحة في شكر ما الله عليها من النعم. مع ما إذا أردت أن تعرف قدرها اعتبر بالمبتلى بالآفة بها؛ لعله يخف عليه بذل الدنيا. ثم كان كل ما بكل جارحة تؤدى من الشكر لا يعرف بالعقل، فيلزم القول بمخبر يخبر عن الله.
Then among what necessitates the assertion of prophethood by rational necessity is that it has been established in reason the goodness of knowing the Benefactor and thanking Him, and the ugliness of denying Him and being ungrateful for His blessing. Then there is no thing that a sense from his senses falls upon except that God is [bestowing] upon him the soundness of his sense and what he perceives of blessings that he is incapable of encompassing. Then after this, there are two statements for him: one of them is the variation in the deservingness of benefactors of thanks, and the differentiation of the magnitudes of blessings, such that no one's knowledge reaches their limit except the knowledge of the One who originated them. So upon this, the intellect does not attain by itself the complete thanks for them except Him, so the intellect necessitates someone who informs about whom those blessings come from. And the other is that those blessings, since they are distributed over the senses and affect each limb from them, it becomes necessary to use each limb in thanking God for the blessings upon it. Along with that, if you want to know their value, consider the one afflicted with a defect in it; perhaps it becomes easy for him to give up the world. Then everything that each limb performs of thanks is not known by the intellect, so the assertion of an informer who informs about God is necessitated.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof for the necessity of prophethood based on the obligation of gratitude. Maturidi first states two rational axioms: it is good to know and thank the Benefactor, and it is evil to deny Him and be ungrateful. He then notes that every sense and every blessing is beyond human comprehension. From this he draws two points (عبارتان). First, the degrees of gratitude due to benefactors and the magnitudes of blessings are beyond human knowledge except for the Creator; thus reason demands someone to inform us of the source of those blessings. Second, blessings affect each limb, so each limb must be used in gratitude, but the specific way to thank with each limb is not known by reason alone; therefore a messenger (مخبر) from God is necessary.

Key Terms
معرفة المنعم
الشكر
الجحود
الكفران
من يخبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that human intellect alone can determine exactly how to thank God for every blessing.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the magnitude and diversity of blessings are beyond our knowledge, so we need divine guidance to know the proper thanks.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's first argument, why does the intellect necessitate a divine informer?
A) Because humans are naturally ungrateful
B) Because the full thanks for blessings cannot be attained by intellect alone, as the true extent and degrees of gratitude are known only to God
C) Because God commands it in scripture
D) Because prophets are needed to govern society

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's first argument shows that the intellect itself recognizes its inability to fully thank God, thereby demanding a prophet to teach us proper gratitude.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you wake up each morning and open your eyes, do you truly acknowledge that even the ability to see is a blessing you are incapable of thanking for fully? In what specific way has this lesson changed how you will begin your day tomorrow?
?
Think of a blessing you often take for granted, such as your health or your family. How can you use the teaching of this passage to offer a more complete gratitude, beyond just saying 'Alhamdulillah'? What specific action will you commit to today to act on that gratitude?
?
This argument shows that the intellect itself demands a prophet. If you truly accept this, how should it affect your relationship with the guidance brought by the Prophet ﷺ? Is there one area of your life where you have been relying on your own reason rather than prophetic guidance? What step will you take to align with that guidance this week?

The Second Rational Argument: Gratitude of Each Limb Requires Divine Informer
Maturidi's second rational proof for prophethood from gratitude

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's second argument that the proper thanks for blessings distributed over each limb is not known by reason alone, necessitating a messenger from God.

Core Idea
Each limb has a specific way to thank God, known only through revelation.

Maturidi now presents the second of his two statements. He observes that blessings are distributed over our senses and limbs, each requiring a specific form of gratitude. However, the intellect cannot determine the proper thanks for each limb. To illustrate the value of these blessings, he invites us to consider how much a person would sacrifice to regain a lost faculty. Thus, again, reason points to the need for a messenger from God to instruct us.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
Blessings are not only general but affect each sense and limb. Each limb has been given a specific blessing, and so each must be used in thanks.
2
Appreciate the value of blessings
Maturidi gives a thought experiment: consider someone afflicted with a defect in a limb; they would be willing to give up the world to be cured. This shows how invaluable these blessings are.
3
Recognize the limitation of reason
Despite the value, the specific acts of thanks for each limb are not known by reason alone. The intellect cannot figure out the correct gratitude for eyesight, hearing, etc.
4
Conclude the need for an informer
Therefore, reason necessitates an informer (a prophet) who tells us how to properly thank God for each blessing.
This completes the second argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم مما يلزم القول بالرسالة بضرورة العقل هو أنه قد ثبت حسن معرفة المنعم والشكر له في العقل، وقبح الجحود له والكفران بنعمته. ثم ما من شيء تقع عليه حاسة من حواسه إلا والله عليه في سلامة حاسته وما أدرك نعما يعجز عن الإحاطة بها. ثم بعد هذا له عبارتان؛ إحداهما تفاوت استحقاق المنعمين الشكر، وتفاضل أقدار النعم مما لا يبلغ علم أحد نهايتها إلا علم من أنشأها. فعلى هذا لا يبلغ عقل بما به تمام شكرها إلا هو، فيلزم العقل من يخبر عمن منه تلك النعم. والأخرى أن تلك النعم إذ هي تفرقت على الحواس وأصابت كل جارحة منها، فلزم استعمال كل جارحة في شكر ما الله عليها من النعم. مع ما إذا أردت أن تعرف قدرها اعتبر بالمبتلى بالآفة بها؛ لعله يخف عليه بذل الدنيا. ثم كان كل ما بكل جارحة تؤدى من الشكر لا يعرف بالعقل، فيلزم القول بمخبر يخبر عن الله.
Then among what necessitates the assertion of prophethood by rational necessity is that it has been established in reason the goodness of knowing the Benefactor and thanking Him, and the ugliness of denying Him and being ungrateful for His blessing. Then there is no thing that a sense from his senses falls upon except that God is [bestowing] upon him the soundness of his sense and what he perceives of blessings that he is incapable of encompassing. Then after this, there are two statements for him: one of them is the variation in the deservingness of benefactors of thanks, and the differentiation of the magnitudes of blessings, such that no one's knowledge reaches their limit except the knowledge of the One who originated them. So upon this, the intellect does not attain by itself the complete thanks for them except Him, so the intellect necessitates someone who informs about whom those blessings come from. Along with that, if you want to know their value, consider the one afflicted with a defect in it; perhaps it becomes easy for him to give up the world. Then everything that each limb performs of thanks is not known by the intellect, so the assertion of an informer who informs about God is necessitated.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof for the necessity of prophethood based on the obligation of gratitude. Maturidi first states two rational axioms: it is good to know and thank the Benefactor, and it is evil to deny Him and be ungrateful. He then notes that every sense and every blessing is beyond human comprehension. From this he draws two points (عبارتان). First, the degrees of gratitude due to benefactors and the magnitudes of blessings are beyond human knowledge except for the Creator; thus reason demands someone to inform us of the source of those blessings. Second, blessings affect each limb, so each limb must be used in gratitude, but the specific way to thank with each limb is not known by reason alone; therefore a messenger (مخبر) from God is necessary.

Key Terms
الحواس
الجوارح
الشكر
المبتلى
مخبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that human reason can figure out the correct acts of worship to thank God for each blessing.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the specifics are not known by intellect; we need revelation.

Checkpoint
What example does Maturidi give to show the value of blessings?
A) A rich man who donates wealth
B) A person afflicted with a defect in a limb who would give up the world to be cured
C) A traveler who loses his way
D) A scholar who writes a book

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's second argument shows that each limb's gratitude is divinely prescribed, so a messenger is necessary to teach us.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider one limb or sense you use daily without gratitude. If you lost it, you would give the world to regain it. How are you thanking Allah for it in a specific way that only revelation teaches? Is your gratitude merely verbal, or does it involve using that limb in acts of worship as prescribed? What will you change today to ensure your gratitude is as Allah instructed?
?
The argument shows that gratitude for each blessing requires divine guidance. In your daily worship—prayer, charity, fasting—are you following the prophetic method exactly, or have you let your own assumptions and culture shape it? How would the first generation of Muslims view your practice of gratitude? Commit to learning one specific prophetic practice for a limb you have neglected.
?
Intellect alone cannot determine the proper gratitude for each limb. Yet, many Muslims rely on their own reasoning for worship. Examine your life: where has your own 'I think this is good enough' replaced the prophetic teaching? What is one act of worship you will research to ensure it aligns with the sunnah, and when will you start?

The Proof from Differential Knowledge for Messengers
Maturidi's proof from differential knowledge for the necessity of prophets

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's concise proof that the observable variation in knowledge among creatures opens the door for affirming that Allah possesses unique knowledge of what benefits His servants, which He conveys through messengers.

Core Idea
If creatures differ in knowledge, Allah may have exclusive knowledge of human benefit, revealed through prophets.

In this brief passage, Imam Maturidi presents a powerful yet simple proof for the necessity of messengers. He starts with an everyday observation—that some people know things others do not—and uses it to argue that it is not unreasonable to believe that Allah possesses knowledge that is exclusively His, particularly knowledge of what is truly good for humanity. Since this knowledge is not accessible through ordinary means, messengers become the necessary channel for its delivery. Let's unpack this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic text and its literal translation. Pay attention to the logical connectors like 'فلا ندفع' (we do not deny) which signal the argument's flow.
The passage is short but dense; read it twice.
2
Identify the premise and analogy
Notice that the argument begins with an observable fact: 'يكون عند واحد من ذلك ما ليس عند غيره' (There may be with one of that what is not with another). This is the premise about creatures. Then Maturidi applies the same logic to the Creator: if it's true among creatures, it's even more fitting for Allah.
This is an argument from fittingness (qiyas al-awlawiyya).
3
Reflect on the conclusion and its implication
The conclusion is that Allah may have exclusive knowledge of what benefits His servants, and this knowledge is conveyed through messengers. Ask yourself: why does this prove the necessity of messengers? Because without them, that knowledge would remain inaccessible.
The phrase 'والله المعين' reminds us that success comes from Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكون عند واحد من ذلك ما ليس عند غيره. وإذا ثبت ذلك فلا ندفع أن يكون عند الله ما به صلاح عباده مما ليس عند خلقه، فيوصله إليهم برسله. والله المعين.
There may be with one of that what is not with another. And when that is established, we do not deny that Allah may have what is for the good of His servants which is not with His creation, so He conveys it to them through His messengers. And Allah is the Helper.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage presents a succinct proof by analogy. The author begins by acknowledging that among created beings, some possess knowledge or qualities that others do not. This is an observable fact. He then argues that if this is true among creatures, it is even more fitting to affirm that Allah possesses knowledge that is exclusively His—specifically, knowledge of what is truly beneficial for His servants. Since this knowledge is not naturally accessible to humans, the only way for them to receive it is through messengers whom Allah sends. The proof is based on the principle of fittingness: what is possible among creation is even more possible for the Creator. The lesson ends with 'And Allah is the Helper,' acknowledging divine assistance.

Key Terms
رسل
صلاح عباده

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this proof implies that Allah is limited like creatures, having only some knowledge and not all.
Correction: The argument does not limit Allah; it only uses the observable difference among creatures to show that it is not impossible for Allah to have exclusive knowledge. Allah's knowledge is perfect and all-encompassing; this specific knowledge is simply not accessible to humans except through revelation.

Checkpoint

A) All creatures share the same knowledge.
B) Some creatures have knowledge that others do not.
C) Allah's knowledge is identical to human knowledge.
D) Messengers are needed because humans cannot think.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's proof uses the observable diversity of knowledge among creatures to argue that it is reasonable to believe Allah has exclusive knowledge of human benefit, which He communicates through prophets.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The lesson ends with 'والله المعين' – Allah is the Helper. Have you ever paused to ask Allah to help you understand and accept His guidance, or do you rely on your own intellect? Will you commit to making that prayer part of your study of every proof of faith?

Preservation of Shari'a and Finality of Prophethood
Maturidi explains divine wisdom behind preservation of Shari'a and finality of prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's explanation of why the Shari'a of Prophet Muhammad is preserved and why no further prophet comes after him.

Core Idea
God sealed prophethood with Muhammad and preserved His Book to protect the Shari'a.

Maturidi addresses a key question: why does Islamic law remain unchanged while previous laws were altered? He explains that God's wisdom included finalizing prophethood with Muhammad and providing a preserved scripture, ensuring the Shari'a remains intact until the end of time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Notice the flow from the problem of alteration to the divine response.
The passage uses a cause-and-effect structure.
2
Identify the two divine actions
Maturidi states two key actions: (1) God seals prophethood with Muhammad, and (2) God favors the community with a preserved book. Identify how these relate to the preservation of Shari'a.
3
Reflect on the community's role
Note that God made the Muslim community such that it cannot bear great changes. Think about why this is significant for the preservation of the law.
This contrasts with earlier communities that could undergo alteration.
4
Connect to the broader purpose
Consider how the preserved book serves as a reference to know alteration and change, ensuring the law remains until the end of the world.
The Quran is the preserved book referred to here.
5
Summarize the theological point
Summarize in your own words: finality of prophethood and preservation of scripture together guarantee the integrity of Islamic law.
This is a key doctrine in Sunni Islam.

Source Text

Afterward, when alteration of the law reaches such an extent that it almost obliterates its trace and its news becomes obliterated, God favors with His grace by sending one who revives that and manifests what the messengers were upon with signs that overwhelm intellects, so that through them they may know alteration and change, and upon that propagation. Then from God's decree is to seal prophethood with Muhammad, peace be upon him, and not to send to his community after him a messenger.
