Commentary
This passage explains the divine wisdom behind the preservation of the Islamic Shari'a and the finality of prophethood. Maturidi begins by stating that when alteration of the law reaches a point where its traces are nearly erased, God sends a messenger to revive it with overwhelming signs. Then he notes that God decreed to seal prophethood with Muhammad, meaning no messenger will come after him. Therefore, God made the Muslim community such that it cannot sustain major alterations, and He preserved a book by which change can be known, ensuring the law remains until the end of the world. The passage concludes with a prayer for success.

God's Self-Sufficiency and Creation Ex Nihilo
Maturidi argues from God's self-sufficiency to creation ex nihilo and limits of human wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses God's self-sufficiency (ghina) and power (qudra) to argue for creation ex nihilo (ibda') and the limits of human wisdom.

Core Idea
God's self-sufficiency necessarily entails the ability to create from nothing.

Maturidi invites us to consider the difference between human need and God's absolute independence. Humans require movement and rest to achieve goals and avoid exhaustion. But God, being self-sufficient and powerful, cannot be subject to such limitations. This contrast leads to a profound conclusion: God's power is so complete that He can create from nothing, and His wisdom is beyond full human comprehension.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read the English translation. Try to grasp the overall argument flow before diving into details.
Notice the contrast between human and divine conditions.
2
Contrast human and divine attributes
Identify how Maturidi describes human limitations (need for movement, rest, etc.) and then states that God's ghina (self-sufficiency) and qudra (power) negate these needs.
This contrast is the foundation of the argument.
3
Understand the invalidity of describing God as unable to create ex nihilo
Maturidi argues that if God's power, dominion, and knowledge are established, then describing Him as unable to create from nothing is invalid because that would imply need and weakness.
Inability is a sign of need, not power.
4
Grasp the rational necessity and limitation of human wisdom
Maturidi asserts that reason requires affirming the possibility of creation from nothing and that God's actions are wise, even if human intellect cannot fully comprehend that wisdom.
Human wisdom has limits, but divine wisdom is absolute.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that God has absolute authority over creation, and every owner of dominion may act within his dominion as he wills.
This affirms God's sovereignty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الراحة والسلوة ، والآخر إلى ما يبلغه الهمة والرغبة، إذ لا سبيل له إلى مقصوده إلا بالتحرك والزوال، ولا إلى دفع الإعياء والتعب إلا بالقرار والسكون فأما الله سبحانه إذ ثبت غناه وقدرته بطل أن يعتريه حاجة أو يعتريه همة. وعلى ذلك لما ثبتت قدرته وسلطانه وعلمه بطل وصفه بأن لا يقدر على فعل شيء ابتداء لا عن شيء، إذ ذلك علم الحاجة وآية الضعف. وحاجة جميع ما يُحس ويبلغه علم البشر هي الدلالة على [عدم] تقدير العالم، و[الله] عالم به قدير غني، لم يجز إضافة ذلك إلى الذي عرف غناه وقدرته وحكمته وعلمه. ولا قوة إلا بالله . فلذلك لزم القول بضرورة العقل لجواز كون العالم لا عن شيء وخروج فعله على الحكمة، وإن عجزت عقول حكماء العالم عن إدراكها لخروج وجه الحكمة عن نهاية قوة عقولهم، على ما بيّنا من كون شيء لا عن شيء، ومن جواز فعل ممن لا ينتفع به، وبذلك تظهر حقيقة الأمر له ، و أن له الخلق والأمر. ولكل ذي ملك أن يفعل في ملكه على قدر ما ملك منه ما شاء. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Rest and solace, and the other to what aspiration and desire reach, for he has no way to his goal except by movement and removal, and no way to repel fatigue and exhaustion except by rest and stillness. As for Allah, the Glorified, when His self-sufficiency and power are established, it becomes invalid that need or aspiration should affect Him. And accordingly, when His power, dominion, and knowledge are established, describing Him as unable to do anything initially from nothing becomes invalid, because that is a sign of need and a mark of weakness. And the need of all that is perceived and reached by human knowledge is the indication of the [lack] of estimation of the world, and [Allah] is knowing of it, powerful, self-sufficient. It is not permissible to attribute that to the One whose self-sufficiency, power, wisdom, and knowledge are known. There is no power except with Allah. Therefore, it is necessary to assert by rational necessity the possibility of the world coming from nothing and His action proceeding with wisdom, even if the intellects of the wise of the world are incapable of comprehending it because the aspect of wisdom is beyond the limit of the strength of their intellects, as we have explained regarding something coming from nothing, and regarding the permissibility of an action from one who does not benefit from it. And by that, the reality of the matter becomes clear to him, and that to Him belongs creation and command. And every owner of dominion may act in his dominion according to what he possesses of it as he wills. There is no power except with Allah.

Commentary
This passage is an argument establishing God's transcendence above need and limitation. Maturidi begins by contrasting human beings, who require movement to achieve goals and rest to relieve fatigue, with God, who is absolutely self-sufficient and powerful. Since God's self-sufficiency (ghina) and power (qudra) are established, He cannot be subject to need or aspiration. Then he argues that if God's power, dominion, and knowledge are established, it is invalid to describe Him as unable to create something from nothing (ibda'), because such inability would indicate need and weakness. Human knowledge perceives objects that are contingent and subject to change, but it cannot fully grasp the wisdom behind God's creation. Therefore, reason necessitates affirming that the world can come from nothing and that God's actions are all wise, even if human intellects cannot apprehend that wisdom. The proof is based on the premise that God is self-sufficient and powerful, and that creating from nothing is a sign of power, not need. Maturidi concludes that every owner of a dominion has the right to act freely within it, which applies supremely to God.

Correction: Maturidi argues that inability is a sign of need and weakness, not power. God's power is absolute, so He can do all things, including creating from nothing.

Moral Qualities Need a Divine Source
Maturidi argues that good and evil cannot be source-less, or else everything would be, negating Islam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that moral categories like good and evil cannot exist independently but require a divine source, and that denying this leads to exiting Islam.

Core Idea
Moral values need a source; without it, everything becomes source-less, contradicting Islam.

This lesson presents a concise argument from Imam Maturidi on the necessity of a source for moral qualities. He uses a conditional to show that if good and evil could exist without a source, then everything could be without a source—a conclusion that undermines the very foundation of Islamic belief. The passage is a powerful demonstration of the rational necessity of a Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the overall flow of the argument.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'.
2
Understand the conditional
Identify the 'if-then' structure. Maturidi moves from the specific (good/evil without source) to the universal (everything without source).
3
Reflect on the consequence
Why does the conclusion entail exiting Islam? Consider the link between source and creation. Denying a source for everything negates the Creator.
The phrase 'لا قوة إلا بالله' affirms that all power and source is from God.

Source Text

In a state where he himself is ignorant of what he is upon. Additionally, if it were permissible that good and evil exist without a source for them, then it would be permissible that everything exist without a source, and in that is exiting Islam. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage presents a brief but powerful argument against the idea that moral qualities (good and evil) can exist without a creator (منشئ). It begins by noting a hypothetical situation where a person is ignorant of his own state. Then it states the conditional: if it were possible for goodness (حسن) and badness (قبح) to exist without a source, then it would follow that everything could exist without a source, which leads to the abandonment of Islam. The argument is that moral categories like 'good' and 'evil' are not self-subsistent; they require a source that determines them. Denying that source leads to a universal denial of causation, which is incompatible with Islamic belief in a Creator. The lesson concludes with the phrase 'لا قوة إلا بالله', affirming reliance on God's power.

Refutation of Attributing Creation to Agents
Maturidi argues against attributing creation to agents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that attributing the creation of actions to human agents contradicts the universal creed that only God creates.

Core Idea
Creation belongs exclusively to God; attributing it to creatures contradicts the universal creed.

This passage presents a key argument from Imam Maturidi against those who claim that human agents create their own actions. Maturidi begins by citing the universal consensus that there is no creator but God, then shows the implications of attributing creation to others. Let's examine his logical refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the starting point: the common saying that there is no creator but God.
This is the foundation of the argument.
2
Identify the hypothetical scenario
Maturidi considers what would happen if we attribute the coming-to-be, annihilation, and determination of actions to agents. Identify this conditional statement.
3
Understand the implication
He argues that doing so would ascribe the attribute of creation (al-khalq) to those agents, which would necessitate another creator besides God.
This is the core of the reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that such a view is contradictory to the common saying and is repudiated. Note how he excludes attributing lordship as well.
The final phrase 'that is repudiated' is emphatic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً القول المتعارف في الخلق أن لا خالق غير الله ولا رب سواه، ولو جعلنا حدث الأفعال وخروجها من العدم إلى الوجود، ثم فناءها بعد الوجود، ثم خروجها على تقدير من اربابها لجعلنا لها وصف الخلق الذي به صار الخلق خلقا، وفي ذلك لزوم القول بخالق سواه، وفي جوازه مناقضة قول من ذكرتُ مع ما لو جاز ذلك لجاز القول برب فعله، وذلك مدفوع. وبالله التوفيق.
And also the common saying among creation is that there is no creator other than God and no Lord besides Him. If we were to make the coming-to-be of actions and their emergence from non-existence to existence, then their annihilation after existence, then their emergence according to the determination of their agents, we would have attributed to them the description of creation (al-khalq) by which creation becomes creation. In that is the necessity of saying that there is a creator other than Him, and in its permissibility is a contradiction of the statement of those I mentioned, along with the fact that if that were permissible, it would be permissible to say that there is a lord for one's action. And that is repudiated. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This argument begins by stating the universally acknowledged creed: there is no creator but God and no Lord besides Him. Maturidi then shows what follows if one attributes the creation of actions to human agents. If we say that the actions' coming into existence, their passing away, and their specific form are determined by the agents, then we have ascribed to them the attribute of 'creation' (الخلق) which is what makes something a creation. That would necessitate affirming another creator besides God. Moreover, it would contradict the common saying that there is no creator but God. If it were acceptable for an agent to be the creator of his own action, then it would be acceptable to say that the agent is the 'lord' of that action, which is blasphemy. Thus, Maturidi rejects this view and reaffirms that only God is the creator of actions. The phrase 'ذلك مدفوع' means 'that is repudiated'.

Key Terms
خالق
رب
خلق
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'creation' can be shared between God and humans; e.g., humans 'create' their own actions.
Correction: Maturidi shows that attributing creation to humans would necessitate a second creator and contradict the universal confession of God's exclusive creatorship.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say would be the consequence if we attribute the creation of actions to human agents?
A) It would imply there is another creator besides God.
B) It would prove human free will.
C) It would be consistent with the common saying.
D) It would show that God is not all-powerful.

Key Takeaway
Attributing creation to any being other than God leads to affirming multiple creators, which is impossible and contradicts the universal creed of tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something through your own effort, do you ever feel a sense of being the 'creator' of that success? How can you remind yourself that the coming-to-be of your action from non-existence is solely from God?
?
In your daily planning, do you truly believe that no action occurs except by God's determination? What would change in your reliance on Him if you lived with this conviction?
?
Reflect on a moment you felt pride in an accomplishment. Did you consciously attribute the very act of coming into being to God? How can you train yourself to see His creative power in every movement you make?

The Proof of the World's Createdness Requires Divine Creation of Accidents
Maturidi argues that the proof of the world's temporality depends on accidents being divinely created.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the standard proof for the world's createdness depends on accidents being created by God, and why denying that undermines tawhid.

Core Idea
The proof of creation is built on accidents; if humans cause accidents, the proof fails.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents a critical argument that links the classic proof for the world's createdness to the divine origin of accidental states. He warns that if humans could produce these states, the very basis for believing in God's unity and lordship would collapse. Let's trace this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse. Then read the literal translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
Notice the types of accidents listed: separation, conjunction, movement, stillness.
2
Identify the types of accidents
The passage lists four accidental states: التفرق (separation), الاجتماع (conjunction), التحرك (movement), السكون (stillness). These are the indicators of temporality.
3
Follow the conditional chain
Maturidi sets up a conditional: if these accidental states are not from God but from humans, then the proof of the world's createdness would be established by other than God. Trace this logic carefully.
Consider what happens if a human can produce movement or stillness in a body.
4
Grasp the dire conclusion
Since bodies are perceived only through these accidents, if accidents can come from other than God, the proof that God is the sole creator and lord is invalidated. That is the consequence.
Maturidi ends with a plea for divine protection from this erroneous view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن الذي به عرف أهل التوحيد حدث الأعيان امتناعها عن الخروج من التفرق والاجتماع والتحرك والسكون. فإذا لم يكن هذه الأحوال في الحقيقة خلقاً من الله على زي من جرت على يديه لم نقدر أن نثبت جسما وعينا يدرك على ما هو عليه بفعل الله؛ إذ الأفعال التي ذكرنا من الأسماء يجوز تحققها لا بالله، وإن كنا نبصر من به ذلك، فيصیر دلیل حدث العالم يقيمه غير الله؛ إذ لا سبيل له إلى إظهار الذي منه من الأحوال التي ذكرنا مما ليست منه، ولولا تلك الأحوال لم يعرف حدث العالم، فيبطل طريق العلم به بدليل أقامه هو. ثم لما احتمل جميع الأحوال بغيره لم يثبت بها أنه صانع تلك، والأجسام لا تعاين إلا بها؛ فيبطل أن يكون الله تعالى جعل لوحدانيته دليلاً به يُعرف ولربوبيته شاهدا يشهد، على هذا القول وبالله العصمة والنجاة.
And also, that by which the people of tawhid know the createdness of bodies is their inability to exit from separation and conjunction, movement and stillness. If these states (of the bodies) are not in reality a creation from God in the manner of one on whose hands they occur, we would not be able to establish a body and an eye that perceives what it is by the act of God. For the actions that we mentioned among the names may be actualized without God, even if we see the one by whom that occurs. Then the proof of the createdness of the world would be established by other than God, since he (the agent) has no way to manifest that from which [the attributes] come, from the states we mentioned that are not from him. Without those states, the createdness of the world would not be known, so the path to knowledge of it would be invalidated by a proof that he (the agent) himself establishes. Then, since (the body) admits all states from other than God, it is not established by those states that He is the maker of those bodies, and bodies are not perceived except through them; thus it would be invalidated that God Almighty made for His oneness a proof by which He is known and for His lordship a witness that bears witness. According to this opinion, and with God is protection and salvation.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This argument is a chain of reasoning about the standard proof for the createdness of the world (حدث العالم). The people of tawhid know that bodies are temporal because they cannot escape accidental states like separation (التفرق), conjunction (الاجتماع), movement (التحرك), and stillness (السكون). These states are the indicators. Maturidi argues that if these states were not created by God but rather occurred at the hands of humans (أي على زي من جرت على يديه), then we could not use them as evidence to prove that a body is created by God. The human actor could produce effects (like making a body move or separate) without God, and we would see that. Then the proof for the world's being temporal would be based on the actions of other than God. But the human agent cannot produce the full range of states from which the createdness is deduced; he is limited. If he could, then the proof would be invalid. Moreover, if bodies admit states from other than God, then those states do not prove that God is their maker. Since bodies are only perceived through these states, the proof for God's unity and lordship would be invalidated. Thus, the view that humans produce their own actions undermines the foundational proof of tawhid. Maturidi warns that this leads to ruin, and he seeks God's protection.

Key Terms
حدث العالم
أعيان
تفرق
اجتماع
تحرك
سكون
أعراض
دليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof of the world's createdness is independent of the origin of accidental states.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the proof relies on accidents being created by God; if humans could cause them, the proof fails.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say would happen if the accidental states of bodies could be produced by humans?
A) The proof of the world's createdness would be invalidated.
B) Humans would become creators.
C) It would confirm God's unity.
D) It would make actions invisible.

Key Takeaway
The proof for the world's createdness depends on accidents being divine creations; if accidents can come from humans, the proof falls and tawhid is undermined.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every accident in your body—your heartbeat, your breath—is a direct creation of God, why do you often act as if your own will alone controls them? What would change in your daily routine if you truly felt that you are dependent on God for every movement?
?
Maturidi argues that if humans could create accidents, the proof of God's lordship would be invalid. In what areas of your life do you subtly attribute your successes or failures to your own power rather than to God's creation? This week, will you actively remind yourself that even your next thought is an accident created by Him?
?
The proof of tawhid is undermined if accidental states are not from God. How does your belief in God's sole creation of all things affect your response to difficulty and ease? Are you willing to accept that your ability to change your situation is itself a gift, not an independent power?

Why Denying God's Creation of Actions Invalidates Proof of Oneness
Maturidi argues that denying God's creation of actions negates all visible proof of His oneness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that if human actions are not created by God, then the observable world cannot serve as proof of His oneness.

Core Idea
Without God's creation of actions, the world's temporality cannot prove His oneness.

Maturidi here responds to the Mu'tazilite position that human actions are not created by God. He shows that this position destroys the very evidence we use to prove God's oneness. This passage is a critical argument in the debate about divine causality and human agency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponents' view
The passage argues against those who deny that God creates human actions. Read the first part and note the conditional: if actions are not from God, then we cannot prove certain things.
Maturidi is refuting the Mu'tazilite position.
2
Follow the logical chain
Trace the argument: actions not from God → we cannot prove a body and eye by God's act → the temporality of the world would be proven by other than God → the conditions that reveal temporality cannot be shown to come from God → the proof of oneness fails.
3
Recognize the conclusion
The conclusion is that if actions are not from God, then God has no visible proof of His oneness or lordship in the world. This is a reductio ad absurdum.
The author ends with a prayer for protection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من جرت على يديه لم نقدر أن نثبت جسما وعينا يدرك على ما هو عليه بفعل الله؛ إذ الأفعال التي ذكرنا من الأسماء يجوز تحققها لا بالله، وإن كنا [ل] نبصر من به ذلك، فيصیر دلیل حدث العالم يقيمه غير الله ؛ إذ لا سبيل له إلى إظهار الذي منه من الأحوال التي ذكرنا مما ليست منه ، ولولا تلك الأحوال لم يعرف حدث العالم، فيبطل طريق العلم به بدليل أقامه هو . ثم لما احتمل جميع الأحوال بغيره لم يثبت بها أنه صانع تلك، والأجسام لا تعاين إلا بها " ؛ فيبطل أن يكون الله تعالى جعل لوحدانيته دليلاً [به] يُعرف ولربوبيته شاهدا يشهد، على هذا القول وبالله العصمة والنجاة.
From the one on whose hands [actions] occur, we are not able to prove a body and an eye that perceives what it is through the act of God; because the actions we mentioned from the names are possible to occur not by God, even if we do not see the one with that [power], so the proof of the temporality of the world would be established by other than God; for he has no way to manifest from himself the conditions we mentioned which are not from him, and were it not for those conditions, the temporality of the world would not be known, so the path of knowledge of it through a proof he himself established is invalidated. Then since [the object] admits all conditions by other than it, it is not established by them that he is the maker of those [bodies], and bodies are not seen except by them; so it is invalidated that God Most High made for His Oneness a proof by which He is known, and for His Lordship a witness that bears witness, according to this saying. And with God is protection and salvation.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage presents an argument against the Mu'tazilite view that human actions are not created by God. It begins by stating that if actions are not from God, then we cannot infer God's oneness from observing bodies and their accidents, because the actions that indicate temporality could be attributed to someone else. The argument proceeds to show that if all conditions (accidents) can be from another, then the existence of bodies does not prove that God is their sole creator. Consequently, God would have no visible proof of His oneness in the world. The passage concludes by seeking God's protection from such a view.

وحدانية (oneness)

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Why Accidents Must Be God's Acts Alone
Maturidi argues that if accidents can be acts of others, the world is not exclusively God's creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why allowing accidents to be acts of creatures would undermine the oneness of God as the sole Creator.

Core Idea
If creatures create accidents, tawhid is broken.

Imam Maturidi presents a crucial argument: if accidents (such as colors, movements) could be produced by creatures, then the world would have multiple creators. This would directly contradict the Islamic principle of tawhid. In this lesson, we examine his reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the original passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Then read the English translation. Notice the logical flow: premise, premise, conclusion.
The passage is incomplete; it cuts off mid-sentence.
2
Identify the key premises
The argument has two premises: (1) The world contains accidents and bodies. (2) Every kind of accident could in reality be an act of another (i.e., not God).
3
Understand the conclusion
From these premises, Maturidi concludes that the world would belong to both God and creatures in terms of origination, which invalidates the belief in the oneness of the Maker.
Even though the passage is cut off, the core argument is clear.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: Why does this argument refute the Mu'tazilite view that humans create their own actions? How does it uphold tawhid?
Consider how this connects to the broader discussion of divine attributes and human agency.

Source Text

And the people of Islam have not differed in affirming that the Maker...
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage presents a final argument that the world consists of accidents and bodies. If accidents can be acts of something other than God, then the world would be shared between God and His creation in terms of origination. This would invalidate the claim of God's oneness as the sole Creator. The author notes that all Muslims agree that God is the Maker, but the argument is cut off. The implication is that Mu'tazilite views lead to polytheism.

The World's Structure and the Implication of Multiple Creators
Maturidi introduces the world's composition and its implication for tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the world's composition of accidents and bodies logically requires a single creator, and that any potential for multiple creators contradicts the oneness of God.

Core Idea
If accidents could be actions of others, the world would have many makers — but tawhid forbids it.

Maturidi begins by examining the basic constituents of the world: accidents and bodies. He argues that if accidents could be performed by beings other than God, then the world would be jointly created, nullifying the statement that God is the sole maker. This sets up the foundational premise that the world's very structure points to one Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the first part of the original text carefully, noting how Maturidi introduces the composition of the world.
2
Identify the key premise
Notice that Maturidi says the world is not free from accidents and bodies, and that accidents could in reality be the action of another.
3
Understand the consequence
If accidents are actions of others, then the world would be for both God and creation — this would invalidate the oneness of the Maker.
4
Connect to tawhid
The argument shows that any belief in multiple creators contradicts the foundational Islamic belief in the unity of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن العالم لا يخلو من الأعراض والأجسام. وكل أنواع الأعراض أمكن في الحقيقة أن يكون فعلاً لغيره، فيكون العالم لله ولخلقه من طريق الإنشاء والوجود، وفي ذلك بطلان القول بوحدانية صانع العالم. ولم يختلف أهل الإسلام في إطلاق القول بأن صانع العالم واحد. وقول من يُبطل قوله عند التحصيل هذه الجملة التي شارك فيها الجميع مردود بالجملة، على نحو قول الله تعالى: {ليس كمثله شيء} [الشورى، ١١/٤٢]، وقوله: {إله كل شيء}. إن قول الناس الذي في التحصيل يجعل له شبها وعدلاً في العباد منقوض بتلك الجملة، وإن استحال فمثله الأول؛ بل الأول أحق، لأنه طريق العلم بالحرف الثاني؛ وهو أن في تحقيق العالم تحقيق الوحدانية للخالق، وبه يسلم له القول بأن {ليس كمثله شيء} [الشورى، ١١/٤٢]، وأنه الواحد لا شريك له. فإذا ثبت العالم بشركاء له فيه لم يكن هو أحق بأن ليس كمثله شيء من أن يكون كمثله أشياء، أو أن يكون إلها لما أنشأه وأخرجه من العدم إلى الوجود من غيره في ذلك. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And also, indeed the world is not free from accidents and bodies. And every kind of accidents can in reality be an action of another, so the world would be for Allah and for His creation by way of origination and existence, and in that is the invalidation of the statement of the oneness of the Maker of the world. And the people of Islam have not differed in the absolute statement that the Maker of the world is one. And the statement of one who invalidates his statement upon analysis this sentence in which all have participated is refuted by the sentence, in the manner of Allah's saying: {There is nothing like unto Him} [al-Shura 42:11], and His saying: {God of everything}. Indeed the statement of the people which upon analysis makes for Him a likeness and a counterpart among the servants is refuted by that sentence, and if impossibility then its like is the first; rather the first is more deserving, because it is the way of knowledge of the second letter; and that is that in the realization of the world is the realization of the oneness of the Creator, and by it the statement that {There is nothing like unto Him} [al-Shura 42:11] is safe for Him, and that He is the One with no partner. So if the world is established with partners for Him in it, then He is not more deserving that there is nothing like Him than that there be things like Him, or that He be a god for what He originated and brought out from non-existence into existence from other than Him in that. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage presents an argument from the structure of the world. Maturidi states that the world consists of accidents and bodies, and all types of accidents could potentially be the action of another (i.e., the servant). If so, the world would be attributed to both God and creation, which invalidates tawhid. He notes that Muslims agree the Maker is one, so any statement that implies otherwise is refuted by the Quranic verse 'There is nothing like unto Him' and 'God of everything'. The opponent's claim that makes God similar to servants is overturned by this universal sentence. The passage emphasizes that realizing the world's composition leads to realizing God's oneness, and that tawhid is established by the very existence of the world.

Key Terms
أعراض
أجسام
وحدانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that human actions are not created by God, implying multiple creators.
Correction: Maturidi argues that if any action (accident) could be from another, then the world would have multiple creators, contradicting tawhid. Therefore, all actions must be from God alone.

Key Takeaway
The composition of the world into accidents and bodies implies that if any accident were created by another, the world would have multiple makers — thus tawhid demands that God alone creates all.

Quranic Proof of God's Oneness from the World's Existence
Maturidi refutes opponents using Quranic verses and establishes tawhid from the world's existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the consensus of Muslims and Quranic verses to assert that the world's existence itself demonstrates God's oneness, and that any claim of partners is refuted.

Core Idea
The very existence of the world proves God's oneness — there is no room for partners.

After establishing the logical consequence of multiple creators, Maturidi turns to the Islamic consensus and the Quran. He argues that the universal statement 'There is nothing like Him' and 'God of everything' refute any attempt to ascribe partners to God. Moreover, the realization of the world leads to the realization of tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the second part of the source, starting from 'ولم يختلف أهل الإسلام' to the end.
2
Note the consensus
Maturidi states that the people of Islam have unanimously agreed that the Maker is one.
3
Examine the Quranic refutation
He cites two verses: 'There is nothing like unto Him' and 'God of everything', which refute any likeness or partner.
4
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that realizing the world's composition leads to realizing God's oneness, and that tawhid is established by the world's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن العالم لا يخلو من الأعراض والأجسام. وكل أنواع الأعراض أمكن في الحقيقة أن يكون فعلاً لغيره، فيكون العالم لله ولخلقه من طريق الإنشاء والوجود، وفي ذلك بطلان القول بوحدانية صانع العالم. ولم يختلف أهل الإسلام في إطلاق القول بأن صانع العالم واحد. وقول من يُبطل قوله عند التحصيل هذه الجملة التي شارك فيها الجميع مردود بالجملة، على نحو قول الله تعالى: {ليس كمثله شيء} [الشورى، ١١/٤٢]، وقوله: {إله كل شيء}. إن قول الناس الذي في التحصيل يجعل له شبها وعدلاً في العباد منقوض بتلك الجملة، وإن استحال فمثله الأول؛ بل الأول أحق، لأنه طريق العلم بالحرف الثاني؛ وهو أن في تحقيق العالم تحقيق الوحدانية للخالق، وبه يسلم له القول بأن {ليس كمثله شيء} [الشورى، ١١/٤٢]، وأنه الواحد لا شريك له. فإذا ثبت العالم بشركاء له فيه لم يكن هو أحق بأن ليس كمثله شيء من أن يكون كمثله أشياء، أو أن يكون إلها لما أنشأه وأخرجه من العدم إلى الوجود من غيره في ذلك. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And also, indeed the world is not free from accidents and bodies. And every kind of accidents can in reality be an action of another, so the world would be for Allah and for His creation by way of origination and existence, and in that is the invalidation of the statement of the oneness of the Maker of the world. And the people of Islam have not differed in the absolute statement that the Maker of the world is one. And the statement of one who invalidates his statement upon analysis this sentence in which all have participated is refuted by the sentence, in the manner of Allah's saying: {There is nothing like unto Him} [al-Shura 42:11], and His saying: {God of everything}. Indeed the statement of the people which upon analysis makes for Him a likeness and a counterpart among the servants is refuted by that sentence, and if impossibility then its like is the first; rather the first is more deserving, because it is the way of knowledge of the second letter; and that is that in the realization of the world is the realization of the oneness of the Creator, and by it the statement that {There is nothing like unto Him} [al-Shura 42:11] is safe for Him, and that He is the One with no partner. So if the world is established with partners for Him in it, then He is not more deserving that there is nothing like Him than that there be things like Him, or that He be a god for what He originated and brought out from non-existence into existence from other than Him in that. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage presents an argument from the structure of the world. Maturidi states that the world consists of accidents and bodies, and all types of accidents could potentially be the action of another (i.e., the servant). If so, the world would be attributed to both God and creation, which invalidates tawhid. He notes that Muslims agree the Maker is one, so any statement that implies otherwise is refuted by the Quranic verse 'There is nothing like unto Him' and 'God of everything'. The opponent's claim that makes God similar to servants is overturned by this universal sentence. The passage emphasizes that realizing the world's composition leads to realizing God's oneness, and that tawhid is established by the very existence of the world.

Key Terms
أهل الإسلام
وحدانية
شريك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the world's existence does not necessarily prove God's oneness, as it could have multiple creators.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the very acknowledgment of the world's composition leads to the realization of tawhid, and Quranic verses confirm that God has no partner.

Checkpoint
Which Quranic verse does Maturidi use to refute the idea that God has a likeness?

B) {ليس كمثله شيء}
C) {قل هو الله أحد}
D) {الرحمن على العرش استوى}

Key Takeaway
The existence of the world itself, together with Quranic testimony, conclusively establishes God's oneness and refutes any partners.

Will you pause right now and say, 'There is no god but Allah,' with the weight of this realization?

Or have you been taking these signs for granted? What concrete step will you take today to make the world around you a living proof of Allah's oneness?

The Unity of the World as Proof of Tawhid
Maturidi argues for tawhid via the unity of the world and refutes polytheism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the oneness of the world as a direct proof for the absolute oneness of Allah and refutes polytheism.

Core Idea
The unity of the world logically requires the unity of its Creator.

Maturidi begins his argument for tawhid by asserting a foundational truth: the world is one. This simple statement carries profound theological weight. In this passage, he explains how the oneness of creation directly proves the oneness of the Creator, and he refutes those who ascribe partners to Allah by showing the logical impossibility of such a claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the use of Quranic verses as immediate proofs.
2
Identify the key Quranic proofs
Find the two Quranic references Maturidi cites: 'There is nothing like unto Him' (42:11) and 'God of everything.' Understand how they support his claim.
3
Trace the logical link between world unity and Allah's oneness
Maturidi argues that verifying the world as one verifies the oneness of the Creator. Follow this reasoning step by step.
This is the core of the lesson.
4
Understand the refutation of polytheism
Consider Maturidi's counterargument: if the world had partners, then Allah would not be uniquely incomparable. See how he uses this to refute polytheism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العالم واحد. وقول من يُبطل قوله عند التحصيل هذه الجملة التي شارك فيها الجميع مردود بالجملة، على نحو قول الله تعالى: ليس كمثله شيء ﴾ [الشورى، ١١/٤٢]، وقوله: «إله كل شيء» . إن قول الناس [الذي] في التحصيل يجعل له شبها وعدلاً في العباد منقوض بتلك الجملة، وإن استحال فمثله الأول ؛ بل الأول أحق، لأنه طريق العلم بالحرف الثاني؛ وهو أن في تحقيق العالم تحقيق الوحدانية للخالق، وبه يسلم له القول بأن ليس كمثله شيء ﴾ [الشورى، ١١/٤٢] ، وأنه الواحد لا شريك له. فإذا ثبت العالم بشركاء له فيه لم يكن هو أحق بأن ليس كمثله شيء من أن يكون كمثله " أشياء، أو أن يكون إلها لما أنشأه وأخرجه من العدم إلى الوجود من غيره في ذلك. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The world is one. And the statement of one who invalidates his statement upon analysis—this statement in which all share—is refuted by the statement, according to the saying of Allah the Exalted: "There is nothing like unto Him" [Quran 42:11], and His saying: "God of everything." Indeed, the statement of the people [which] upon analysis makes for Him a likeness and a rival among worshippers is refuted by that statement. And if it is impossible (to have a partner), then the first (i.e., His incomparability) is even more true; rather, the first is more deserving, because it is the path to knowledge of the second point—namely, that in verifying the world there is a verification of the oneness of the Creator, and by that the saying "There is nothing like unto Him" [Quran 42:11] is secure for Him, and that He is the One, no partner for Him. So if the world is established with partners in it, He is not more deserving of the attribute "There is nothing like unto Him" than that He has likes, or that He should be a god for what He originated and brought out of non-existence into existence with another in that. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage is a definitional argument for the oneness (tawhid) of Allah and a refutation of those who ascribe partners to Him. Maturidi begins by asserting that the world is one. He then states that the claim of anyone who invalidates this statement upon analysis is itself refuted by the same comprehensive statement that all (monotheists) share—namely, the Quranic verse "There is nothing like unto Him" (Quran 42:11) and the statement "God of everything." He explains that when people claim that Allah has a likeness or a rival among worshippers, this claim is automatically refuted by that verse. And if it is impossible (mustahil) for Allah to have a partner, then the first claim—that He is incomparable—is even more certain; indeed, it is the way to know the second point: that verifying the existence and unity of the world leads to verifying the oneness of its Creator. By this reasoning, the truth of "There is nothing like unto Him" is established, and that He is the One without partner. He then argues: if the world were established with partners (i.e., if multiple creators were real), then Allah would not be more deserving of being described as "nothing like unto Him" than of having likes, or of being a god for what He created along with another. Thus, the unity of the world logically entails the absolute oneness of Allah.

Key Terms
عالم
وحدانية
شريك
ليس كمثله شيء
إله كل شيء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the unity of the world is a separate doctrine, not directly linked to tawhid.
Correction: Maturidi presents the unity of the world as a direct proof of tawhid: the oneness of creation necessitates the oneness of the Creator.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi in this passage, what is the consequence if the world were established with partners (shuraka')?
A) Allah would still be incomparable.
B) Allah would not be more deserving of the attribute 'nothing like unto Him' than having likes.
C) The world would be eternal.
D) The Quranic verse would be meaningless.

Key Takeaway
The oneness of the world is a direct logical proof for the absolute oneness of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you genuinely act as if the world is one and under the control of one God, or do you sometimes feel divided loyalties—as if different aspects of your life are governed by different forces? How can the unity of the world, as argued here, transform the way you face your challenges?
?
Maturidi argues that if there were multiple creators, then Allah would not be more deserving of the attribute 'There is nothing like unto Him'. Have you ever, even subtly, attributed the good things in your life to other than Allah—your own efforts, luck, or other people? What would it mean for you to truly internalize that there is no power except with Allah?
?
The passage uses the Quranic verse 'There is nothing like unto Him' as a refutation. How does this verse affect your view of Allah's attributes? Are there any attributes you mistakenly compare to creation—perhaps thinking of Allah's mercy like human mercy, or His power like human power? How can you worship Allah without any likeness?

The Objection: If Atoms Cannot Be Created, Are They Eternal?
Maturidi presents a counterargument based on the impossibility of creating atoms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dualist argument that the impossibility of creating atoms implies their pre-eternity, and why this is a challenge that Maturidi addresses.

Core Idea
Impossibility of creation does not automatically prove pre-eternity.

The passage presents a key objection raised by those who believed in the eternity of matter, such as the dualists. They argue that since atoms cannot be created (as they are the basic building blocks), they must be pre-eternal. This challenge forces the theologian to clarify the nature of divine power and the distinction between logical impossibility and incapability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compressed style and the abrupt ending.
2
Identify the opponent's argument
Find the line where the opponents conclude that atoms are pre-eternal because their creation is impossible. Understand the logical chain: (1) creation of atoms is impossible → (2) atoms must be eternal.
The opponents are likely dualists who believe in eternal matter.
3
Consider the logical move
The opponents turn the author's own principle against him. They ask: 'Do you take what you mentioned as a principle?' This is a classic dialectical move in kalām.
4
Reflect on the subtle distinction
The common misunderstanding is that impossibility of creation always implies eternity. But in kalām, one must distinguish between impossibility due to logical contradiction (neither God nor anyone can do it) and impossibility due to lack of capability (only God can do it, but He chose not to).
This distinction will be crucial for the refutation that follows.
5
Connect to the broader debate
This objection is one of the key arguments for the eternity of the world. Understanding it helps you see how kalām theologians like Maturidi defend the creation of the universe ex nihilo.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال : فيما لا يكون مثله من العباد ما يوجب إحالته . أرأيت لو عارضك إخوانك فقالوا : تجعل للذي ذكرته أصلا؟ ثم كون الجواهر بالخلق محال ، فثبت قدمها به ،
Then it is said: concerning what does not have its like from servants, what necessitates its impossibility. Do you not see that if your brothers (the dualists) opposed you and said: do you make what you mentioned a principle? Then the being of atoms through creation is impossible, so their pre-eternity is established by it.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This brief passage presents a potential objection and response in the debate. The author states that one might say: regarding that which has no parallel among created beings, that fact necessitates its impossibility (iḥāla). He then imagines an opponent (here referred to as 'your brothers,' likely the dualists or a sect) who retort: 'Do you take what you mentioned (i.e., your principle) as a basis?' The opponent then argues that if the creation of atoms (jawāhir) is impossible, then it follows that atoms are pre-eternal (qadīm). This is a typical argument used to prove the eternity of matter. The author appears to be presenting this as a challenge that needs to be answered. The passage is truncated, but the core idea is that the opponent tries to turn the impossibility of creation into a proof for the pre-eternity of atoms. This is likely part of a larger refutation where Maturidi would argue that the impossibility of creation from one aspect does not preclude creation from another aspect, or that the analogy is flawed. However, the lesson only covers the objection itself.

Key Terms
الجواهر
الخلق
القدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something cannot be created, it must be eternal.
Correction: In kalam, impossibility of creation can be due to logical contradiction (which would imply eternity) or due to lack of capability (which does not imply eternity, as God can create, but the thing in itself cannot be created because it is eternal? Actually the correction is: the opponent's argument conflates impossibility of creation with logical impossibility, but creation of atoms is only impossible if we assume they are eternal; the argument is circular. The correct distinction is that impossibility due to being a necessary existent is different from impossibility due to being an attribute, etc. But per the source: 'kalam philosophers distinguish between impossibility due to logical contradiction and impossibility due to lack of capability.'

Checkpoint
What conclusion do the opponents draw from the supposed impossibility of creating atoms?
A) Atoms are created by God
B) Atoms are contingent
C) Atoms are pre-eternal
D) Atoms are illusions

Key Takeaway
The objection that atoms must be eternal because they cannot be created is a central challenge that Maturidi refutes by distinguishing between different types of impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face an obstacle that seems impossible to overcome, do you assume it is permanent, forgetting that Allah's power is not limited like yours? What would change today if you truly believed nothing is impossible for Him?
?
Have you ever accepted a logical argument that contradicted your faith because it seemed convincing on the surface? Will you commit to examining the premises behind every doubt, as Maturidi did?
?
What is one area of your life where you have mistaken 'difficult for me' for 'impossible for Allah'? What step can you take today to surrender that impossibility to the Creator of atoms?

Consensus as Evidence for Divine Creation
Maturidi cites Muslim consensus as evidence for divine creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the consensus (ijmā') of the Muslim community as a sufficient evidence for God's role as Creator and Lord.

Core Idea
Muslim consensus on God as Creator is itself a sufficient proof.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents a unique kind of evidence: the unanimous belief of the Muslim community. He argues that the coherent transmission of this belief, without any internal contradiction or bias, provides a sufficient proof for God's creatorship. This lesson explores how communal testimony functions as a valid argument in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the flow. Notice the phrase 'من غير اضطراب' (without disturbance).
2
Identify the key claim
Maturidi states that the entirety of what Muslims have transmitted about God being Creator is sufficient evidence. Identify the elements of this claim: God is Creator, everything else is created, He is powerful, Lord, God.
3
Reflect on the role of consensus
Consider why Maturidi uses consensus here. How does it complement rational arguments? This connects to his broader methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الدلالة، فقد أوضحنا لمن عقل لو أنصف؛ مع ما في جملة ما أدّاه المسلمون أن الله خالق وما سواه مخلوق، وأنه قادر على كل شيء، وهو رب كل شيء، وإلهه، من غير اضطراب في ذلك أو ميل قلب إلى خصوص في ذلك دليل كاف. وسنذكر أيضاً بعض ما في ذلك.
As for the evidence, we have already made it clear to whoever understands and is fair. Moreover, in the entirety of what the Muslims have transmitted—that God is the Creator and everything else is created, and that He is powerful over all things, and He is the Lord of everything and its God—without any disturbance in that or inclination of the heart towards any particularity in that, there is sufficient evidence. And we will also mention some of what is in that.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This short passage asserts that the consensus (ijmā') of the Muslim community that God is the Creator of everything, and that all besides Him are created, is itself a sufficient proof (dalīl kāf). Maturidi emphasizes that this belief is transmitted coherently (fi jumlat mā addāhu al-muslimūn) without any internal contradiction or biased inclination. This appeal to consensus serves as a straightforward evidence for God's role as Creator and His absolute power and lordship. He promises to mention more details later. The passage reflects the Maturidi reliance on revealed tradition and communal consensus alongside rational arguments.

Key Terms

إجماع (consensus)
خالق (Creator)
مخلوق (created)
رب (Lord)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Maturidi considers consensus alone sufficient without rational proof.
Correction: Maturidi places consensus as one evidence among others, as he also uses rational arguments elsewhere.

Key Takeaway
The coherent transmission of the Muslim community's belief in God as Creator is itself a sufficient evidence for His existence and attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If the consensus of Muslims—free from disturbance and bias—is sufficient proof of God's creation, then what is the state of your heart when you recite 'Allahu Akbar'? Is there any part of you that hesitates, that wishes for a different lord? Confront that inclination now.

The Wise Maker
Maturidi argues that an action without benefit is not wise, proving a wise creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that an action lacking benefit or harm prevention cannot be attributed to wisdom, and therefore the existence of the world points to a wise Creator.

Core Idea
A purposeless action cannot be wise, so the world must have a wise Maker.

This fragment from Imam Maturidi presents a concise logical argument: if an action provides no benefit and does not prevent harm, it is not an act of wisdom. Applying this to the universe, its existence implies its Creator acted with wisdom and purpose. Let's explore this foundational principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic fragment and its English translation carefully. Notice the incomplete opening word, which likely connects to a prior sentence.
The fragment is short but contains a complete logical unit.
2
Identify the premise
Identify the key claim: an action that does not benefit its doer nor repel harm is not an act of wisdom.
Focus on the phrase 'ليس بحكمة'.
3
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes from this that the One who made the world did so with wisdom ('به'). Understand that 'wisdom' here implies purpose and benefit.
The pronoun 'به' refers back to 'حكمة'.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this argument is important: it establishes that the Creator must be wise, because if His action (creating the world) were not wise, it would be meaningless.
This leads to affirming divine attributes like wisdom and purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انتفع و [ ثبت ] كون فعل لا ينفع فاعله ولا يدفع عنه الضرر ليس بحكمة، فدل أن الذي صنع العالم به.
Benefited [without certainty], [it is established] that the existence of an action that does not benefit its doer and does not repel harm from him is not wisdom, so it indicates that the One who made the world [did so] with [wisdom].
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This short passage appears to be a fragment of a proof. It argues that if an action provides no benefit or prevention of harm, it is not an act of wisdom. Therefore, the fact that the world exists indicates that its Maker created it with wisdom, i.e., for a purpose. The text is incomplete, so the full argument is not present.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an action does not benefit me personally, it may be pointless.
Correction: The argument concerns benefit to the doer himself, not to us. Allah's actions are for His own purpose, not necessarily for our immediate benefit, but they are still wise.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage says a wise action must benefit the doer. Since Allah is free from need, why did He create the world? Your life is part of that creation—are you living as if you were created for a wise purpose? What needs to change?
?
If an action without benefit is not wise, then consider your daily habits. Which routine lacks benefit for your soul? How can you transform it into an act of worship that seeks the ultimate benefit of Allah's pleasure? Commit to one change now.

The Logical Necessity of Universal Creation for Praise
Maturidi's logical proof that universal creation is necessary for divine praise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that if anything in existence were not created by God, His praise would be false or shared with others.

Core Idea
God's praise demands His creation of all things without exception.

Maturidi begins by establishing a fundamental principle: the Qur’anic verses that praise God as Creator are genuine. He then argues that if any thing were excluded from His creation, the praise would either apply to something not His or He would be sharing praise with every weak being. This introduction sets the stage for a rigorous rational proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening argument
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning to 'وفي التخصيص إيجاب الأول'. Pay attention to how Maturidi lays out the two possibilities if anything were excluded.
Note the logical structure: if not created by God, then either the thing belongs to someone else (praise for other) or it is shared.
2
Analyze the two absurd consequences
Identify the two absurd possibilities: either praise is for something not His (lie) or others become equal creators (corrupt). Consider why each is untenable.
Maturidi shows that the second possibility also leads to the first via transfer argument.
3
Trace the conclusion
Follow how Maturidi concludes that the initial description (praise and lordship) requires universal creation, and that specification of some things only affirms the first.
The phrase 'وفي التخصيص إيجاب الأول' is key: specifying some things as not created actually affirms the first description.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : نقول وبالله التوفيق إذ ثبت أن الآية بحق الامتداح، كان في خروج شيء من الكائنات امتداحاً بغير الذي له، أو بما يشاركه فيه كل ضعيف؛ لأنه لو أراد كلية الأشياء ولم يكن خلقها، فامتدح بغير الذي له، وذلك كذب . وفي إخراج البعض مساواة غيره في أنه صانع كل شيء، يريد ما لا صنع له فيه، وذلك فاسد. مع ما لو جاز ذلك على الصرف إلى الغير الذي هو فعل للغير ليجوز أن يُقال: ليس بخالق شيء، على أنه ليس بخالق ما هو فعل لغيره. فإذ كان وصفا له بالذم والعبودة ثبت أن الأول وصف له بالمدح والربوبية، وفي التخصيص إيجاب الأول . وأيضا إنه قال : هو رب كل شيء وإله كل شيء، وهو على كل شيء وكيل ، ولم يجز إخراج شيء عن ذلك؛ وإن كان لا يليق القول به على التخصيص لقبح، نحو أن يقال: رب الخبائث وإله القبائح ووكيل الشياطين وإبليس، وقائم على كل نَتِن وقذر، فمثله الأول، وإن كان يقبح على التخصيص في أشياء من حيث التسمية. وبهذا الوجه الذي قال شهدت المجوس والزنادقة أن الله تعالى لم يخلق مؤذياً ولا فسادًا، ولا أمات وليا ولا قوسى عدوا ولا أبقى الشيطان، ولا أعطى من يعلم أنه يشتمه ويصد عن طاعته أحدا قوة . ذكرنا ذلك ليعلموا أن أصل الاعتزال مقدر عن ذلك؛ إذ إليه فزعهم عند مخالفتهم المفهوم من القرآن ومما جرى عليه قول الإسلام، ولذلك قال رسول الله عليه السلام: «القدرية مجوس هذه الأمة». ولو جاز خروج شيء من أن يكون هو له خالقا لجاز مثله عن الملك والربوبية ونحو ذلك من أسماء الامتداح ، فيبطل أن يكون له مدح بشيء، لما في كل شيء له شركاء في حقيقة معناه. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The Shaykh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: We say, and with Allah is success: Since it is established that the verse is a rightful praise, if something among created things were excluded, it would be praise for something not His, or for something in which every weak one shares. For if He intended all things but did not create them, He would be praising by what is not His, and that is a lie. And in excluding some things is making another equal to Him in that he is a maker of everything, intending what he has no making in, and that is corrupt. Moreover, if that were allowed in transferring [the act] to another which is the act of another, it would be permissible to say: He is not the creator of anything, on the basis that He is not the creator of what is the act of another. So since it is a description of Him by blame and servitude, it is established that the first is a description of Him by praise and lordship, and in specification is affirmation of the first. And also He said: He is the Lord of everything and the God of everything, and He is over everything a guardian, and it is not permissible to exclude anything from that; even if it is not fitting to say it of specification due to ugliness, such as saying: Lord of filths, God of abominations, Guardian of devils and Iblis, and standing over every foul and unclean thing, then the like of it is the first, even if it is ugly in specification in some things by name. And in this respect, the Magians and the Zindiqs have testified that Allah Almighty did not create anything harmful nor corruption, nor did He cause the death of a saint nor did He strengthen an enemy nor did He leave the devil, nor did He give power to anyone knowing that he would revile Him and turn away from His obedience. We have mentioned this so that they may know that the foundation of Mu'tazilism is based on that; for to it they flee when they contradict the understanding of the Qur'an and what the saying of Islam has proceeded upon, and therefore the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'The Qadariyya are the Magians of this community.' And if it were permissible that anything should be outside His being Creator of it, the like would be permissible concerning sovereignty and lordship and similar names of praise, so that there would be no praise for Him in anything, since in everything He has partners in the reality of its meaning. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God must be the Creator of all things, including actions, and that any exception would undermine His praise and lordship. Maturidi begins by stating that the verse (such as 6:102) is a praise (imtidaah). If anything among created things were outside God's creation, then the praise would either be for something not His, or He would share it with every weak being—both absurd. If God intended all things but did not create them, His praise would be false. If He created only some, then others are equal to Him as creators, which is corrupt. Moreover, if one transfers the act to another, then one could say God is not the Creator of anything, which is blameworthy. Thus, the first description—praise and lordship—requires that He be Creator of all. Maturidi then cites the verse 'He is the Lord of everything, the God of everything, and over everything a guardian' to show nothing can be excluded. Even if specific designations like 'Lord of filths' sound inappropriate, that does not change the universal meaning. He then notes that the Magians and Zindiqs claimed God did not create harmful things, and this is the root of Mu'tazilism. The Prophet (peace be upon him) declared the Qadariyya the Magians of this community. If anything were outside God's creation, then other exalted names like 'King' would also have exceptions, and His praise would be nullified because everything would have partners in its meaning.

Key Terms
امتداح
خالق كل شيء
تخصيص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, if some created things were not created by God, what would be the consequence for His praise?
A) His praise would be specifically true for those things
B) His praise would be for something not His or shared
C) His praise would be limited to good things
D) His praise would be unaffected

Key Takeaway
God's praise necessitates His creation of all things, including actions, because any exception makes the praise false or incomplete.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly praise God as Creator of every detail in your life, including your own choices and their outcomes, or do you subtly attribute some good to your own ability? How will you train your heart to see His hand in everything today?
?
When you fail, do you blame circumstances or yourself, forgetting that God is the Creator of all events? What would change in your repentance if you believed He created even your mistakes?
?
If you praise God as 'Creator of everything', does your life reflect that belief in how you view the actions of others? Are you quick to judge, or do you see God's creation in their deeds as well?

The Qur’anic Evidence for Universal Lordship and the Issue of Ugly Names
Maturidi cites Qur’anic evidence for universal creation and addresses the objection from ugly names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the verse 'He is the Lord of everything' to argue that nothing can be excluded from God's dominion, even if specific designations sound inappropriate.

Core Idea
God’s lordship and guardianship cover everything, even if naming some things is verbally unpleasant.

Maturidi now turns to a direct Qur’anic proof: Allah says He is the Lord of everything, the God of everything, and over everything a guardian. He argues that it is not permissible to exclude anything from these descriptions, even though applying them to specific objects like 'Lord of filths' sounds ugly. This illustrates that the universal meaning must be upheld regardless of the name's aesthetic quality.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and its application
Read the passage starting with 'وأيضا إنه قال : هو رب كل شيء' up to 'في أشياء من حيث التسمية'. Notice how Maturidi quotes the verse and then discusses the prohibition of exclusion.
The verse is likely 6:102 or 11:57 or similar.
2
Understand the 'ugliness' issue
Maturidi acknowledges that saying 'Lord of filths' or 'God of abominations' is ugly in Arabic expression, but that does not change the universal truth. He distinguishes between the literal meaning and the verbal form.
This shows that Maturidi is attentive to linguistic propriety while maintaining theological rigor.
3
Connect to the previous argument
Reflect on how this Qur’anic proof strengthens the earlier logical argument. The verse explicitly declares universality, so any exception would contradict the divine text.
The phrase 'ومثله الأول' indicates that the same principle applies to the earlier description of praise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that God is the Guardian over everything, including the filth and ugliness in your own life, how does that change your understanding of His constant presence? Are you comfortable that He sees every part of you, even the parts you consider shameful? What would it mean for you to truly accept that nothing is excluded from His lordship—including your darkest secrets?
?

Have you ever avoided acknowledging God's sovereignty over something because it felt too ugly or shameful to bring before Him? What would it take for you to place that thing under His lordship right now?
?
This passage shows that God's lordship covers everything without exception, yet we use language that obscures that universality. In what areas of your life do you verbally acknowledge God's control, but internally exclude certain things? How can you change your speech and thoughts to truly reflect 'He is Lord of everything'?

Refutation of Dualism and Mu‘tazilism: God Creates All Things
Maturidi uses the example of Magians to show the error of excluding things from God's creation, and concludes that any exception undermines all divine praise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's connection of the creation argument to refute Majian dualism and Mu‘tazilite libertarianism, and the final universal implication for divine names.

Core Idea
Denying God’s creation of anything leads to attributing partners in lordship.

Maturidi sharpens his argument by pointing to the historical error of the Magians and Zindiqs, who claimed God did not create harmful things. This, he says, is the foundation of Mu‘tazilism. He then broadens the conclusion: if any thing were outside God’s creation, then other names of praise like 'King' would also have partners, destroying all praise. The lesson ends with the famous hadith: 'The Qadariyya are the Magians of this community.'

Guided Path
1
Read the historical references
Read the passage from 'وبهذا الوجه الذي قال' to the end. Notice how Maturidi mentions the claims of Magians and Zindiqs and links them to the Mu‘tazilites.
This shows the real-world implications of the theological error.
2
Understand the hadith
The Prophet said: 'The Qadariyya are the Magians of this community.' Consider why the Qadariyya (those who deny predestination) are compared to Magians.
Both attribute some events to a source other than God.
3
Trace the broader conclusion
Maturidi argues that if any exception is allowed, then all divine names of praise would have partners, nullifying all praise. Reflect on why this is a necessary conclusion.
The phrase 'ولا قوة إلا بالله' is a closing prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God does not create evil actions; humans create their own evil acts.
Correction: Maturidi shows that such a view aligns with Magian dualism and would allow partners in divine lordship, destroying the basis for God's praise.

Checkpoint
What does the Prophet's hadith say about the Qadariyya?
A) They are the best of the community
B) They are the Magians of this community
C) They are the most knowledgeable
D) They are the source of guidance

Key Takeaway
Excluding anything from God’s creation leads to associating partners with Him in lordship and nullifying His praise, as exemplified by the Qadariyya.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do I ever, even for a moment, think that a good or bad event in my life was not decreed or created by Allah? How does that thought affect my gratitude or patience? This lesson demands that I examine my heart: do I truly attribute everything to Allah, or do I carve out exceptions that diminish His sovereignty? Will I repent of that subtle shirk today?
?
The hadith says the Qadariyya are the Magians of this ummah. What if I have a Qadari-like tendency in my own thinking—for example, when I blame my sin on my own free will without acknowledging that Allah creates my actions? Am I willing to fully submit to Allah's creation of even my evil deeds, trusting that His wisdom is perfect? This lesson calls me to abandon any sense of independence and to say 'La hawla wa la quwwata illa billah' with complete conviction.
?
If any exception to Allah's creation nullifies His praise, then my praise of Him must be based on His absolute exclusivity. When I praise Him, do I truly mean that He is the sole creator of everything, including the trials I face and the strength I have? Or do I unconsciously reserve some credit for myself? This lesson forces me to check my tongue and heart: is my praise undivided? Let me resolve to say 'Alhamdulillah' for everything, not just the pleasant things.

Allah's Existence
beginner
6 min
Divine Creation vs. Human Action
Maturidi discusses the establishment of similarity in action and its negation, and distinguishes divine creation from human acquisition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Allah refutes anthropomorphism by establishing that no creature can produce a creation like His, and the distinction between divine creation ex nihilo and human acquisition.

Core Idea
Allah alone creates from nothing; human acts are only acquisition, never creation like His.

Maturidi begins by showing that Allah Himself addresses the claim of similarity in action. He first states that the polytheists' claim of their idols creating like Allah would have excused their worship if true, but it is false. Then he explains the nature of creation: it cannot be observed directly; it is known by its product. He asks whether creation means bringing from nothing or merely acquisition and motion. This distinction is crucial to understand why no human act can be considered a creation like Allah's.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start with Maturidi's premise: Allah established similarity in action, then negated it. Understand the hypothetical excuse for polytheists.
The passage uses a hypothetical 'if' to highlight the logical consequence.
2
Understand the distinction
Notice the two types of origination: coming from non-existence (divine) vs. acquisition and movement (human). This is the core of the argument.
The word 'or' (أو) indicates a binary distinction.
3
Reflect on the implication
If one equates human acts to divine acts, that would imply a creation like Allah's, which is false. This shows why tashbih is refuted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه رحمه الله : نقول وبالله التوفيق: أثبت الله تعالى التشابه من حيث الفعل، حيث قال: خلقوا كخلقه فتشابه الخلق عليهم، نفي أن يكون من أحد خلق كخلقه، وأوجب لهم العذر في عبادتهم ما كانوا يعبدون لو كان منهم خلق كخلقه. ثم لا سبيل إلى معاينة كيفية الإنشاء وإنما يعلم بالمنشأ : إنه في حق الخروج من العدم إلى الوجود والحدث من لا أصل، أو هو في حق الكسب والتحرك والسكون؟ فمن حقق للعبد من الفعل من الوجه الذي يحقق من الله فقد وجد خلق كخلقه، إذ لا وجه لفعله غير ذلك. ولو كان بالذي يذكره لكان لا وجه للاحتجاج؛ لأنهم لو أثبتوا يقولون : ليس ذلك كذلك، لأن الذي منكم كان بعلاج، وهذا النوع من الخيال. ثم أتبع ذلك الحرف ودفع إمكان حقيقته بقوله : خالق كل شيء ليعلم كل أنه أي شيء أضافه إلى أحد أنه خلقه لم يقدر عليه لوجود ضرورات له فيه توجب تدبير غيره في ذلك. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقد قال الله وجل : إذا لذهب كل إله بما خلق، وإذا جعلت لكل من المعنى الذي به وصف الله تعالى بالخلق، ويذهب كل بالذي منه، فكان في ذلك تثبيت آلهة، ذهب كل بما خلق ولا قوة إلا بالله.
The jurist, may Allah have mercy on him, said: We say, and with Allah is success: Allah the Exalted established similarity in terms of action, when He said: 'They created like His creation, so the creation became similar to them.' He negated that anyone should have a creation like His creation, and He excused them in their worship of what they used to worship if only there had been from them a creation like His creation. Then there is no way to observe the modality of origination; rather it is known by the originated thing: Is it in terms of coming out from non-existence to existence and temporality (al-ḥadath) from no origin, or is it in terms of acquisition (al-kasb), movement, and stillness? Whoever attributes to the servant an act in the same manner that he attributes to Allah has indeed found a creation like His creation, for there is no aspect of his act other than that. And if it were as he (al-Kaʿbī) mentions, there would be no ground for argument; because if they (the polytheists) asserted, they would say: 'It is not so, because that which is from you is through effort,' and this is a kind of fantasy. Then He (Allah) followed that with the letter and refuted the possibility of its reality by His saying: 'Creator of everything,' so that everyone may know that whatever one attributes to anyone, that he created it, he cannot do so because there are necessities in it that necessitate the management of another in that. And there is no power except with Allah. And Allah, may He be glorified and exalted, said: 'Then each god would have taken away what he created,' and if each is given some of the meaning by which Allah is described with creation, and each would take away what is from him, then in that would be the establishment of gods, each taking away what he created, and there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage is a complex proof against anthropomorphism (tashbīh). The author, Imam Maturidi, begins by stating that Allah established a kind of similarity (tashābuh) with respect to action by pointing out that the polytheists claimed their idols created like His creation, so the creations appeared similar to them. But then Allah negated that any being can have a creation like His. Next, Maturidi discusses the nature of creation: it cannot be observed in its modality; rather it is known by the originated thing. He asks whether creation means bringing into existence from nothing (al-ikhṯirāʿ) or acquisition (kasb) and motion. If a person's act is considered in the same way as Allah's act, then that person would have a creation like Allah's, which is impossible. He then refutes al-Kaʿbī's suggestion that similarity arises from the perspective of temporality (ḥadath). Maturidi concludes by citing the verse 'Creator of everything' and the verse 'Then each god would have taken away what he created' to demonstrate that if multiple creators existed, each would have a distinct creation, implying multiplicity of gods, which is false. The argument shows that Allah alone is the creator and any notion of similarity is refuted.
