Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human acts are a form of creation like Allah's creation from nothing.
Correction: Human acts are only acquisition (kasb) and involve movement and stillness, not bringing from non-existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for distinguishing divine creation from human action?
A) The modality of origination is observable.
B) Divine creation is bringing from nothing; human action is acquisition and movement.
C) Both are similar in essence.
D) Human action is also creation from nothing.

Key Takeaway
Allah's creation is uniquely bringing from nothing; human acts are only acquisition, and any equation leads to anthropomorphism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accomplish something through your own effort, do you ever feel you 'created' that success? How does this passage challenge that feeling, forcing you to admit your act is only acquisition, not origination? Where in your heart do you need to surrender the illusion of being a co-creator with Allah?
?
The polytheists were excused if their idols had actually created something, but they hadn't. What excuses do you make for attributing effects to yourself or others that only Allah can create? In what specific areas of your life today—your work, your relationships, your worship—do you subtly claim a 'creation like His creation'?
?
If every human action is merely acquisition, then what is your true role before Allah? This lesson says that attributing any act to a human in the same manner as to Allah leads to anthropomorphism. Where do you need to realign your understanding of your own agency, so that you neither deny your responsibility nor overstep into claiming divine-like power?

Refuting Multiplicity of Creators
Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi's argument and uses Quranic verses to prove Allah's exclusive creativity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the claim that similarity in temporality (hadath) implies similarity in creation, and the Quranic proof that only Allah is the Creator of everything, negating any shared divinity.

Core Idea
Temporality does not make human acts like divine acts; the Quran affirms Allah alone as Creator of everything.

After establishing the distinction between divine and human acts, Maturidi addresses a specific objection from al-Ka'bi, who argued that similarity arises from the fact that both divine and human acts are temporal (hadith). Maturidi refutes this by showing that if that were the case, the polytheists would have no basis to argue, as they would point out that the prophet's acts involve effort while the idol's acts do not. Then he brings Quranic proof from the verse 'Creator of everything' and the verse 'Each god would have taken away what he created' to demonstrate that multiple creators would necessitate multiple distinct creations, which is false.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
Understand al-Ka'bi's argument and Maturidi's counter: if temporality were the basis, polytheists could object that human acts involve effort, not like divine acts.
This highlights the flaw in reasoning.
2
Examine the Quranic proof
Reflect on the verse 'Creator of everything' and its implication: no one else can truly create.
This verse establishes exclusive creation.
3
Analyze the verse about multiple gods
Consider the logical consequence: if multiple creators existed, each would have his own creation, leading to separate dominions.
This contradicts the unity of Allah.
4
Conclude
Thus, any claim of similarity in creation is false, and only Allah is the Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Tashabuh Necessitates Contingency
Maturidi's argument from tashabuh to huduth to the single Maker.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi uses the similarity (tashabuh) in occurents to prove the contingency of the world and the existence of a single Creator.

Core Idea
Similarity among events points to their contingent nature and back to the Maker.

Imam Maturidi begins by establishing a foundational argument: the similarity we observe in the world is not accidental but reveals that all things are contingent. He then shows how this contingency necessarily points to an originating Creator. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compressed style—each phrase carries significant meaning.
2
Identify the premise and conclusion
The passage states that tashabuh necessitates contingency. Then it uses a conditional to reinforce that the presence of tashabuh confirms the necessity of contingency. Find these elements.
The conclusion is that tashabuh is a sign (aya) of the Maker.
3
Reflect on tashabuh as evidence
Ask yourself: why does similarity serve as a proof? Consider how the regular patterns in nature imply dependence on a cause.
This connects to the broader kalam argument for God's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تشابه يوجب حدثه بحدث الشيء. فلو لم يقع من حيث الحدث تشابه لم يكن يُنفى من حيث لزوم الحدث مع ما كانت الحوادث في الأجسام هي أدلة حدثها، وحدث الأجسام هو دلالة المحدث الصانع، وذلك كله آية التشابه. فقوله : لا يقع بذا تشابه، لا معنى له. وقوله : ذا ذا، فإن خلق الشيء عندهم هو الخلق. ولا شك في الخلق ذلة وخضوع وحاجة وعيوب وشيطان وشر وفتنة وبلاء وفساد ونتن وخبث وقذر، كل هذه أوصاف فعل الله تعالى عند المعتزلة بقوله : خلق الشيء هو ذلك الشيء، فكيف أنكر هذه الأحوال خلقه ؟ وقوله : هو تطوّل وتفضل؛ فإذا إبليس هو خلقه عندهم، وفعله في الحقيقة تطوّل وتفضّل، وهو خير وحسن، وهو حكمة وصواب. وهذا كله قول وحش، لم يجز إطلاق ذلك إلا بصلات توضح المراد، فمثله الذي ذكر، وليس من هذا الوجه دفع التشابه ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم العجب ممن يعجب منه، وفي ذلك أنه واحد، والتشابه والاختلاف أبدا يقع في الأغيار. وجملة ذلك أنا نجد فعل العبد من الوجه الذي عليه أمر العالم الله، فثبت أن خالق العالم كله واحد، وإنما يجعل للعبد لا من ذلك الوجه. والله الموفق.
The similarity necessitates its contingency by the contingency of the thing. For if similarity did not occur from the aspect of contingency, it would not be negated from the aspect of the necessity of contingency with what the occurents in bodies are the proofs of their contingency, and the contingency of bodies is the indication of the originating Maker, and all of that is a sign of similarity. So his statement: 'similarity does not occur from this' has no meaning. And his statement: 'this, this' — for the creation of a thing according to them is the creation itself. And there is no doubt that in creation there is lowliness, submission, need, defects, satan, evil, trial, calamity, corruption, stench, badness, filth — all these are attributes of the act of God Almighty according to the Mu'tazila by his statement: 'the creation of the thing is that thing.' So how can he deny these states of His creation? And his statement: 'it is grace and favor' — then Iblis is His creation according to them, and His act is in reality grace and favor, and it is good and beautiful, and it is wisdom and correctness. All of this is a wild statement, not permissible to utter except with qualifying clauses that clarify the meaning. So the like of it which he mentioned is not from this aspect a refutation of similarity. And there is no power except through God. Then the marvel is at the one who marvels at it, and in that He is One, and similarity and difference always occur in the others. The summary of that is that we find the act of the servant from the aspect upon which the affair of the world is of God, so it is established that the Creator of the whole world is One, and it is attributed to the servant only from that aspect. And God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal argument and refutation. Imam Maturidi begins by stating that similarity (tashabuh) between occurents necessarily entails their contingency (huduth). He argues that if similarity did not occur from the aspect of contingency, then the necessity of contingency would not be negated, since occurents in bodies are themselves proofs of contingency, and the contingency of bodies points to the originating Maker. Thus, tashabuh is a sign of the Creator. He then refutes an opponent (likely al-Ka'bi) who claims that no similarity occurs. Maturidi says this claim is meaningless. He also refutes the idea that creation (khalq) is identical to the created thing, a Mu'tazilite position. If that were so, creation would include lowliness, evil, etc., which are attributed to God, contradicting the notion that God's act is pure grace. The opponent's claim that all is grace leads to absurdity, such as considering Iblis (Satan) as grace. Maturidi calls this a wild statement that requires qualification. He then marvels at those who object to this reasoning, noting that God is One, and similarity and difference occur only among contingent things (aghyar). The conclusion: the act of the servant is ultimately from God, so the Creator is One, and the act is only attributed to the servant from a specific aspect.

Key Terms
تشابه (tashabuh)
حدث (huduth)
إحداث (ihdath)
آية (sign)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that similarity in the world is random and does not prove anything about God.
Correction: Maturidi argues that similarity is precisely what necessitates contingency and points to a single Creator.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Maturidi, what does tashabuh (similarity) in occurents primarily prove?
A) The contingency of the world and the oneness of the Creator
B) The eternity of the world
C) That God's acts are purely grace without any mixed attributes
D) That human acts are fully independent of divine power

Key Takeaway
Tashabuh in the world is a rational proof for the contingency of all things and the existence of one Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the repeated patterns and similarities in your daily life—like the rising of the sun or the beating of your heart—do you recognize them as signs of a single, wise Creator? What would change in your gratitude if you truly saw each similarity as a divine proof?
?
Your existence is similar to every other human who has lived and died. That similarity points to your contingency and need for Allah. How does this awareness affect your sense of purpose and dependence on Him right now?
?
The proof from tashabuh shows that even the mundane similarities we overlook are divine signs. Is there a similarity in your life—a repeated test, a blessing, a relationship—that you have been dismissing as mere coincidence? How can you turn it into a moment of faith and accountability today?

Refuting Denials of Tashabuh and Misconceptions about Creation
Maturidi refutes opponent's denial of tashabuh and the Mu'tazilite identity of khalq and makhluk, then concludes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutations of the claim that tashabuh has no role and the Mu'tazilite identification of creation with the created thing.

Core Idea
Denying tashabuh is meaningless; equating creation with the thing created leads to absurd attributions to God.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi addresses two objections. First, he dismisses the claim that tashabuh does not occur. Second, he refutes the Mu'tazilite view that creation is identical to the created thing, showing how it would force one to attribute evil and defects to God. He then concludes with the unity of the Creator and the proper understanding of human acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Note the abrupt dismissal of the opponent's claim.
This passage contains a refutation, so expect a tone of critique.
2
Identify the two refutations
First, the opponent's denial of tashabuh is rejected as meaningless. Second, the Mu'tazilite view that khalq equals the created thing is attacked by listing the negative attributes that would then apply to God's act.
Notice how Maturidi uses reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that the Creator is one, that similarity occurs only among contingent things, and that the servant's act is from God in one aspect.
This reinforces tawhid and proper attribution of actions.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this refutation protects God's transcendence and justice. Why does Maturidi call the Mu'tazilite statement a 'wild statement'?
It underscores the need for qualified language in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The similarity necessitates its contingency by the contingency of the thing. For if similarity did not occur from the aspect of contingency, it would not be negated from the aspect of the necessity of contingency with what the occurents in bodies are the proofs of their contingency, and the contingency of bodies is the indication of the originating Maker, and all of that is a sign of similarity. So his statement: 'similarity does not occur from this' has no meaning. And his statement: 'this, this' — for the creation of a thing according to them is the creation itself. And there is no doubt that in creation there is lowliness, submission, need, defects, satan, evil, trial, calamity, corruption, stench, badness, filth — all these are attributes of the act of God Almighty according to the Mu'tazila by his statement: 'the creation of the thing is that thing.' So how can he deny these states of His creation? And his statement: 'it is grace and favor' — then Iblis is His creation according to them, and His act is in reality grace and favor, and it is good and beautiful, and it is wisdom and correctness. All of this is a wild statement, not permissible to utter except with qualifying clauses that clarify the meaning. So the like of it which he mentioned is not from this aspect a refutation of similarity. And there is no power except through God. Then the marvel is at the one who marvels at it, and in that He is One, and similarity and difference always occur in the others. The summary of that is that we find the act of the servant from the aspect upon which the affair of the world is of God, so it is established that the Creator of the whole world is One, and it is attributed to the servant only from that aspect. And God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal argument and refutation. Imam Maturidi begins by stating that similarity (tashabuh) between occurents necessarily entails their contingency (huduth). He argues that if similarity did not occur from the aspect of contingency, then the necessity of contingency would not be negated, since occurents in bodies are themselves proofs of contingency, and the contingency of bodies points to the originating Maker. Thus, tashabuh is a sign of the Creator. He then refutes an opponent (likely al-Ka'bi) who claims that no similarity occurs. Maturidi says this claim is meaningless. He also refutes the idea that creation (khalq) is identical to the created thing, a Mu'tazilite position. If that were so, creation would include lowliness, evil, etc., which are attributed to God, contradicting the notion that God's act is pure grace. The opponent's claim that all is grace leads to absurdity, such as considering Iblis (Satan) as grace. Maturidi calls this a wild statement that requires qualification. He then marvels at those who object to this reasoning, noting that God is One, and similarity and difference occur only among contingent things (aghyar). The conclusion: the act of the servant is ultimately from God, so the Creator is One, and the act is only attributed to the servant from a specific aspect.

Key Terms
خلق (khalq)
إحداث (ihdath)
أغيار (aghyar)
تطوّل (grace/favor)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mu'tazilites believed that creation (khalq) is identical to the created thing, so God's act of creating is the thing itself.
Correction: Maturidi shows this leads to attributing evil and imperfections directly to God, which is unacceptable.

Checkpoint
What does Imam Maturidi say about the Mu'tazilite view that creation (khalq) is identical to the created thing?
A) It is a valid opinion
B) It leads to attributing defects to God's act
C) It proves human free will
D) It is the same as the Ash'ari view

Key Takeaway
Denying tashabuh is baseless, and equating creation with the created leads to theological absurdity; true tawhid is maintained by recognizing the Creator's oneness and the proper attribution of acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness evil and suffering in the world, do you secretly doubt God's goodness? How does Maturidi's separation of the act of creation from the thing created protect your heart from accusing God of injustice? What will you remind yourself of when such doubts arise?
?
Are there ways in which you, like the Mu'tazilites, try to rationalize God's actions by your own limited standards, effectively making Him accountable to your sense of 'grace' and 'favor'? Will you repent from this subtle arrogance and submit to His absolute wisdom, even when you cannot comprehend?
?
The opponent's denial of tashabuh is declared meaningless. In your daily speech, do you sometimes utter statements about God that are equally meaningless—statements that seem pious but actually misrepresent His nature? What specific phrase will you stop using today?

Maturidi's Balanced Path to Knowing God
Maturidi defines proper knowledge and refutes Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's balanced approach to knowledge of God: neither negating nor affirming what is not due, and how this refutes the Mu'tazila's agnosticism.

Core Idea
True knowledge of God avoids both false denial and false affirmation.

Before entering into theological disputes, Maturidi sets out the foundation of proper knowledge of God. He insists that our knowledge is limited to what God has revealed in creation, and that we must not cross the boundaries of truth. This lesson unpacks his critique of the Mu'tazila, who he claims make knowledge of God impossible, leading to perpetual doubt.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to get the overall sense.
The Arabic has some textual ambiguity; focus on the main ideas.
2
Identify Maturidi's positive definition
Notice how Maturidi defines proper knowledge: it is about what God has defined in creation. This sets the limits.
He avoids speculative metaphysics.
3
Understand the boundaries
Maturidi states two boundaries: do not negate what is God's, and do not affirm what is not His. Violating these is falsehood and disbelief.
This is the middle path.
4
Grasp the critique of the Mu'tazila
Contrast with the Mu'tazila who claim no way to know God, leading to perpetual doubt.
Maturidi treats this as undermining God's self-revelation.
5
Reflect on the Quranic proof
Maturidi cites Quran 23:91 to show that the Mu'tazila's position inadvertently implies multiple gods, which is refuted.
The verse speaks of each god taking away what he created.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المكتسب وإنما [ هو ] على معرفة ما الله ، لا عَرَّفه في خلقه. لا أنفي عنه ما هو له، ولا أُثبت له ما ليس له فأكون كاذبًا عليه، وذلك كفر . وعلى قول المعتزلة لا وجه لمعرفته، فيحصل أبدا على الشك، ولا يصل إليه ؛ وذلك هو المعنى الذي نفي الله أن يكون معه إله، حققه أهل الاعتزال سفها بغير علم، وذلك في قوله : إذا لذهب كل إله بما خلق ﴾ [المؤمنون، ۹۱/۲۳]. والله الموفق.
The acquired [knowledge] is only about knowing what God has defined in His creation. I do not negate from Him what is His, nor do I affirm for Him what is not His, lest I be a liar against Him, and that is disbelief. And according to the saying of the Mu'tazila, there is no way to know Him, so one remains forever in doubt and does not reach Him. And that is the meaning of God negating that there is any god with Him, which the Mu'tazila have actualized foolishly without knowledge, and that is in His saying: 'If there were other gods with Him, each would have taken away what he created.' [Quran 23:91] And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage defines the proper approach to knowledge of God. Maturidi states that the acquired knowledge is confined to what God has defined in His creation. He then clarifies the boundaries: one must not negate what is truly attributed to God, nor affirm what is not His, as that would be falsehood and disbelief. He contrasts this with the Mu'tazila's claim that there is no way to know God, which leads to perpetual doubt and failure to reach knowledge. Maturidi then connects this to the Quranic verse that refutes the existence of multiple gods, implying that the Mu'tazila's stance inadvertently undermines divine unity. The passage concludes with a prayer for success.

Key Terms
معرفة الله
المعتزلة
إله
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that negating attributes from God is acceptable as long as one affirms His existence.
Correction: Maturidi explicitly states that both false negation and false affirmation constitute lying against God and are disbelief.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the Mu'tazila's claim about knowing God lead to?
A) Certainty
B) Perpetual doubt and failure to reach Him
C) Knowledge of His essence
D) Affirmation of all attributes

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of God is achieved by affirming what He has revealed and denying false claims, avoiding both extremism and agnosticism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been content with doubt about God, thinking it is safe? Maturidi calls that disbelief. Where in your life have you settled for uncertainty instead of seeking the knowledge God has provided? What step will you take today to move from doubt to conviction?
?
Maturidi warns against affirming for God what is not His. Are there false images of God you hold—perhaps from culture, emotions, or your own preferences—that you need to discard? How will you replace them with what God has defined about Himself in revelation?
?
The Mu'tazila's denial of knowledge leads to perpetual doubt. Are there teachings of Islam you have treated as unknowable, using that as an excuse not to act or commit? What would it mean for you to trust that God has made Himself known clearly enough for you to respond with certainty?

The Proof of Human Incapacity for Divine Action
Maturidi argues that human incapacity reveals God as the sole creator of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that human incapacity provides clearer proof of God's creation of actions than the creation of the heavens and earth.

Core Idea
Human limits prove divine action more directly than the cosmos.

Maturidi presents a powerful argument from direct human experience. Instead of looking outward to the heavens, he looks inward at our own actions and their limits. He claims that the very incapacity we feel in performing certain actions is the clearest indication that those actions are created by God. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim
Maturidi states that the indication of God's creation of every person's action is greater than the indication of creating the heavens and earth. Why? Because human reason can directly apprehend the limits of human capacity.
This is a bold claim – it prioritizes personal experience over cosmic observation.
2
Examine the reasoning
He argues that we haven't tested all substances to know what they can produce; rather, we know something is beyond possibility because its like cannot be produced. For example, flying, piercing objects, and swimming are beyond the capacity of certain substances despite having some powers.
This reasoning shows that limits are known by the impossibility of performing similar acts.
3
Apply to human actions
Everyone knows that their actions are not fully under their control; things exceed intention or fall short of intended limits. This demonstrates that the action is determined by another.
Our own experience of incapacity becomes evidence for a creator.
4
Conclude the argument
The one who established the action is the one who determined it – and that is God. The passage ends with 'there is no power except in God'.
This conclusion ties back to the principle that God is the ultimate agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يفعل ، وقد ألزم المؤمنين أن يحمدوه على الإيمان" ثبت أن كان [ذلك " ] بفعله. ولا قوة إلا بالله . والأصل فيه أن دلالة خلق فعل كل أحد عنده أعظم من دلالة خلق السموات والأرض فيما أريد تعرف / حقيقة ذلك بالعقل ؛ إنه لا أحد امتحن قوى جواهر العالم حتى يعلم خروج [١٣٤] كل شيء عن ذلك " [ و ] احتمال خلق مثله، بل إنما يعرف ذلك بخروجه عن إمكان مثله. ومعلوم وجود أمور في غيره من الجواهر مما امتنع جوهره عن احتمال ذلك، نحو الطيران وإخراق الأشياء والسباحة بالجوهر وغير ذلك بقوى فيها . ويعلم كل أن ليس لأحد من الخلق تدبير في فعله ؛ فيعلم بالضرورة بما خرج عن مقصوده وقصر عن الحد الذي يحده، وكان مقدرًا بما لا يحتمل وسعه التقدير به، فيعلم به ضرورةً أن الذي به قام هو الذي قدره وأخرجه على ما أراد ولا قوة إلا بالله .
He does, and He has obligated the believers to praise Him for faith; it is established that that was by His action. And there is no power except in God. The principle in this is that the indication of creating the action of everyone in His sight is greater than the indication of creating the heavens and the earth in what is intended to be known / the reality of that by reason; because no one has tested the powers of the substances of the world so as to know the emergence of everything from that [and] the possibility of creating the like of it; rather, that is known only by its being beyond the possibility of its like. And it is known that there exist things in other substances whose substance is prevented from accepting that, such as flying, piercing objects, swimming in the substance, and other things by powers in them. And everyone knows that there is no arrangement for any of creation in his action; so he knows by necessity, by what has gone beyond his intention and fallen short of the limit that he limits, and was determined by what his capacity does not bear to determine, that he knows by necessity that the one by whom it is established is the one who determined it and brought it out as He willed, and there is no power except in God.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that God is the sole creator of human actions. It begins by stating that God's creation of every individual's action is a clearer proof of His power than the creation of the heavens and earth, because human reason can directly observe the limits of human capacity. No one has tested the powers of all substances to know what they can produce; rather, we know an action is from God because it exceeds human capability. Examples are given: flying, piercing objects, swimming—things that certain substances cannot do despite having powers. Similarly, humans have a predetermined limit in their actions; when something exceeds that limit or falls short, we know by necessity that it is determined by another (God). The conclusion is that the one who performed it is the one who decreed it, and God is the ultimate cause.

Key Terms
خلق الأفعال (creation of actions)
قدرة (power)
اضطرار (necessity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Humans are independent creators of their actions.
Correction: This passage shows that human incapacity points to God as the real creator; our limits prove we are not self-sufficient agents.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi consider the proof from human action stronger than that from the heavens and earth?
A) Because humans directly experience their own limitations in action.
B) Because the heavens and earth are too far away to study.
C) Because human actions are more complex than the cosmos.
D) Because the Quran emphasizes actions over creation.

Key Takeaway
Human incapacity in action reveals God as the true agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you experienced your actions falling short of your intention? What did that reveal about your need for God's power?
?
Do you truly believe that every action you perform is created by God? How would your day change if you lived with that consciousness?
?
Does the fact that you cannot fully control your actions humble you or frighten you? Why?

The Conclusive Argument on Power and Action
Maturidi's concluding argument on the necessity of power for action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that if power does not enable action, its existence is meaningless and leads to the nullification of tawhid.

Core Idea
If power is unnecessary for action, then God's power is contradicted.

Maturidi concludes his discussion on power and action with a decisive logical argument. He shows that if power does not actually produce its effect, then the very concept of power becomes vacuous. This would undermine the proof that God is capable and threaten the foundation of monotheism. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the argument's rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the repetition of 'لا قوة إلا بالله' for emphasis.
2
Identify the hypothetical scenario
The passage begins with a conditional: if power exists but does not cause action, yet action occurs when power is absent. Understand this premise.
3
Trace the logical consequence
Follow how Maturidi derives that action becomes evidence of the agent's incapability, which contradicts the proof that God is capable.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Understand the conclusion
The final part states that if power is useless, its presence and absence are equal, leading to either action without power or attributing power with it—both undermine tawhid.
Maturidi emphasizes 'لا قوة إلا بالله' as a counterpoint.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصل في ذلك أن القدرة لو لم يكن لها فعل وهي موجودة يكون بها فعل وهي غير موجودة، فتكون سببًا لفعل إذا عدم القدرة في التحقيق، فيصير القول به قولاً بوجود الفعل بعدم القدرة، فيكون الفعل دليلاً على أن ليس الفاعل بقادر؛ وبه استدلوا على أن الله قادر، فبطل موضع الاستدلال بالشاهد؛ إذ الحق فيه أن يعلم أنه كان غير قادر وقت الفعل، فيصير الفعل دليل نفي القدرة، وفي ذلك إبطال التوحيد ولا قوة إلا بالله. على أن وجود القدرة إذ كانت لا تنفع وهي موجودة فوجودها وقت الوجود وعدمها سواء، وفي ذلك لزوم القول بالفعل لا بقدرة عليه ألبتة، أو بجعل القدرة معه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And the principle in that is that if power had no action while existing, but action occurs by it while it is non-existent, then it becomes a cause for action when power is absent in reality. Thus, the statement regarding it becomes a statement that the action exists without power, so the action becomes evidence that the agent is not capable. And by that they argued that Allah is capable, so the place of inference by the witnessed becomes invalid; for the truth in it is that it would be known that He was not capable at the time of action, so the action becomes evidence of the negation of power, and in that is the nullification of monotheism. And there is no power except through Allah. Moreover, if the existence of power, when it does not benefit while existing, then its existence at the time of existence and its non-existence are equal, and in that is the necessity of saying the action [occurs] with no power over it at all, or of attributing power with it. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This final argument draws a principle: if power exists but does not enable an action, and yet the action occurs when power is absent, then power is unnecessary. This would lead to the conclusion that the agent is not capable, which contradicts the proof that God is capable. If power is useless, then its presence or absence is irrelevant, which would mean actions occur without power, a position that undermines tawhid (monotheism). The argument reinforces that power must be effective in enabling action.

Key Terms
قدرة
فعل
دليل
توحيد
فاعل
قادر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's power can be inferred even if power is not necessary for action.
Correction: Maturidi shows that if power is not necessary, then action would indicate the agent's incapability, contradicting the inference of God's power.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if power exists but does not enable action?
A) Action occurs by power
B) Action occurs without power
C) Power becomes more effective
D) Nothing changes

Key Takeaway
Power must be effective for action; otherwise, the concept of power and the proof of God's capability collapse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's power were not necessary for any of your daily actions, would you still feel dependent on Him? In what area of your life have you been acting as if your own ability alone suffices, forgetting that all power comes from Allah? Pause right now and ask Him to make you truly reliant on His power.
?
This passage shows that attributing ineffective power to God would actually negate His perfection. How often do you make du'a (supplication) without truly expecting that Allah's power will change your situation? Today, identify one specific request where you doubt His ability to respond, and plead with Him to increase your certainty that His power is always effective.
?
Do you live as if your own power is independent or as if it is merely a gift that must be used for action? Reflect: when you succeed, do you thank Allah for giving you the power, or do you credit yourself? Commit to acknowledging His power in every small action today, from the moment you wake up.

Refutation of Mu'tazila on Power and Act
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazila doctrine that power does not last two moments and precedes the act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the Mu'tazila view on power (qudra) leads to compulsion and contradicts moral responsibility.

Core Idea
Without power at the moment of action, the act is compelled, not freely chosen.

Imam Maturidi critiques the Mu'tazila claim that human power does not persist over time and is absent at the time of the act. While their view may sound plausible, Maturidi shows that it logically implies that acts occur by compulsion and nature, which undermines the basis for moral accountability and moral categories like friendship with God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of Maturidi's argument. Notice how he begins by acknowledging the superficial appeal of the Mu'tazila view.
Pay attention to terms like 'qudra', 'idtirar', 'tab'.'
2
Identify the Mu'tazila claim
Extract the specific Mu'tazila position being refuted: power does not last two moments and is not present at the time of the act.
3
Understand the consequence of power absence
Maturidi argues that if power is absent when the act occurs, the act is by compulsion (idtirar) and by nature (tab'), not by free choice.
This is the core of the refutation.
4
Follow the analogical argument
Maturidi uses an analogy: loss of all causes that usually bring about an act makes the act impossible and the person compelled; losing the specific power for the act is even more deserving of that description.
This strengthens the claim of compulsion.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by stating that basing friendship and enmity on such a view is intellectually monstrous. Consider why this is a strong critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نذكر طرفا مما يبين قبح قول المعتزلة عند التحصيل، وإن كان قولهم في الإطلاق قولا لذيذا في السمع يشبه أن يكون حقا. والله الموفق. وهو أن من قولهم: إن القدرة لا تبقى وقتين وإنها ليست وقت الفعل. فوقع الفعل في الحقيقة ولا قوة له وقت وجوده، وذلك علم الاضطرار ووقوع الفعل بالطبع. ثم الدلالة أن حق مثله الاضطرار وأن فقد جميع الأسباب التي بها الفعل لوقته يحيل الفعل ويوصف صاحبه بالاضطرار فوت القدرة التي بها الفعل أحق بذلك؛ فصيروه مضطرا إلى ما يصير به وليا لله تعالى، وعدوا له عند الاختيار. ومما يوضح ذلك أيضا أن من قولهم: إن من أراد التحرك للوقت الثاني منه أنها تقع لا محالة، ولا يقدر صرفها إلا بمنع من قبل غيره. وذلك آية الضرورة. ثم وجبت الولاية والعداوة بمثله، وذلك وحش في العقل.
Then we mention a portion of what clarifies the ugliness of the saying of the Mu'tazila upon investigation, although their saying in absolute terms is a pleasant saying to the ear that seems to be true. And Allah is the giver of success. And it is that among their sayings is that power (qudra) does not last two moments and that it is not at the time of the act. So the act occurs in reality while there is no power at the time of its existence, and that is the sign of compulsion (idtirar) and the occurrence of the act by nature (tab'). Then the indication is that the like of it is compulsion, and that the loss of all the causes by which the act occurs at its time prevents the act and its possessor is described as compelled, and the loss of the power by which the act occurs is more deserving of that; so they made him compelled to that by which he becomes a friend (wali) of Allah Almighty, and they counted him as having choice. And what clarifies that also is that among their sayings is that whoever intends to move for the second moment, it occurs inevitably, and he cannot avert it except by prevention from another. And that is the sign of necessity. Then friendship (wilaya) and enmity become obligatory by the like of that, and that is monstrous in the intellect.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazila doctrine that power (qudra) does not persist over two moments and is not present at the moment of the act. The author acknowledges that this view sounds plausible superficially, but upon investigation it leads to absurd consequences. The critique is that if power is absent at the time of the act, then the act occurs by compulsion (idtirar) and by nature (tab'), not by choice. The analogy is given: if all causes for an act are absent, the act is impossible and the person is considered compelled. The Mu'tazila position would therefore imply that a person becomes a friend of Allah (wali) by compulsion, yet they still attribute choice to him. Additionally, the Mu'tazila claim that if one intends to move at a future moment, the movement occurs necessarily unless prevented by an external barrier. This is a sign of necessity, not freedom. The author concludes that basing moral friendship and enmity on such a foundation is intellectually monstrous (wahsh fi al-'aql).

Key Terms
القدرة (al-qudra)
الاضطرار (al-idtirar)
بالطبع (bi al-tab')
الولاية (al-wilaya)
العداوة (al-'adawa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila view that power does not last and is absent at the act seems obviously true because it sounds plausible.
Correction: Maturidi shows that this view leads to the conclusion that acts occur by compulsion and nature, contradicting the Mu'tazila's own commitment to human choice and moral responsibility.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi argue follows from the Mu'tazila claim that power is not present at the time of the act?
A) The act is voluntary and praiseworthy.
B) The act occurs by compulsion and by nature.
C) The act is impossible.
D) The act is caused by human free will.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazila by showing that their view on power necessarily implies compulsion, making moral responsibility impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an action, do you ever feel that you are compelled by your nature or circumstances? How does this lesson's emphasis on power at the moment of act challenge that feeling? Will you consciously affirm your choice in the next action you take?
?
If you believe that God creates your actions, how do you reconcile that with the idea that you have power at the moment of act? This lesson shows that without concurrent power, you are merely compelled. Does your daily experience of decision-making feel like compulsion or free choice? Commit to noticing one decision today where you exercise real choice.
?
The Mu'tazila view sounds pleasant but leads to moral absurdity. What pleasant beliefs do you hold that might actually undermine your sense of responsibility? Are you willing to examine them? This week, identify one belief about your own actions that might be a comfortable illusion.

Refuting al-Aslah: God's Sovereignty Over Benefit
Maturidi refutes al-aslah by a conditional argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazili principle of al-aslah (most beneficial) is false because it would limit God's absolute sovereignty.

Core Idea
God's ownership is absolute; He is not bound by human notions of benefit.

Maturidi addresses a key Mu'tazili doctrine: that God must always do what is most beneficial for His creatures. He argues that this principle actually implies a constraint on God's power. Read carefully to follow his concise logical refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation. Notice the compact argument style.
Maturidi uses a conditional (if... then) structure.
2
Identify the main claim being refuted
Locate the phrase 'القول بالأصلح' (the statement about al-aslah). This is the target of refutation.
3
Trace the conditional argument
Maturidi argues: if God's ownership were not as it is (i.e., limited by al-aslah), then He would not be able to lead astray or prevent obedience. But these actions are attributed to God in the Quran. Therefore, the principle is false.
The argument shows that al-aslah would make God's power conditional on human benefit.
4
Understand the conclusion
The final sentence states that the doctrine of al-aslah is false and null. Reflect on why this refutation is foundational for Maturidi's theology.
This passage establishes that God's actions are not subject to external criteria of benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : ووجه الاعتبار به ليس على ما قدر ، ولكن بما يقول بالأصلح. ومعلوم أن الله لو لم يكن ملكه على ما مَلَكه لم يكن ليقدر أن يضلَّ مَن أضَلَّه ويمنع من يمنعه عن طاعة رسوله، وكان ذلك أقل للغواية وأقرب إلى الطاعة. فثبت أن القول بالأصلح باطل مضمحل .
Shaykh Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: The point of consideration regarding it is not as was estimated, but rather according to what is said about the principle of the most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ). It is known that if God's ownership were not over what He owns, He would not be able to lead astray whom He leads astray and prevent whom He prevents from obedience to His messenger, and that would be less conducive to error and closer to obedience. So it is established that the statement about al-aṣlaḥ is false and null.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage is a direct refutation of the Mu'tazilī principle of al-aṣlaḥ (the most beneficial). Maturidi begins by stating that the correct way to consider God's actions is not by the limited human estimation of what is beneficial, but by what God actually does. He argues that if God's ownership (mulk) were constrained by what creatures consider beneficial, then God would be unable to lead some astray and prevent them from obeying His messenger — yet these actions are clearly attributed to God in the Quran. Moreover, if God were limited in this way, it would theoretically reduce error and increase obedience, which contradicts the actual state of affairs. Therefore, the doctrine of al-aṣlaḥ is false and null.

Key Terms
الشيخ أبو منصور
الأصلح
ملك
إضلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God must always do what is most beneficial for His creatures.
Correction: Maturidi shows that if God were bound by al-aslah, He would be unable to perform actions (like leading astray) that He actually does, which would contradict His sovereignty and the Quran.

Checkpoint
What principle does Maturidi refute in this passage?
A) The principle of al-badāʾ.
B) The principle of al-aṣlaḥ.
C) The principle of al-ʿadl.
D) The principle of al-tawḥīd.

Key Takeaway
God's absolute sovereignty means He is not bound by human notions of benefit; the Mu'tazili principle of al-aslah is therefore false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly submit to God's absolute sovereignty when He allows trials, hardship, or even misguidance in your life, or do you secretly believe He 'owes' you a certain level of benefit and ease? What would it take for you to surrender that expectation today?
?
If God's actions are not bound by what you consider 'best' for you, how does that challenge your reliance on your own judgment? Are you willing to trust His decree even when it contradicts your sense of what is beneficial?
?
The Mu'tazili insisted that God must always do the most advantageous thing for His creatures—a belief that sounds noble but is here refuted. Can you identify any area in your life where you impose your notion of 'benefit' on God, demanding that He act according to your standards of good? Will you repent of that and accept His unrestricted wisdom?

The Third Refutation: Dualism
Maturidi states the third refutation of dualism based on mutual ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that belief in two gods is falsified by the impossibility of one god being ignorant of what the other knows.

Core Idea
Dualism fails because one god would be ignorant of the other's power.

Maturidi presents his third and final refutation of dualism. He argues that the only way to recognize the corruption of the two-god theory is that each god would have power over what the other does not know — implying ignorance in one divine being. If this were not a contradiction, the monotheist's argument against dualists would collapse. This lesson explains that crucial argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
Notice the compact style.
2
Understand the logical structure
Identify the main claim: the way to know dualism is false is the power of one over what the other does not know. Then note the conditional: if that were permissible, monotheists' refutation would fail.
The conditional reinforces the argument's strength.
3
Consider the implication
Ask yourself: what does 'power over what the other does not know' imply about the other god? It implies ignorance, which corrupts divinity.
This is the key point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث أن طريق معرفة فساد القول باثنين ليس إلا قدرة أحدهما على ما لا يعلمه الآخر، وفي ذلك إيجاب ذلك . ولو جاز ذا من غير أن يكون في ذلك فساد الألوهية لبطل قول الموحدين فيهما به أبطلوا قول الثنوية. وقوله : يعلم أنه آمن كيف يكون، فهذا معنى لا منفعة فيه، لأنه مع علمه بذلك يعلم أنه لا يؤمن أو لا؟ فإن قال: لا، سفّهه، وإن قال: نعم، قيل: في ذلك وقعت المطالبة، وقد ذكرت أنه لو آمن لم يخرج من علمه، فكيف لم يخرج، وعلمه أنه لا يكون، وقد كان؟ ولا قوة إلا بالله . وأما قوله : «لولم يقدر عليه لم يكن ملوما ، فهو مثل القول سواء ، ودليله أنه قوله . بل عليه أعظم اللائمة، لما هو ضيّع القدرة حيث أعرض عن الذي به يأتيه. وقوله «لا يلزمنا لأن القدرة غير الإيمان يُدفَع أيضاً، فما فيه ما يمنع هذا اللزوم؛ بل إنما لزم ذلك لأن القدرة غير الإيمان. ثم اعتباره بالأمر فاسد لأنه استعباد به يظهر ذله وعبوديته، والقوة هي الغنى والعلو والرفعة ؛ فهو الوجه الذي به يبطل ربوبية غير الله، وليس في الأمر ذلك. على أنه لو لم يكن أمر ولا نهى كان القول بـيؤمن ويكفر ويقدر ولا يقدر» لا معنى له. ولا قوة إلا بالله . وبعد فإن القدرة ثمرتها الفعل ، وبها يكون الذي ذكر، لا بالأمر؛ لذلك
The third: that the way of knowing the corruption of the statement of two [gods] is only the power of one over what the other does not know, and in that lies the affirmation of that. And if this were permissible without there being in that a corruption of divinity, then the statement of the monotheists regarding them would be invalidated by which they refute the dualists. And His statement: 'He knows that if he believed, how would it be?' — this is a meaning with no benefit, because with His knowledge of that, does He know that he will not believe or not? If he says: no, he calls him foolish; and if he says: yes, it is said: there lies the demand, and I have mentioned that if he believed he would not leave His knowledge, so how did he not leave, and His knowledge that it will not occur, and it had been? And there is no power except through God. As for his statement: 'If he did not have power over it, he would not be blameworthy' — it is the same as the saying, and its proof is his saying. Rather, upon him is the greatest blame, because he wasted the power when he turned away from that by which it comes to him. And his statement: 'It does not necessitate us because power is other than faith' is also rebutted, for there is nothing in that that prevents this necessity; rather, it is necessary precisely because power is other than faith. Then his comparison with the command is corrupt because it is an enslavement by which his lowliness and servitude appear, while power is wealth, highness, and exaltation; so that is the aspect by which the lordship of other than God is nullified, and there is not in the command that. Moreover, if there were no command and no prohibition, the statement 'he believes and disbelieves, and he has power and does not have power' would have no meaning. And there is no power except through God. Then, power's fruit is the action, and by it that which was mentioned occurs, not by the command; therefore.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This lengthy passage comprises the third point and several subsequent objections and answers. The third point refutes dualism (belief in two gods) by arguing that the only way to know it is false is that one god would have power over what the other does not know — which implies ignorance in the other and corrupts divinity. If that were not a problem, monotheists' argument against dualists would fail. Then comes an objection: the claim that 'God knows how it would be if he believed' is meaningless because it does not address whether God knows he will not believe. The answer is that if God knows the hypothetical, He also knows the actual outcome; the objection collapses. Next, the claim that 'if he did not have power, he would not be blameworthy' is refuted: the person is blameworthy because he wasted the power he had. Further, the argument that 'power is other than faith, so it does not necessitate blame' is rebutted: the necessity of blame is precisely because power is distinct from faith — the person has power but does not act on it. The comparison with a command (al-amr) is invalid because a command implies servitude, while power implies independence and lordship. Finally, Maturidi concludes that power's fruit is action; actions occur by power, not by command alone.

Key Terms
اثنين
إلوهية
توحيد
ثنوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that dualism is refuted by mutual conflict or inability to create.
Correction: Maturidi focuses on the impossibility of one god being ignorant of the other's power, not on physical conflict.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the key to refuting dualism?
A) Their mutual inability to create.
B) The power of one over what the other does not know.
C) Their lack of power.
D) Their disagreement.

Key Takeaway
Dualism is refuted because it would require one god to be ignorant of what the other controls, which is incompatible with divinity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever live as if there are two powers—one controlling good and another controlling evil? How does this lesson's argument that ignorance corrupts divinity challenge you to trust only Allah in every situation?
?
If you truly believe that Allah alone has full knowledge and power over all things, what area of your life are you still reluctant to submit to His control? What would it take for you to surrender that today?
?
The dualists' error was attributing power to another alongside Allah. In what subtle ways do you attribute power to yourself, to others, or to circumstances, ignoring that only Allah knows and controls all? Commit to one specific change this week.

Objections on Blame and Power Answered
Maturidi answers objections about hypothetical knowledge, blame for lack of power, and the role of power vs faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's responses to objections about God's knowledge of hypotheticals, blameworthiness, and the relationship between power and faith.

Core Idea
Blame arises from wasting the power one has, not from lacking it.

Maturidi addresses several objections regarding free will, blame, and God's knowledge. He first refutes the claim that if God knows how a person would act hypothetically, it implies a different outcome. He then argues that a person is blameworthy for wasting the power they possess, not for lacking power. Finally, he explains that power is distinct from faith, and that actions occur through power, not command. This lesson navigates these nuanced arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the first objection on hypothetical knowledge
Read the passage and note the objector's claim: 'He knows that if he believed, how would it be?'
2
Understand Maturidi's response
Maturidi argues that the objection is meaningless because God knows both the hypothetical and the actual outcome.
The objector is forced into a dilemma: either God knows the actual or not.
3
Address the blame objection
The objector says lack of power removes blame. Maturidi counters that the person is blameworthy for wasting power they had.
Blame is not about lack of power, but misuse of existing power.
4
Examine the power vs faith objection
The objector says power is other than faith, so blame is not necessary. Maturidi reverses it: blame is necessary precisely because power is other than faith.
5
Understand the command comparison
Maturidi rejects the analogy with command because command implies servitude, while power implies lordship.
Actions occur by power, not by command alone.

The Third Proof: Mutual Ignorance Refutes Dualism
Maturidi presents the third proof against dualism based on mutual ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's third proof against dualism, which shows that two gods cannot both be divine because one would lack knowledge of what the other knows.

Core Idea
If one god is ignorant, divinity is corrupted.

Maturidi now presents a third argument against the belief in two gods. This argument focuses on the relationship between power and knowledge: if one god had power over something the other didn't know, that ignorance would be a flaw, and flaws cannot belong to divinity. The proof is subtle but devastating—it shows that the very structure of the dualist claim is self-defeating.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Notice the logical structure: a premise, a conditional, and a conclusion.
The passage uses technical terms like 'قدرة' (power) and 'فساد' (corruption).
2
Understand the argument
Identify the key point: the way to show dualism is false is through the concept that one god would have power over something the other does not know. This implies a limitation in knowledge.
3
Reflect on the conditional
Consider what happens if we allowed that limitation: the monotheists' own argument would be invalidated. Why does Maturidi say this? Because if imperfection doesn't corrupt divinity, then the dualists could accept it.
This shows the argument is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Check your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you grasp the central claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث أن طريق معرفة فساد القول باثنين ليس إلا قدرة أحدهما على ما لا يعلمه الآخر، وفي ذلك إيجاب ذلك. ولو جاز ذا من غير أن يكون في ذلك فساد الألوهية لبطل قول الموحدين فيهما به أبطلوا قول الثنوية.
The third (point) is that the way to know the corruption of the statement of two (gods) is only the power of one of them over that which the other does not know, and in that is an affirmation of that. And if this were permissible without there being in that a corruption of divinity, then the statement of the monotheists would be invalidated by the very thing by which they invalidate the statement of the Dualists.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof against the existence of two gods. It argues that the way to demonstrate the falsity of dualism is through the concept that one god would have power over something the other does not know. This implies a limitation, which corrupts the idea of divinity. If such a limitation were allowed without corrupting divinity, then the monotheists' own argument against dualism would be invalid. So the argument is that the impossibility of one god not knowing something that the other knows shows that two gods cannot both be divine.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Maturidi shows that any lack of knowledge in a supposed god corrupts divinity itself; a god cannot be ignorant of anything.

D) The concept of divine simplicity.

Key Takeaway
The third proof against dualism argues that one god's ignorance of something the other knows would corrupt divinity, making two gods impossible.

Weighing of Deeds After Faith Shows Forgiveness
Maturidi argues from the verse of the atom's weight that good and evil are seen after faith, disproving Mu'tazila and Khawarij.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses Quranic verses to prove that the reward for deeds is seen only after faith, thereby refuting the Mu'tazila and Khawarij.

Core Idea
Good deeds only count after faith; shirk nullifies them.

Maturidi presents a powerful argument from the Quran that the retribution for good and evil deeds occurs only after a person has faith. He cites several verses to show that the 'seeing' of the atom's weight of good cannot happen while one is in shirk, and that upon repentance, past sins are forgiven. This directly challenges the Mu'tazila and Khawarij.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Get a sense of the flow of Maturidi's argument before diving into analysis.
Notice how Maturidi starts with the verse from az-Zalzalah.
2
Understand the core argument
The key claim is that one does not see the reward for good deeds while in a state of shirk. Therefore, such seeing must occur after faith, meaning that faith is a prerequisite for reward.
3
Identify the supporting verses
Maturidi cites three additional verses: an-Nisa (4:110), al-Anfal (8:38), and al-Furqan (25:70). Each supports the idea that sins are forgiven upon repentance and faith.
These verses collectively show that past sins are wiped out after turning to faith.
4
Contrast with opposing views
Maturidi explicitly states that this conclusion is impossible according to the Mu'tazila (who hold that major sinners are punished eternally) and the Khawarij (who excommunicate sinners). Reflect on why.
5
Reflect on the implication
This lesson establishes that faith is the turning point for accountability. Prior disbelief does not carry the same weight because it nullifies good deeds, but repentance opens the door to forgiveness.
This is a key difference between Maturidi and the Mu'tazila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن الله تعالى قال: فمن يعمل مثقال ذرة خيرا يره الآية ، ثم معلوم أنه لا يرى الخير وجزاءه مع الشرك، ولا جزاء شر في حال الكفر فإنا يرى ذلك بعد الإيمان، لقوله تعالى: ومن يعمل سوءا أو يظلم نفسه الآية ، وقوله : وإن ينتهوا يغفر لهم ما قد سلف، وقال: فأولئك يبدل الله سيئاتهم حسنات. دل ما ذكرت من تحقيق حال على أن فيها جزاء الأمرين، وذلك لا يكون على قول المعتزلة في وقت الكبائر ولا في وقت الصغائر، وكذا في قول الخوارج. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And also Allah the Exalted said: 'So whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it' the verse, then it is known that he does not see the good and its reward with shirk, nor the reward of evil in the state of disbelief, so he sees that only after faith, because of His saying: 'And whoever does evil or wrongs himself' the verse, and His saying: 'And if they desist, what is past will be forgiven them' (8:38), and He said: 'Those are the ones for whom Allah will replace their evil deeds with good.' What I have mentioned from the verification of the situation indicates that in it [the afterlife] there is requital for both [good and evil], and that does not occur according to the Mu'tazila during major sins nor during minor sins, and likewise according to the Khawarij. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from the Quran that the retribution for good and evil deeds occurs after faith, not in a state of shirk or disbelief. Maturidi cites the well-known verse from Surah az-Zalzalah (99:7-8) that one will see the reward of an atom's weight of good and evil. He argues that this seeing cannot happen while one is in a state of polytheism (shirk) because polytheism nullifies good deeds. Therefore, the seeing of reward and punishment must occur after faith – that is, when a person has repented and is a believer. He supports this with other verses: Surah an-Nisa (4:110) about seeking forgiveness, Surah al-Anfal (8:38) that if they desist from disbelief, past sins are forgiven, and Surah al-Furqan (25:70) about replacing evil deeds with good. All these indicate that punishment and reward for sins are only applied after faith, implying that sins are forgiven upon repentance. This directly contradicts the Mu'tazila, who claim that major sinners are punished without forgiveness, and the Khawarij, who declare them disbelievers and thus outside the scope of this promise.

Key Terms
مثقال ذرة
جزاء
شرك
كفر
سيئات
حسنات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the verse about the atom's weight applies to everyone regardless of faith, and that it means one sees the reward of every good deed instantly.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that one does not see the reward with shirk, so the seeing must be after faith in the afterlife, and that faith forgives prior sins.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say that the reward for an atom's weight of good is seen only after faith?
A) Because good deeds are not seen at all in the afterlife
B) Because shirk nullifies good deeds, so they are only seen after faith
C) Because only believers perform good deeds
D) Because the verse is abrogated

Key Takeaway
Maturidi proves that the reward for deeds is seen only after faith, meaning that sins are forgiven upon repentance, contrary to the Mu'tazila and Khawarij.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to examine your own deeds honestly, is your good done for the sake of Allah alone, or do you detect any hidden shirk—like seeking praise or recognition—that could nullify your reward? What step can you take today to purify your intention?
?
Think of a sin you committed in the past that you still feel guilty about. Does that guilt stem from a lack of trust in Allah's promise of forgiveness for those who repent and believe? How would your life change if you truly internalized that your past evil deeds can be replaced with good deeds through faith?
?
If you were to die right now, would you be relying on your good deeds to save you, or on your iman in Allah's mercy? Based on this lesson, which one actually matters? What needs to change in your daily priorities to reflect that truth?

Maturidi on Judging by Allah's Revelation
Maturidi critiques the Khariji interpretation of Quran 5:44.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that Quran 5:44 refers to suspension of hudud penalties, not to disbelief, and how this refutes the Khariji position.

Core Idea
Not judging by Allah's law is kufr only if one declares it lawful.

Imam Maturidi addresses a key sectarian dispute over the interpretation of Quran 5:44. The Kharijites used this verse to declare Muslim rulers who fail to implement Islamic law as disbelievers. Maturidi argues that the verse refers specifically to the suspension of hudud penalties, and that kufr only occurs when one considers the act lawful. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of Maturidi's argument.
2
Identify the two verses discussed
Notice that Maturidi references two verses: one about disobeying Allah in suspending hudud, and Surah al-Ma'idah 5:44 about judging by Allah's revelation. Identify how each is used.
The first verse is not quoted directly but described.
3
Understand the opponent's claim
The opponent (likely Kharijites) interprets both verses as implying kufr for any failure to judge by divine law. Maturidi points out an inconsistency: the opponent interprets a similar verse about eternal fire as not implying kufr.
4
Grasp the key distinction
Maturidi contrasts 'suspension of hudud' (تعطيل الحدود) with 'declaring it lawful' (استحلال). The verse about not judging by Allah's revelation, according to Maturidi, refers to suspension, not necessarily kufr, unless accompanied by istihlal.
This distinction is central to Maturidi's theology of faith and action.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters: it protects the faith of Muslims who fail to implement Islamic law due to weakness or oppression, while still condemning those who intentionally legitimize un-Islamic rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم احتج بقوله : ومن يعص الله ورسوله في تعطيل الحدود؛ وقد ذكر في مثله الخلود في النار من غير ذكر كبيرة ولا صغيرة. فإن كان ذا على التأويل فمثله الأول. ما قال الله في مثله : ومن لم يحكم بما أنزل الله فأولئك هم الكافرون ﴾ [المائدة، ٤٤/٥]، فذلك أيضا في تعطيل الحدود، وهو يأبى القول به، ويصرف الآية إلى الاستحلال، فمثله الذي ذكر.
Then he argued by His saying: "And whoever disobeys Allah and His Messenger in suspending the hudud" and he mentioned in its like eternal abidance in the Fire without mentioning major or minor sin. If that is to be interpreted, then the first is similar. What Allah said in its like: "And whoever does not judge by what Allah revealed – those are the disbelievers" [5:44] – that also is concerning the suspension of hudud, but he refuses to accept that and interprets the verse as referring to declaring it lawful. So the matter is the same as what he mentioned.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage addresses the Khariji interpretation of Surah Al-Ma'idah 5:44 that those who do not judge by Allah's revelation are disbelievers. Maturidi argues that the verse is specifically about making halal what Allah has made haram (استحلال), not about mere failure to implement. The opponent uses the verse to claim that any sin of judging otherwise leads to kufr, but Maturidi points out that the same opponent interprets a similar verse about eternal punishment in the Fire as not implying kufr. Thus, there is inconsistency. The suspension of hudud (penalties) is a grave sin but does not remove one from faith.

Key Terms
تعطيل الحدود
الاستحلال
الكفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any failure to judge by Islamic law is kufr.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that kufr requires istihlal (declaring it lawful), not mere suspension of hudud.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, the verse "Whoever does not judge by what Allah revealed" refers to what action?
A) Suspending hudud penalties
B) Declaring the judgment lawful
C) Committing any sin
D) Rejecting prophethood

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that Quran 5:44 is about suspension of hudud, not kufr, and that the Khariji interpretation is inconsistent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been quick to label someone's shortcoming as a sign of weak faith or even disbelief, without pausing to consider their intention or the full context of the Quran? How does Maturidi's exposure of the Kharijites' inconsistency challenge your own tendency to make snap judgments about others' iman? This week, before you judge, ask yourself: 'Am I applying a double standard?'
?
When you read the verse 'Whoever does not judge by what Allah revealed' (5:44), does your heart immediately think of others—rulers, scholars, or sinners—or do you first turn the mirror on yourself? In what specific area of your life are you failing to judge by Allah's revelation, and what excuse do you use? Write down one concrete step you will take this week to submit that area to Allah's law, and commit to it with a du'a.
?
The Kharijites demanded perfect implementation of Islamic law but were themselves inconsistent. Is there a gap between the standards you hold for others and the standards you live by? Reflect on one double standard in your religious practice—for example, in prayer, honesty, or family duties—and resolve to close that gap starting today.

Double Punishment Is for Kufr Alone
Maturidi explains the Quranic verse on double punishment and refutes equating kufr and kabirah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the Quranic double punishment applies uniquely to kufr and not to all major sins.

Core Idea
Double punishment is for kufr alone, not for all major sins.

Maturidi engages with a Quranic verse that mentions a double punishment for those who lead others astray. Some might think this applies to any major sin, but Maturidi clarifies that the severity and scope of punishment for disbelief is fundamentally different from that for other sins. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Get a feel for Maturidi's concise argument.
2
Identify the key argument and Quranic reference
Notice that Maturidi cites a Quranic verse about followers saying 'Our Lord, these led us astray.' He then draws a conclusion about double punishment, rejecting that it applies to major sins generally.
3
Understand the contrast between kafir and believer
Observe the comparison: the kafir is punished for all minor and major sins, while the believer who commits sins is not punished in the same way. This contrast supports Maturidi's point that double punishment is specific to kufr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقول الأتباع : ربنا هؤلاء أضلونا اليه الآية ، وجعل لكل ضعفًا، فبطل أن يكون ذلك عقوبة الكبيرة، بل هي " لو كانت في الكفر كان أحق للضعف منه في الإسلام للمثل؛ ألا ترى أنه يعاقب الكافر بجميع الآثام من صغائر وكبائر، ولا كذلك أمر من اعتقد دين الإسلام. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And the saying of the followers: 'Our Lord, these led us astray' up to the verse, and He made for each a double [punishment], so it is invalid that that is the punishment for a major sin, rather it is that if it were in unbelief (kufr), it would be more deserving of the double than in Islam for the like. Do you not see that He punishes the unbeliever (kafir) for all sins, minor and major, and not so the case of one who has embraced the religion of Islam. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage opens with a Quranic citation (from Surat al-A'raf 7:38) where the followers of misguidance say 'Our Lord, these led us astray' and Allah says for each is a double punishment. Maturidi argues that this double punishment cannot be the punishment for a major sin (kabirah) in general; rather, if it were applied to kufr (disbelief), it would be more appropriate because of the principle of equivalence. He then draws a contrast: the kafir is punished for every sin, both minor and major, whereas a believer who commits sins is not punished in the same way. This establishes that the punishment for kufr is of a different category from punishments for other sins.

كفر (kufr)

كبائر (major sins)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quranic double punishment applies to all major sins equally.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the double punishment is specifically for kufr, as the kafir is punished for all sins, whereas a believer is not.

Checkpoint

B) It is invalid as a punishment for a major sin and is specific to kufr.
C) It applies only to minor sins.
D) It applies to both believers and unbelievers equally.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic double punishment is a unique punishment for kufr, not a general punishment for major sins.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Have you ever taken His mercy for granted, thinking that your belief alone exempts you from the consequences of your actions? Are you truly grateful for the distinction, or do you use it as an excuse to sin?
?
The double punishment is specifically for kufr. Does your life reflect a true embrace of Islam, not just in name? Is there any area where you are following your own desires over Allah's command? If you were to be judged today on the basis of this lesson, would you be among those who 'embraced the religion of Islam' in practice? What will you change starting now?

The Permanence of Disbelief vs. Temporality of Other Sins
Maturidi presents the second argument contrasting kufr as a permanent doctrine with temporary major sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the eternal punishment for disbelief is based on its nature as a permanent doctrine, while temporary sins merit temporary punishment.

Core Idea
Disbelief is a permanent doctrine; thus its punishment is eternal.

Maturidi continues his discussion on the justice of punishment by presenting a second argument. He draws a distinction between kufr as a permanently held conviction and other major sins which are momentary acts driven by desire. This lesson unpacks that line of reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Maturidi contrasts kufr with other major sins (kaba'ir).
Pay attention to the key terms: كفر, مذهب, كبائر.
2
Understand 'permanent doctrine'
Reflect on why kufr is considered a madhhab that is held forever. Doctrines are not fleeting; they are lasting commitments.
3
Contrast with temporary sins
Compare with other sins that happen at specific times due to overpowering desires. They are not permanent states.
4
Connect punishment to nature of the sin
See how the duration of punishment matches the permanence or temporality of the sinful act.
This is the core of Maturidi's argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢ - ثم الوجه الآخر من طريق الاعتبار أن الكفر مذهب يعقد ، والمذاهب ["؟"] تعقد للأبد، فعلى ذلك عقوبته ؛ وسائر الكبائر تفعل للأوقات، وهو عند غلبة الشهوات، لا للأبد، فعلى ذلك عقوبتها. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
2. Then the other aspect from the way of consideration: that kufr (disbelief) is a doctrine that is held, and doctrines are held forever, so accordingly is its punishment; and all other major sins are committed at times, and they occur at the time of overpowering desires, not forever, so accordingly is their punishment. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (al-wajh al-akhar) for the distinction between kufr and other sins. Maturidi states that kufr is a 'madhhab' (doctrine or belief system) that is held permanently. Doctrines, once embraced, are intended to be lasting. Therefore, the punishment for kufr is eternal because the sin itself is an eternal stance. In contrast, other major sins (kaba'ir) are committed at specific times, driven by overwhelming desires (ghalabat al-shahawat). They are not permanent states, so their punishments are temporary and proportionate. The argument relies on the nature of the act: a permanent belief warrants eternal punishment, while a transient act warrants temporary punishment.

Key Terms
كفر
مذهب
عقوبة
كبائر
دوام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All sins are equal in deserving eternal punishment.
Correction: Maturidi distinguishes based on permanence: kufr is a permanent doctrine, while other sins are temporary acts, so punishments differ accordingly.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say the punishment for kufr is eternal?
A) Because it is a major sin like others.
B) Because kufr is a permanent doctrine embraced forever.
C) Because God's justice requires it.
D) Because it is a sin committed repeatedly.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that the eternal punishment for disbelief stems from its nature as a permanent doctrine, unlike temporary sins which only merit temporary punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever considered your own beliefs as a 'doctrine' that you hold permanently, or do you see them as fluid and subject to change? What does this lesson's definition of kufr as a permanent stance demand of you regarding certainty in faith?
?
The passage says other sins are committed 'at the time of overpowering desires.' Think of a sin you struggle with: when you commit it, do you treat it as a momentary lapse, or does it reveal a deeper, more permanent inclination in your heart? How does Maturidi's distinction force you to examine the root of your disobedience?
?
If eternal punishment is based on the permanence of a doctrine, then what does that imply about your daily commitment to tawheed? Are you holding your faith as a permanent, unshakable madhhab, or is it just a temporary label? What would need to change for you to truly hold it 'forever'?
