Maturidi's Use of Report in Demonstrating the Origination of Bodies
Maturidi's proofs for origination compared with Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Maturidi uniquely incorporates revealed report (khabar) alongside rational and sensory proofs to establish the creation of bodies.

Core Idea
Maturidi affirms creation through report, reason, and sense.

This passage compares Maturidi's approach to proving the origination of bodies with that of the Mu'tazila. While the rational proofs are similar, Maturidi opens a third avenue: testimony from divine report. This integration sets him apart and shows his commitment to both reason and revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the key terms: حدوث الأجسام, المعتزلة, النظام, الخبر, الأعيان.
The passage is a single paragraph summarizing Maturidi's position.
2
Identify the three sources of knowledge
According to Maturidi, what are the paths to knowing that entities are originated? The passage mentions report (الخبر), sense (الحس), and reason (العقل).
The Mu'tazila typically rely only on reason and sense for this proof.
3
Compare with the Mu'tazila
The passage states that Maturidi's proofs are the same as those of the Mu'tazila, except that he gives report a place. Consider why this difference is significant.
Al-Nazzam is mentioned as a representative Mu'tazili who came about a century earlier.
4
Reflect on the role of report
Maturidi cites that God has informed that He is the Creator of everything. How does this scriptural testimony complement rational and sensory evidence?
This shows Maturidi's methodology of integrating revelation with rational inquiry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ج - وفي الكتاب أدلة يستدل بها الماتريدي على حدوث الأجسام، هي بعينها الأدلة التي نجدها عند المعتزلة وخاصة إبراهيم بن سيار النظام الذي جاء قبل الماتريدي بحوالي قرن من الزمان. ولا يختلف الماتريدي عن المعتزلة إلا في أنه يفسح للخبر مكاناً في التدليل على حدوث الأجسام أو الجواهر أو الأعيان كما يفضل أن يسميها، وعنده سبلنا إلى العلم الأعيان حادثة بشهادة الخبر والحس والعقل التي بحقائق الأشياء. أما الخبر فإن الله تعالى أخبر أنه خالق كل وبديع.
C - And in the book there are proofs by which al-Maturidi argues for the origination of bodies (huduth al-ajsam); they are precisely the proofs we find among the Mu'tazila, especially Ibrahim ibn Sayyar al-Nazzam who came about a century before al-Maturidi. And al-Maturidi does not differ from the Mu'tazila except in that he makes room for report (al-khabar) in the demonstration of the origination of bodies, or substances, or entities (al-a'yan) as he prefers to call them, and in his view our paths to knowledge are that entities are originated by the testimony of report, sense, and reason concerning the realities of things. As for report, God Almighty has informed that He is the Creator of everything and the Originator.
Source pages p. 571

Commentary
This passage is a comparative analysis of Maturidi's arguments for the creation (huduth) of bodies. The author states that Maturidi uses the same proofs as the Mu'tazila, particularly al-Nazzam. The only difference is that Maturidi gives report (khabar) a role in demonstrating the origination of bodies (ajsam), substances (jawakhir), or entities (a'yan) — a term Maturidi prefers. According to Maturidi, our paths to knowledge are that entities are originated by the testimony of report, sense, and reason. The author then cites an example from report: God has informed that He is the Creator of everything and the Originator (badi'). This shows Maturidi's integration of revealed information into rational demonstration.

Key Terms

المعتزلة
النظام
الخبر
الأعيان

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that rational proofs for creation are strengthened by scriptural testimony.

Sense Argument for the Temporality of Bodies
Sense-based proof for the temporality of bodies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how sense perception demonstrates the temporality (createdness) of bodies according to Imam Maturidi.

Core Idea
Everything we perceive by our senses points to its createdness, not to its eternity.

This passage presents a foundational argument in Islamic theology: the sense-based proof for the temporality of bodies. Imam Maturidi argues that our direct observation of the world reveals that all entities are marked by need, change, and dependence—clear signs that they are not eternal but created. Let's examine his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The Arabic uses a chain of reasoning from observation to conclusion.
2
Identify the main premise
Notice that the argument begins with a sense perception: we see entities (a'yan) built upon need (haja) and necessity (darura). This is the empirical foundation.
3
Understand the link between need and temporality
The author then deduces that need implies dependence on another, and since eternity (qidam) requires self-sufficiency (ghina), what is needy cannot be eternal—hence it is temporal (hadith).
This logical step connects observation to theological conclusion.
4
Examine the additional observations
The passage lists three more sense-based observations: entities need repair, have opposing natures that need a unifier, and require composition of parts. Then it adds change and perishment. Each supports the main point.
These observations reinforce the initial premise of neediness.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The argument concludes: whatever is like that cannot exist by itself, so it must be temporal. Ask yourself why this conclusion is necessary for proving the existence of a Creator.
This connects to larger Aqeedah discussions about the world's createdness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الحس فإننا نحس الأعيان مبنية على الحاجة والضرورة ، والحاجة والضرورة يحوجانها إلى غيرها ، وكل ما هو كذلك فهو حادث ؛ لأن القدم هو شرط الغنا ؛ إذ القديم يستغني بقدمه عن غيره . نحس الأعيان محتاجة إلى من يصلح ما فسد منها ، عاجزة عن إصلاح نفسها حتى ولو كانت في حال كمالها وقوتها ، ونحسها محتاجة إلى من يقهر طبائعها المتضادة المتنافرة على الاجتماع ، ونحسها محتاجة إلى من يؤلف بين أجزائها وأبعاضها ، كذلك نحسها متغيرة فانية زائلة ، وكل ما كان كذلك لم يجز كونه بنفسه ، ذلك حدثه
As for sense, we perceive entities as built upon need and necessity, and need and necessity necessitate them to something else, and whatever is like that is temporal, because eternity is the condition of self-sufficiency; indeed the eternal is self-sufficient by its eternity from other than it. We perceive entities in need of one who repairs what has decayed from them, incapable of repairing themselves even if they were in their state of perfection and strength. And we perceive them in need of one who overcomes their opposing and conflicting natures to unite them, and we perceive them in need of one who composes their parts and limbs. Likewise, we perceive them as changing, perishable, passing away, and whatever is like that cannot exist by itself; that is its temporality.
Source pages p. 571

Commentary
This passage presents an argument from sense perception (al-hiss) for the temporality (huduth) of bodies. The commentator presents Maturidi's reasoning: we observe that all entities (a'yan) are built on need and necessity, which require a cause beyond themselves. Since eternity (qidam) implies self-sufficiency, that which is needy cannot be eternal; it must be temporal. Additional observations support this: entities decay and cannot repair themselves, they contain opposing natures that require an external unifier, and they undergo constant change and perishment. All these indicate that they cannot exist by themselves, hence they are created (hadith).

Key Terms
الحس
الأعيان
الحاجة
القدم
الحادث
الغنا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that need and change could be self-caused or inherent to the nature of entities, allowing them to be eternal despite apparent neediness.
Correction: The argument asserts that need and change, by definition, imply dependence on an external cause, which contradicts the self-sufficiency required for eternity. Therefore, entities exhibiting need cannot be eternal; they must be created.

Checkpoint
What is the main premise of the sense argument for the temporality of bodies?
A) Bodies are composed of atoms
B) Entities exhibit need and necessity
C) Bodies are self-sufficient
D) Eternity requires change

Key Takeaway
The sense argument shows that everything we observe in the physical world is marked by need and change, proving that bodies are not eternal but created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Look at your own body. It hungers, tires, ages, and heals. You did not create these needs nor can you fully satisfy them. If your very existence is a tapestry of needs, how can you pretend to be independent of the One who meets them? What will you consciously depend on Him for today?
?
You experience constant change—your thoughts, your health, your circumstances. Nothing about you stays the same. The argument says that what changes cannot exist by itself. If you are not self-sufficient, what right have you to feel self-reliant? When will you admit to yourself that you need a Sustainer every moment?
?
The passage says that even in your peak strength, you are incapable of repairing yourself. Recall a time your body failed you. Did your willpower or knowledge fix it? Who did? What does that powerlessness tell you about who deserves your utmost trust and reliance? How will you live that truth tomorrow?

Intellectual Argument for the Temporality of Bodies
Intellectual proof using the principle of accidents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classic kalam argument from accidents to prove that bodies are temporal (created), and how it establishes the need for a creator.

Core Idea
Bodies are inseparable from temporary accidents, so bodies themselves must be temporary.

Imam Maturidi presents one of the foundational rational proofs for the existence of God by examining the nature of bodies. The argument goes: bodies always have accidents (motion, rest, etc.), these accidents come and go, so they are temporal; whatever cannot exist without temporal things is itself temporal. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and shows why the entire world needs a creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Notice it begins with 'أما أدلته العقلية' and then lists several concepts.
The passage is dense; don't worry about understanding every word at first.
2
Identify the foundational principle
Look for the phrase 'الأجسام لا تخلو عن الحوادث' – 'bodies cannot be devoid of accidents.' This is the key premise.
3
List the accidents mentioned
The passage lists several pairs: motion/rest, composition/separation, good/bad, beautiful/ugly, increase/decrease. These are all examples of accidents (temporary attributes).
Notice the pattern: each pair are opposites that cannot coexist at the same time.
4
Understand the analogy with writing
The passage uses the analogy: writing requires a writer, separation requires a separator, etc. This illustrates that composition and change in the world require an external cause.
This is a reasoning from the observed (shahid) to the unseen (ghaib).
5
Trace the conclusion
Conclude: Since bodies are always accompanied by temporary accidents, bodies themselves must be temporary. The entire world, being composed of bodies, is therefore temporal and requires a creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما أدلته العقلية فانها مبنية على قاعدة مشهورة عند المتكلمين تقرر بأن الأجسام لا تخلو عن الحوادث ، وكل ما لا يخلو عن الحوادث فهو حادث . لا تخلو الأجسام عن حركة أو سكون ، واجتماع أو تفرق ، وطيب أو خبيث ، وحسن أو قبيح، وزيادة أو نقصان ، وهن حوادث بالحس والعقل ؛ لأن الضدين لا يجتمعان ، فثبت التعاقب وفيه المحدث : كذلك فإننا لا نعلم كتابة بلا كاتب ، ولا تفرقا إلا بمفرق ؛ وكذلك الاجتماع ، وكذلك السكون والحركة ، فيلزم في جملة العالم ذلك ؛ إذ هو مؤلف مفرق ، بل الأعجوبة في تأليف العالم أرفع ، فهو أحق أن لا يتشرق ولا يجتمع إلا بغيره . ثم كل ما في الشاهد والكتابة يكون أحدث كان ، فمثله جميع العالم ؛ إذ هو في معنى ما ذكرت .
As for his intellectual proofs, they are based on a well-known principle among the mutakallimun: that bodies cannot be devoid of accidents, and whatever cannot be devoid of accidents is temporal. Bodies cannot be devoid of motion or rest, composition or separation, good or bad, beautiful or ugly, increase or decrease, and these are accidents by sense and reason, because two opposites cannot coexist. Thus succession is established, and in it there is the one who brings about the succession. Likewise, we do not know of writing without a writer, nor separation without a separator; similarly, composition, rest, and motion. So that applies to the entirety of the world, for it is composed and separated. Rather, the marvel in the composition of the world is greater, so it is more deserving that it cannot come about nor come together except by another. Then everything in the witnessed world and writing had a beginning, so the entire world is like it, for it is in the same sense as what was mentioned.
Source pages p. 572

Commentary
This passage presents the classic kalam argument for the temporality of bodies based on reason. It starts with the well-known principle: bodies cannot be devoid of accidents (hawadith), and whatever cannot be devoid of accidents is itself temporal (hadith). The argument then lists accidents that always accompany bodies: motion/rest, composition/separation, good/bad, etc. These accidents are temporal because they change and opposites cannot coexist, establishing succession. The argument uses analogies: writing requires a writer, separation requires a separator, etc. It concludes that the entire world, being composed and separated, requires an external cause. The marvel of the world's composition makes it even more deserving of a creator.

Key Terms
الأدلة العقلية
الأجسام
الحوادث
التعاقب
المحدث
التأليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that some bodies could be eternal despite containing accidents, or that accidents could be eternal.
Correction: The argument asserts that whatever is inseparable from temporal things must itself be temporal. Since bodies always have accidents that change, they cannot be eternal.

Checkpoint
What is the foundational principle of the intellectual argument for the temporality of bodies?
A) Bodies are composed of atoms
B) Bodies cannot be devoid of accidents
C) Accidents are eternal
D) The world is eternal

Key Takeaway
The entire world, because it consists of bodies that are always accompanied by changing accidents, is temporal and requires a creator who brought it into existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are a body that is constantly changing – breathing, thinking, aging. If every part of you is temporal and dependent on an external cause, where is your true security? Are you relying on that which is temporary or the One who created it all?
?
The analogy of writing is used: no writing without a writer. Your entire life is a composition. Have you ever considered that the very fact that you are alive right now is proof of a Creator who sustains you at every moment? How would your daily choices change if you truly felt that dependence?
?
The argument shows that the world cannot come together except by another. But you often act as if your life is self-made. Where in your life are you denying the need for the Creator by assuming independence?

Argument from Opposites to a Unifier Creator
Ibrahim al-Nazzam argues from the coexistence of opposites to a transcendent creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the coexistence of opposites, such as heat and cold, serves as evidence for a transcendent Creator who unites and subjugates them.

Core Idea
The union of opposites demands a unifier beyond themselves.

This lesson introduces a profound theological argument from Imam Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid, attributed to Ibrahim al-Nazzam. By observing the natural opposition between fire and water, heat and cold, we infer that their coexistence cannot be self-caused; it requires an external agent. This argument points to a Creator who is utterly unlike His creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the structure of the argument sink in.
Notice the logical progression from observation to inference.
2
Identify the key observation
The passage begins with the observation that heat and cold are opposites and cannot coexist naturally. Find this statement.
3
Understand the inference
Since opposites are found together, they must have a unifier and subjugator. Understand why this inference is necessary.
The subjugator forces them together against their nature.
4
Trace the extension
The argument extends to the mixture of fire, water, earth, and air in the world. See how this reinforces the same point.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The final conclusion is that the Creator is unlike anything, as stated in Quran 42:11. Consider why this is important.
If He were like His creation, He would also be temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجدت الحر مضاداً للبرد ، ووجدت الضدين لا يجتمعان في موضع واحد من ذات أنفسهما ، فعلمت بوجودي لهما مجتمعين أن لما جامعاً جمعها وقاهرا قهرهما على خلاف شأنهما . وما جرى عليه القهر والمنع فضعيف ، وضعفه ونفوذ تدبير قاهره فيه دليل على حدثه وعلى أن محدثاً أحدثه ومخترعاً احترمه لا وهو رب لا يشبهه ؛ لأن حكم ما أشبهه حكمه في دلالته على الحدث ، الله جمع من سوى الله بين النار والماء والتراب والهواء فذلك دليل أيضاً على حدثها ، غير أن حدثها ليس هو الإنسان الذي جمعها ؛ لأن الإنسان يجري عليه من القهر ما يجري عليها ، مخترع هذه الأشياء ومخترع الإنسان المشبه لها الله الذي لا يشبه شيء لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ
I found heat opposed to cold, and I found the two opposites cannot coexist in one place by themselves, so by my finding them together I knew that they have a unifier that united them and a subjugator that subjugated them contrary to their nature. What is subjected to subjugation and prevention is weak, and its weakness and the effectiveness of the subjugator's control over it is evidence of its temporality and that a temporally originated being brought it into existence and a creator created it. He is a Lord unlike anything; because the ruling of what resembles Him is like His ruling in indicating temporality. God collected between fire, water, earth, and air—that is also evidence of their temporality, but their temporality is not due to the human who collected them; because the human is subject to the same subjugation as they are. The creator of these things and the creator of the human resembling them is God, whom nothing resembles, 'There is nothing like Him' (Quran 42:11).
Source pages p. 573

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the existence of a creator using the argument from opposites. It begins by observing the coexistence of heat and cold, which are opposites, in the same place. Since opposites cannot naturally unite, their union necessitates a unifier (jāmiʿ) and a subjugator (qāhir) that forces them together against their nature. The fact that these elements are subjected to subjugation shows they are weak and temporally originated (ḥādith). The subjugator must be powerful and not like the created things, because if He were like them, He would also be temporal. The argument extends to the mixture of fire, water, earth, and air in the world, indicating their temporality. The human who collects these elements is himself temporal and subject to subjugation, so the true creator is God, who is completely unlike anything, as stated in Quran 42:11.

Key Terms

مخترع
ليس كمثله شيء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Opposites cannot coexist at all.
Correction: The argument shows that opposites cannot coexist by their own nature, but they can be forced together by an external agent.

Checkpoint
What is the main evidence from the passage for the existence of a creator?
A) The fact that heat and cold are opposites
B) The fact that opposites are found together despite their natures
C) The weakness of fire
D) The Quranic verse

Key Takeaway
The coexistence of opposites necessitates a transcendent unifier and subjugator, who is God, unlike anything.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument shows that opposites cannot coexist without a unifier. Are there contradictions in your own life—between what you claim to believe and how you actually live—that you have not resolved? Will you continue to let them coexist without acknowledging the Unifier who demands consistency?
?
Everything temporal is weak and subject to subjugation. Where do you place your trust—in people, possessions, or plans that are themselves temporal? If you truly believe God alone is unlike anything and not subject to weakness, what must change in where you anchor your security today?
?
You, like the elements, are created and subject to a power greater than yourself. In what specific area of your life do you resist acknowledging your own weakness and dependence on God? What would it look like to surrender that area to the Subjugator today?

God's Subjugation of Opposites
Al-Nazzam explains that God forces opposites to unite against their natural repulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Nazzam's argument that the union of opposites is evidence of God's power, as they unite only under subjugation, not naturally.

Core Idea
Opposites unite only by God's subjugation, not by nature.

Al-Nazzam presents a philosophical argument for God's existence based on the behavior of opposites. He observes that things with opposing natures tend to repel each other when left alone, yet we see them co-existing in the world. This co-existence, he argues, is forced upon them by a higher power—Allah. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the key terms: قهر (subjugation), متضادات (opposites), اجتماع (union), جوهر (essence), منافرة (repulsion).
The passage has OCR issues; the core idea is still clear.
2
Understand the natural state
Recognize that according to Al-Nazzam, when opposites are left alone ('takhliya'), their essence (jawhar) drives them to repel each other. This is their natural state.
Think of fire and water: they naturally oppose each other.
3
Recognize the role of subjugation
The passage states that when opposites are subjugated (qahr), they unite. This union is not from their own nature but imposed. This points to an external agent—God.
This is the key move: the fact that opposites do unite shows a power overcoming their nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن ابراهيم رأي أن الله قهر الأشياء المتضادات على الاجتماع جوهرها إذا خليت وما عليه ، فأما إذا منعت مما عليه من المنافرة وشهرت النغلام] ، كان يزعم منها هي الذي ليس في على الاجتماع ، فإن من جوهرها وشأنها الاجتماع عند القهر لها ، كما أن من جوهرها وشأنها المنافرة عند تخليتها وما هي عليه
Verily, Ibrahim held that God subjugated the opposing things to union, even though their essence when left alone and what it is upon [is separation]. But when they are prevented from what they have of repulsion and [?], he claimed that from them is that which is not in union, for it is of their essence and nature to unite when subjugated, just as it is of their essence and nature to repel when left alone and what they are upon.
Source pages p. 573

Commentary
This passage clarifies the mechanism of the argument from opposites. Opposing things have a natural tendency to repel each other when left alone (when freed from external influence). However, when God subjugates them, they are forced to unite. This shows that the union of opposites in the world is not natural but imposed by a powerful agent, which is God. The essence (jawhar) and nature (sha'n) of things is to repel each other when free, but to unite when subjugated.

Key Terms

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the passage claims opposites never unite naturally, or that the argument is about natural phenomena without a Creator.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that opposites do unite, but only when subjugated by God. Their natural state is repulsion, so union requires an external agent.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Consider your own heart: is there a part of you that naturally repels from obedience, from unity with fellow believers, or from submission? Have you recognized that your ability to do good is not from your nature but from God's subjugation of your soul? What would happen if He left you to your own essence?
?
This passage presents a stark reality: without divine intervention, everything would break apart. Does your daily life reflect that dependence on God's constant maintenance of order? Or do you take for granted the unity you experience in your family, community, or even your own body?

Maturidi's Proof from Evil for God's Existence
Maturidi's proof from evil for God's existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the presence of evil in the world disproves the possibility of the world being self-caused, thereby establishing the necessity of a Creator.

Core Idea
Evil in the world proves the world is not self-caused.

Maturidi presents a concise philosophical proof for God's existence rooted in the observable fact of evil. The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: if the world were self-sufficient and self-causing, it would only produce perfect states, but we see evil, so it must depend on an external maker. This lesson unpacks the logical steps of that proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Note the conditional structure: 'If... then...' and the conclusion that follows from the observed existence of evil.
The proof is very compressed; pay attention to the logical flow.
2
Identify the premise and conclusion
Identify the conditional premise: if the world were self-caused, there would be no evil. Then identify the observed fact: evil exists. Work out the conclusion.
The conclusion is explicitly stated in the second sentence.
3
Understand the reasoning behind the premise
Reflect on why Maturidi claims a self-caused world would have no evil. The reasoning is that a self-sufficient entity would choose only the best states for itself.
This is a key point: self-sufficiency implies perfection.
4
Connect to the existence of God
Realize that the argument does not directly prove God's attributes, only that the world has a maker. This maker is necessarily external and powerful, aligning with the Islamic concept of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لو كان العالم قد وجد بنفسه من غير صانع لانتفى الشر؛ وجوده بنفسه لا يرتضي لنفسه إلا أحوالا أحسن الأحوال وأوقاتا خير الصفات. فثبت من وجود الشر في العالم أنه لم يكن بنفسه.
If the world had come into existence by itself without a maker, evil would be absent; its existence by itself would not approve for itself except states of the best conditions and times of the best attributes. So it is established, from the existence of evil in the world, that it did not come to be by itself.
Source pages p. 575

Commentary
This passage presents Maturidi's proof for the existence of God from the presence of evil in the world. It is an argument by contradiction. He begins by stating a conditional: if the world were self-caused (existed by itself without a maker), then evil would be nonexistent. The reason given is that a self-sufficient entity would only choose for itself the most perfect states and times. Since we observe evil in the world, the antecedent is false—the world did not come to be by itself, implying it has a Maker. The proof is succinct: evil implies dependency, dependency implies a Creator.

Key Terms
وجود بنفسه
شر
صانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The existence of evil disproves the existence of a benevolent God.
Correction: Maturidi uses evil not to question God's goodness but to show that the world is contingent and dependent on a Creator. The argument does not address the problem of evil directly but rather uses evil as evidence of contingency.

Checkpoint

A) That the world is eternal
B) That evil is an illusion
C) That the world did not come to be by itself
D) That God is the source of evil

Key Takeaway
The presence of evil demonstrates that the world is not self-sufficient, thus requiring an external Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If the world were self-caused and chose only the best, your life would have no pain. But you know pain intimately. Will you allow the evil you face to push you toward the Maker or away from Him? What would it take for you to choose the former starting today?
?
The existence of evil proves you are not self-sufficient. In what area of your life have you been living as though you are your own cause, and how will you surrender that to the Creator this week?

Proof of Oneness from the Finitude of the World
Maturidi argues that if there were multiple gods, the world would not be finite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical argument that the finitude of the world necessitates the oneness of God.

Core Idea
If the world is finite, there cannot be more than one God.

Maturidi presents a concise yet powerful proof for tawḥīd (God's oneness) grounded in the observable finitude of the world. By considering the logical implications of multiple creators, he shows that only a single Creator can account for the limited nature of creation. This argument is a classic example of kalām reasoning, using rational deduction from observed reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the flow of the argument. Pay attention to the conditional structure.
The argument is very brief but dense; read it twice.
2
Identify the conditional statement
Notice that the argument begins with 'If God were not one' — this is the hypothetical premise from which the conclusion follows. Understand that the conclusion is that the world would not be finite.
3
Follow the logical chain
The passage then explains why: if multiple gods were possible, infinite gods could be assumed, each producing at least one thing. The cumulative result would make the world infinite. This chain is the core of the proof.
This step shows how multiplicity leads to infinitude.
4
Connect to observed reality
Since we observe that the world is finite, the initial assumption of multiple gods must be false. Therefore, God is one. This is the conclusion that Maturidi implies through the conditional reasoning.
The argument does not explicitly state 'God is one' but it is the logical result.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لو لم يكن الله واحداً لم يكن العالم متناهياً، إذ لو جاز أن يكون هناك أكثر من إله واحد لأمكن حينئذ أن نفترض ما لا نهاية له من الآلهة، التي لو كان من كل منهم شيء واحد لخرجت جملة العالم عن التناهي بخروج المحدثين له.
If God were not one, the world would not be finite. For if it were possible that there be more than one god, then it would be possible to assume infinite gods, each of whom would produce one thing, and thus the entirety of the world would go out of finitude due to the emergence of its originated things.
Source pages p. 576

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the oneness of God (tawḥīd) based on the finitude of the world. It is a type of argument from impossibility of infinite regress or multiplicity. Maturidi begins with a conditional: if God were not one (i.e., if there were multiple gods), then the world would not be finite. The reasoning is that if multiple gods were possible, we could posit an infinite number of gods. Each of these gods would bring into existence at least one thing. Consequently, the totality of the world would lack finitude, because each god adds an originated being. Since the world is observed to be finite, the premise of multiple gods is false, establishing the oneness of the Creator.

Key Terms
وحدانية
تناهي العالم
آلهة
محدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument is about the physical size of the world (i.e., whether it is spatially finite or infinite).
Correction: The argument is about the logical implication of multiple creators leading to an infinite number of effects, not about spatial dimensions. The finitude referred to is the limitedness of all originated things.

Checkpoint
What is the logical consequence that Maturidi draws from the possibility of multiple gods?
A) The world would be chaotic.
B) The world would be finite.
C) The world would be infinite.
D) The gods would conflict.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that the observed finitude of the world logically requires a single Creator, because multiple creators would lead to infinite effects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
When you contemplate the simplicity and necessity of one God, how does it cut through the chaos of your divided heart? What will you do today to realign your worship?

First Proof of Tawhid: Preventing Messengers' Miracles
Maturidi presents a proof of tawhid based on the logical impossibility of a partner preventing messengers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the impossibility of a partner preventing messengers proves the oneness of Allah.

Core Idea
If there were another god, he would stop the prophets — but he didn't.

This lesson examines a concise but powerful argument from Imam Maturidi for tawhid. The proof is based on a logical deduction: if there were another god sharing dominion, he would necessarily prevent the messengers from performing miracles to protect his own claim. Since we know the messengers were not prevented, it proves there is no other god.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if...then...'
2
Identify the premises
The argument has two premises: (1) If there were a partner, he would prevent miracles. (2) Miracles were not prevented. Therefore, no partner.
3
Reflect on the logic
Consider why the partner would prevent miracles: because allowing them would nullify his divinity. This shows the partner's self-interest.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على وحدانية الله؛ إذ لو كان الله شريك في ملكه لمنع الرسل من إظهار آياتهم لأن في ذلك إبطالا لشركته وألوهينه!
For the oneness of God; for if God had a partner in His kingdom, He would have prevented the messengers from manifesting their miracles because that would nullify His partnership and divinity.
Source pages p. 577

Commentary
This passage presents a proof of God's oneness (tawhid). The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: if there were another god sharing dominion with Allah, that partner would necessarily prevent the messengers from performing miracles, because allowing miracles would establish the truth of the messengers and thus affirm the exclusive divinity of the one God, which would nullify the partner's claim. Since we observe that messengers were indeed sent with miracles and were not prevented, it proves that there is no partner. The passage is concise but contains a logical argument based on divine jealousy and protection of sovereignty.

Key Terms
وحدانية
شريك
رسل
آيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Multiple gods could coexist peacefully without interfering with each other.
Correction: If there were another god, he would necessarily act to preserve his divinity, preventing messengers from proving the one God's sole divinity.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why would a partner prevent messengers from showing miracles?
A) To avoid competition
B) Because showing miracles would nullify the partner's divinity
C) Because messengers are not allowed
D) Because the partner is powerless

Key Takeaway
The logical impossibility of a partner preventing messengers proves the oneness of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that no one else shares sovereignty with Allah? When you feel overwhelmed, do you act as if you need help from other than Allah? This proof shows that if there were another god, he would have stopped the messengers. Examine your heart: Are you giving undue reliance on created beings, as if they have independent power? Commit to affirming tawhid in your daily life by consciously turning to Allah alone in your moments of need.
?
This proof is logical, but does it shape your conviction? When you encounter doubts about God's oneness, do you recall this argument or let doubts linger? This lesson gives a decisive proof. Will you memorize it and use it to strengthen your faith every time uncertainty creeps in? Resolve to learn and repeat this proof until it becomes second nature.
?
If you truly believe that no partner can exist, then why do you sometimes fear people more than Allah? Or why do you put your hopes in them? The same logic that disproves a partner also means that only Allah has ultimate control. Reflect on one specific area where you are relying on other than Allah and resolve to turn to Him alone starting today.

Second Proof of Tawhid: Consistency of Creation
Maturidi proves tawhid from the perfect order and consistency of the universe.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the perfect order and consistency of the universe proves the oneness of God.

Core Idea
The universe's flawless consistency points to a single, wise Director.

Imam Maturidi presents a powerful argument for tawhid drawn from the observable order of the cosmos. He argues that the flawless regularity we see in nature—from the movements of celestial bodies to the alternation of night and day—cannot be the product of multiple gods, whose conflicting wills would cause chaos. Instead, this harmony points unmistakably to a single, all-wise Creator. Let's examine his reasoning closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the author's own words set the stage.
Notice the key terms like دقة صنع and اتساق.
2
Identify the core argument
Identify how Maturidi connects the consistency of the world to the oneness of God. What is the logical connection?
The argument is conditional: if many gods, then disorder; but we see order, so one God.
3
Examine the hypothetical scenario
Pay attention to the phrase 'if there were gods with God' and what would happen. What would be the consequences of multiple deities?
The passage mentions fluctuation and disparity in administration, earth, celestial bodies, and time.
4
Connect to the Quranic verse
Read the Quranic citation (67:3) and see how it supports the argument. What does 'no disparity' mean?
The verse directly affirms the absence of flaw in creation.
5
Reflect on the implications
Think about why this proof is considered rational and empirical. How does it strengthen faith in divine oneness?
This proof is accessible to anyone observing nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كذلك فان دقة صنع العالم واتساق ذلك على سنن واحد دليل على وحدانية الله؛ إذ لو كان آلهة مع الله لتقلب فيهم التدبير وتفاوت بينهم والأرض وتسيير الشمس والقمر والنجوم وتقدير الليل والنهار والساعات؛ فإذ جرى ذلك كله على الاتساق دل على أن الكل بمدير حكيم عليم لا ينازع في التدبير ولا يخالف في التقدير وذلك معنى قوله تعالى: مَا تَرَى فِي خَلْق الرَّحْمَن مِنْ تفاوت
Likewise, the precision of the making of the world and its consistency upon a single pattern is evidence for the oneness of God; for if there were gods with God, they would have caused fluctuation in administration and disparity among them, and in the earth, and the course of the sun, moon, and stars, and the determination of night, day, and hours; but since all that proceeds in consistency, it indicates that the whole is under a wise, knowing director who is not contested in administration nor opposed in determination. That is the meaning of His statement, 'You do not see in the creation of the Most Merciful any disparity.'
Source pages p. 577

Commentary
This passage presents another proof of tawhid based on the observable order of the cosmos. The argument: The universe functions with perfect consistency—the celestial bodies follow regular paths, night and day alternate in fixed patterns. If there were multiple gods, their conflicting wills would cause disorder and inconsistency. Since we observe absolute coherence, it points to a single, wise, all-knowing director (مدير حكيم عليم). The proof concludes by citing Quran 67:3, which directly affirms the absence of disparity in creation. The proof is empirical and rational.

Key Terms

دقة صنع
اتساق
مدير حكيم
تفاوت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The universe's order could result from many gods cooperating harmoniously.
Correction: Maturidi argues that multiple independent wills would inevitably cause conflict and inconsistency, as each would have its own administration. The flawless consistency we observe rules out multiple gods.

Checkpoint

A) That there are multiple gods working together
B) That the universe is random
C) That there is a single wise director
D) That the planets control themselves

Key Takeaway
The flawless consistency of the universe is direct evidence for a single, wise, all-knowing Director.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The passage argues that multiple 'gods' would lead to disparity. But in your life, you serve many masters: career, relationships, status, comfort. ?
Allah says there is no disparity in His creation, yet you experience loss, pain, and chaos. Does this argument feel distant or even false in those moments? How can Maturidi's proof help you trust that even what seems broken is part of a coherent plan from the Most Merciful?

Twofold States of Human Actions
A proof of the twofold nature of human actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's proof that human actions have two aspects: one created by God and one acquired by humans.

Core Idea
Human actions have a divine side and a human side.

Maturidi provides a concise proof for the Middle Path between fatalism and free will. He points to the two types of states in our actions: those we cannot produce (existence itself) and those we can (choice within commands). This distinction establishes that actions are both from God and from us.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage silently. Then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: a claim, then evidence with two examples.
The passage uses 'first' and 'second' to contrast the two states.
2
Identify the two states
Identify the two kinds of states (ahwal) Maturidi describes in human actions. What is the first state? What is the second?
The first involves existence from nothing; the second involves intention and command.
3
Understand the conclusion
See how the two states lead to the conclusion that from one aspect actions are not ours, from another they are ours. This is Maturidi's balanced view.
The conclusion is stated explicitly in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الدليل على لزوم القول بهذا - مع ما فيما بينا كفاية - وجود أحوال في أفعال العبد لا يبلغها أوهامهم ولا يقدرها عقولهم، وأحوال فيها ينتهي إليها قصدهم وتبلغها عقولهم، فثبت أنها من الوجه الأول ليست لهم، ومن الوجه الثاني لهم. فالأول كتصوير خروج الشيء من العدم إلى الوجود... والثاني نحو التحرك والسكون بالمأمور به والمنهى عنه، ثبت أن فعلهم من الوجه الأول ليس لهم، ومن الوجه الثاني لهم.
Then the proof for the necessity of affirming this—sufficient from what we have already explained—is the existence of states in the actions of the servant that their imaginations do not reach and their intellects do not grasp, and states in which their intention reaches and their intellects comprehend, so it is established that from the first aspect they are not theirs, and from the second aspect they are theirs. The first is like depicting the emergence of a thing from non-existence to existence... and the second is like movement and stillness in accordance with what is commanded and prohibited; it is established that their action from the first aspect is not theirs, and from the second aspect it is theirs.
Source pages p. 583

Commentary
This passage is a proof (dalil) that demonstrates the necessity of Maturidi's previous definition of justice. The proof is based on the existence of two types of states (ahwal) in human actions. First, there are states that human imagination and intellect cannot produce or control, such as the very act of bringing something from non-existence into existence—this shows that from one aspect, actions are not human. Second, there are states that are within human intention and comprehension, such as choosing to move or stay still in response to commands—from this aspect, actions belong to humans. Therefore, the action is twofold: created by God from one side, acquired by the human from the other.

Key Terms
أحوال
فعل العبد
قصد
خلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think humans create their own actions entirely, independent of God.
Correction: Maturidi shows that actions have aspects beyond human control (like existence itself), so they depend on God, while other aspects depend on human intention.

Checkpoint
What does the existence of two states in human actions prove according to Maturidi?
A) Humans are independent agents
B) Actions are from one aspect not ours and from another aspect ours
C) Actions are entirely determined by God
D) Humans have no real role in actions

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's proof of twofold states shows that human actions have a divine creative aspect and a human acquisitive aspect, resolving the tension between divine omnipotence and human responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you feel that it is entirely your own achievement, or do you perceive Allah's creative power enabling it? How does this lesson change your sense of gratitude and humility?
?
Consider a sin you have committed. Which aspect do you typically focus on—your own choice or Allah's decree? How does acknowledging both aspects guard against both despair and arrogance?
?
If every action of yours has a divine aspect beyond your control, how does that affect your trust in Allah's plan, especially during trials? Are you willing to surrender the illusion of total control over your life?

The Necessity of Prophethood from Human Conflict
Maturidi argues for a unifying authority from human conflicting natures and the world's preservation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational argument that human conflicting natures necessitate a divinely appointed authority to guide and unite society.

Core Idea
Human conflict demands a sent guide; the world's preservation proves it.

Maturidi presents a powerful argument from human nature. He observes that the world is full of conflict because people have opposing desires. If left to themselves, they would destroy each other. But the world was created for preservation, so there must be an authority to unite them. This leads to the necessity of prophethood and imamate.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise of preservation
Maturidi starts by asserting that the world was created for preservation, not annihilation. This sets the foundation for the argument.
The Arabic phrase 'إنشاء العالم للبقاء لا للفناء' is key.
2
Observe human nature's conflict
Humans have different natures and desires. If left to their natural inclinations, they would compete for benefits and status, leading to hatred and fighting.
Maturidi describes humans as having 'أهواء مختلفة وطبائع متشتتة'.
3
Recognize the need for a unifier
Because conflict leads to destruction, there must be an 'origin' (أصل) that unites them and restrains conflict. This is the core need.
The phrase 'لابد من أصل يؤلف بينهم' is central.
4
Identify the director and proof
God provides a director (مدير) who knows their conditions and appoints a guide with a clear proof (برهان) of his imamate. This guide is the refuge for humanity.
The argument culminates in the necessity of a sent prophet or imam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ثبت ذلك دل أن إنشاء العالم للبقاء لا للفناء، هو كان العالم بأصله مبنيا على طبائع مختلفة ووجوه متضادة، وبخاصة الذي مقصود من حيث العقل الذي يجمع بين المجتمع ويفرق بين الذي حقه التفريق، وهو الذي سمته الحكماء العالم الصغير، فهو على أهواء مختلفة وطبائع متشتتة، وشهوات ركبت فيهم غالبة، لو تركوا وما عليه جبلوا لتنازعوا في تجاذب المنافع وأنواع العز والشرف والملك والسلطان، فيعقب ذلك التباغض ثم التقاتل، وفى ذلك التفاني والفساد الذي لو أمر كون العالم له لبطلت الحكمة في كونه. مع ما جعل البشر وجميع الحيوان غير محتمل للبقاء إلا بالأغذية وما به قوام أبدانهم إلى المدد التي جعلت لهم. فلو لم يرد بتكوينهم سوى فنائهم لم يحتمل إنشاء ما به بقاؤهم. وإذ ثبت ذا لابد من أصل يؤلف بينهم ويكفهم عن التنازع والتباين الذي لديه الهلاك والفناء. فلزم أن الأحق في ذلك طلب أصل يجمعهم عليه لغاية ما احتمل وسعهم الوقوف عليه. إذ علم بحاجة كل من يشاهد وضرورة كل من أن لهم مديرا عالما بأحوالهم وبما عليه بقاؤهم، وأنه جبلهم على الحاجات، لا يدعهم وما هم عليه من الجهل وغلبة الأهواء - مع ما لهم من الحاجة في معرفة أن يقيم لهم من يدلهم على ذلك ويعرفهم ذلك. ولا بد من أن يجعل له ذليلا وبرهانا يعلمون خصوصه بالذى خصه به من الإمامة. لهم وأحوجهم إليه فيما عليه أمرهم، فيكون في ذلك ما بينا من صدق من ينتهي قوله إلى قول من دل عليه العالم بأمر العالم أنه هو الذي جعله المفزع والمعتمد، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And when that is established, it indicates that the creation of the world is for preservation, not for annihilation. The world is originally built upon different natures and opposing aspects, especially that which is intended from the perspective of the intellect—which brings together what should be together and separates what should be separated, and which the sages call the small world—so it is upon various inclinations and scattered natures, and overpowering desires implanted in them. If they were left and what they were created upon, they would contend in seeking benefits and kinds of might, honor, kingship, and authority, and that would result in mutual hatred then fighting, and in that is destruction and corruption such that if the world's existence were intended for that, the wisdom in its existence would be nullified. Along with the fact that humans and all animals are not capable of survival except through nourishment and what sustains their bodies for the periods appointed for them. If He did not intend by their creation anything but their annihilation, He would not have initiated what sustains them. And when this is established, there must be an origin that unites them and restrains them from conflict and divergence, which lead to destruction and annihilation. Therefore, the right thing in that is to seek an origin that brings them together to the utmost extent possible for them to grasp. Since it is known by the need of every observer and the necessity for everyone that they have a director knowing their conditions and what their survival depends on, and that He created them with needs, He does not leave them with what they are upon—ignorance and overwhelming inclinations—along with their need to know that He establishes for them someone who guides them to that and makes it known to them. It is necessary that He makes for him a sign and proof by which they know his particularity by what He distinguished him with of imamate. They are in need of him for their affairs, so in that is what we have made clear of the truthfulness of one whose saying terminates at the saying of the one indicated by the world through the matter of the world, that he is the one whom He made the refuge and the reliance, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 604

Commentary
This passage proves the necessity of a unifying authority (such as a prophet or imam) for human society. Maturidi begins by asserting that the world was created for preservation, not annihilation. He then describes the inherent conflict in human nature: the world is built on diverse and opposing elements, especially the human microcosm, which suffers from conflicting desires. If left to their natural tendencies, humans would fight over benefits and status, leading to hatred and mutual destruction, which would nullify the wisdom of creation. Moreover, humans and animals require sustenance to survive; if God intended only their annihilation, He would not have provided the means of survival. Therefore, there must be an origin (أصل) that unites them and restrains conflict. The right course is to seek such an origin to the extent possible. Maturidi then argues that this origin requires a director (مدير) who knows their conditions, and that God, who created them with needs, does not abandon them but appoints someone to guide them. This guide must be distinguished by a clear proof (برهان) of his imamate. Ultimately, the truthfulness of the guide is established through the signs of the world, and he becomes the refuge and reliance for humanity. The passage concludes with a prayer: 'There is no power except with God.'

Key Terms
أصل يؤلف بينهم
مدير
إمامة
برهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That humans can naturally resolve conflicts and establish order without divine guidance.
Correction: Maturidi argues that due to overpowering desires and ignorance, humans left to themselves will inevitably descend into conflict and destruction. Therefore, a divinely appointed authority is necessary.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what would happen if humans were left to their natural inclinations without a unifying authority?
A) They would live in peace and harmony.
B) They would compete for benefits and eventually fight, leading to destruction.
C) They would develop rational systems of governance.
D) They would all agree on the same truth.

Key Takeaway
Humanity's inherent conflict necessitates a divinely appointed prophet or imam to preserve order and guide them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see conflicts in your own relationships or community, do you turn to divine guidance as the ultimate solution, or do you rely solely on your own reasoning and strategies? How does Maturidi’s argument challenge your default approach?
?
Maturidi says that if left to our natural inclinations, we would destroy each other. Where in your life do you see your own overpowering desires leading you away from peace and unity? What would it cost you to submit fully to the origin (asl) God has appointed?
?
The proof (burhan) of a true guide is his alignment with the divine order of the world. Are you actively seeking and following such a guide, or have you settled for leaders who merely satisfy your desires? What would change in your daily choices if you truly believed that God has not left you without a director?

The Threefold Proof for the Contingency of All Created Entities
Maturidi presents a threefold proof for the contingency of all entities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses revelation, observation, and reasoning to demonstrate that all physical entities are temporally originated (contingent) and dependent on a Creator.

Core Idea
Everything in creation is contingent, proven by three convergent ways: revelation, observation, and reason.

Maturidi begins by stating that the proof for the contingency (temporal origination) of all entities rests on three fundamental ways of knowing. These ways—revelation, direct observation, and rational inference—are not separate but converge to establish the same truth. In this passage, he elaborates each way, showing how even the seeming claimant to eternity (the living being) is shown by its own origin to be contingent. This lesson walks you through the three prongs of this foundational proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation without pausing to analyse. Get a feel for Maturidi's argumentative flow.
Notice that he mentions three ways but focuses on two in detail.
2
Identify the first way: al-khabar (revelation)
Locate where Maturidi explains the proof from revelation. What does revelation say about creation? Why is it authoritative?
He refers to the Quranic statement that Allah is the Creator of everything.
3
Identify the second way: observation of living beings
Find where he uses observable evidence: living beings that claim eternity are contradicted by their own beginnings. What does their small origin prove?
He argues that everyone knows their own origin as small, so they cannot be eternal.
4
Identify the third way: rational reasoning on need and deficiency
Find the argument from need (haja) and ignorance. How do these qualities prove contingency?
Eternity implies self-sufficiency; contingent things show dependence.
5
Reflect on the unity of the proof
Consider why Maturidi presents three ways rather than one. How do they reinforce each other?
The three ways together make the proof comprehensive and irrefutable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل على حدث الأعيان قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : الدليل على حدث الأعيان هو شهادة الوجوه الثلاثة التي ذكرنا من سبل العلم بالأشياء . فأما الخبر فما ثبت من الله تعالى من وجه يعجز البشر عن دليل مثله لأحد : إنه أخبر أنه خالق كل شيء، وبديع السماوات والأرض ، وأن له منك ما فيهن . وقد بينا لزوم القول بالخبر . الأحياء ادعى لنفسه القدم أو أشار إلى معنى يدل على قدمه بل ليس أحد من لو قال لعرف كذبة هو بالضرورة، وكذا كل من حضره بما رأوه صغيرا، ويذكر ابتداؤه ايضا . لذلك لزم القول بحدث الأحياء . ثم الأموات تحت تدبير الأحياء ، أحق بالحدث ، والله الموفق . وتعلم الخيس ، وهو أن كل عين من الأعيان يحس مُحاطا بالضرورة مبنيا بالحاجة ، والقدم هو شرط الغنا ؛ لأنه يستغى بقدمه عن غيره ، والضرورة والحاجة يحوجانه إلى غيره ، فلزم به حدثه. وأيضا أن كل شيء جاهل يبدو اخاله عاجز عن إصلاح ما يفسد منه فى المال التو منه في المحال التي هو فيها موصوف بالكمال فى القوة والعلم إذا كان ذلك حيا ، ولو كان ميتا فسلطان الحى عليه جَارٍ
The proof for the contingency of entities. The Shaykh Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: The proof for the contingency of entities is the testimony of the three aspects that we mentioned from the ways of knowledge of things. As for the report (khabar), it is that which is established from God Almighty in a manner that incapacitates humans from producing a like proof for anyone: that He informed that He is the Creator of everything, and the Originator of the heavens and the earth, and that to Him belongs what is in them. And we have explained the necessity of accepting the report. [As for] the living beings, it claims for itself eternity or indicates a meaning that implies its eternity, but there is no one who, if he said [that], would not know his lie necessarily, and likewise everyone who was present at it [seeing it] as small, and also mentions its beginning. Therefore it is necessary to hold the contingency of living beings. Then the dead, under the management of the living, are more deserving of contingency, and God is the Grantor of success. And learn the [??] (al-khiss), which is that every entity that is perceived is surrounded by necessity, built on need, and eternity is the condition of self-sufficiency because it is self-sufficient by its eternity from others, and necessity and need render it needful of others, so its contingency is established by that. And also that everything ignorant appears as if(?)[?] incapable of repairing what corrupts from it, in the wealth[?] from it[?] in the places in which it is described as perfect in power and knowledge if it is alive, and if it were dead, then the authority of the living over it is ongoing.
Source pages p. 610

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the temporal origination (hadath) of all physical entities (al-a'yan). The proof relies on three ways of knowing: khabar (revealed report), 'iyan (direct observation), and i'tibar (analogical reasoning), though only two are fully elaborated. First, the report: revelation states unequivocally that God is the Creator of all things, and we have already established the necessity of accepting prophetic reports. Second, observation: living beings claim eternity for themselves, yet everyone knows this is false because they are seen to originate and grow from a small state. Their obvious beginning proves they are contingent. Moreover, dead things, being under the control of the living, are even more clearly contingent. Third, reasoning: every perceived entity is characterized by need and dependence; eternity requires self-sufficiency, but contingent things depend on others, proving their contingency. Also, all things are ignorant and incapable of self-repair without external aid, which further demonstrates their need and thus their contingency. The proof collectively establishes that everything in the physical world is created and dependent on a Creator.

Key Terms
حدث الأعيان (hadath al-‘ayan)
الخبر (khabar)
الوجوه الثلاثة (the three ways)
القدم (qidam)
الغنا (self-sufficiency)
الضرورة (necessity)
الحاجة (need)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the proof for contingency relies solely on revelation, ignoring rational arguments.
Correction: Maturidi explicitly includes observation and reasoning as independent and necessary ways of proof, showing that reason and revelation converge.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is one of the three ways of knowledge used in the proof for contingency?
A) Al-ijma' (consensus)
B) Al-khabar (revelation)
C) Al-qiyas (analogical reasoning)
D) Al-hiss (sense perception)

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that the contingency of all created entities is established through three convergent ways: revelation affirms creation, observation shows origins, and reasoning reveals dependence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if you are contingent and in constant need of God's preservation, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient? What concrete change would fully internalizing your contingency bring to your daily life?
?
The proof uses observation of your own beginning. When was the last time you sincerely reflected on the fact that you did not exist and were brought into being by God? How does that humble you right now, and what specific attitude or action should it correct?
?
If everything in creation is needy and dependent, what does that mean for your attachments to worldly things? Identify one thing you are currently relying on as if it were self-sufficient, and commit to reorienting your trust toward the Self-Sufficient alone. What is your first step?

Proof for Temporal Origination via Testimony and Living Beings
Maturidi presents the proof for temporal origination via divine testimony and the nature of living beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's proof for the temporal origination of entities based on divine testimony and the rational observation that every living being has a beginning.

Core Idea
Creation is proven by God's word and the obvious beginning of every living thing.

Maturidi grounds his proof in three reliable ways of knowing. Here he focuses on divine report and a rational argument from living beings. You'll see how he combines revelation and reason to establish that everything in creation is originated, not eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with an open mind
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the two main parts: the argument from report and the argument from living beings.
2
Identify the three ways of knowledge
Notice how Maturidi references three paths but only elaborates on two in this passage. Consider why he mentions all three.
The three ways are report, sense perception, and reason.
3
Analyze the argument from living beings
See the logical structure: no living being can credibly claim eternity because everyone knows they began small. This makes originating them necessary.
4
Connect report and rationality
Observe how testimony from God and rational observation converge on the same conclusion that all entities are originated.
Both are valid proofs in their own right.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : الدليل على حدث الأعيان هو شهادة الوجوه الثلاثة التي ذكرنا من سبل العلم بالأشياء . فأما الخبر فما ثبت من الله تعالى من وجه يعجز البشر عن دليل مثله لأحد : إنه أخبر أنه خالق كل شيء، وبديع السماوات والأرض ، وأن له منك ما فيهن . وقد بينا لزوم القول بالخبر . الأحياء ادعى لنفسه القدم أو أشار إلى معنى يدل على قدمه بل وليس أحد من لو قال لعرف كذبة هو بالضرورة، وكذا كل من حضره بما رأوه صغيرا، ويذكر ابتداؤه ايضا . لذلك لزم القول بحدث الأحياء . ثم الأموات تحت تدبير الأحياء ، أحق بالحدث ، والله الموفق
Shaykh Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The proof for the origination of entities is the attestation of the three ways that we mentioned among the paths of knowledge of things. As for report, it is what is established from Allah Almighty from a facet that humans are incapable of producing a proof similar to it for anyone: that He informed that He is the Creator of everything, and the Originator of the heavens and the earth, and that to Him belongs what is in them. And we have shown the necessity of accepting report. The living being claims eternity for itself or points to a meaning indicating its eternity, but there is not anyone who, if he said [something], his lie would not be known necessarily, and likewise everyone who witnesses it, as they saw it small, and its beginning is also mentioned. Therefore accepting the origination of living beings is necessary. Then the dead, being under the management of the living, are more deserving of origination. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 610

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to the proof for the temporal origination (ḥudūth) of entities. The author begins by stating that the proof rests on three recognized ways to knowledge of things: report (khabar), sense perception (ḥiss), and reason (ʿaql). He then elaborates on the first way, report: the divine reports in the Quran attest that Allah is the Creator of all things and the Originator of the heavens and the earth, and that everything belongs to Him. Since these reports come from a source that no human can match (i.e., divine revelation), they are authoritative. The author then moves to a rational argument based on the observable nature of living beings. He observes that no living being can credibly claim eternity for itself; anyone who did would be known as a liar by necessity, because all living beings are seen to begin small and have a known beginning. Therefore, logically, living beings must be originated. Moreover, the dead, which are under the control of the living, are even more clearly originated. The author concludes with a prayer for divine assistance.

Correction: Maturidi also uses a rational observation of living beings, showing that the proof is not solely based on revelation.

B) Because humans are incapable of producing a similar proof.
C) Because it is based on sense perception.
D) Because it is self-evident.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi proves that all entities are originated by combining divine testimony that God is the Creator with the rational observation that every living being has a clear beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What does that mean for your claim to self-sufficiency? Do you live as if you control your own existence, or do you consciously owe your being to your Creator?
?
The proof says that if someone claimed eternity for themselves, their lie would be known necessarily. Do you live with the same unwavering certainty about your own createdness? Or do you sometimes act as if you are the master of your fate, forgetting the One who brought you from nothing?
?
Maturidi argues that even dead things are under the management of the living. Death is a constant reminder of your temporality. Are you preparing for that moment when you will be among the dead, managed by others? What change must you make today to live in light of your inevitable end?

The Proof from Need
Maturidi argues that neediness implies dependence and thus origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the observed neediness of all entities to argue for their temporal origination.

Core Idea
Need implies dependence, and dependence negates eternity.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents a core argument for the contingency of the world. He observes that every entity we see is characterized by need and necessity. Since eternity requires self-sufficiency, neediness proves that these entities cannot be eternal and must have been brought into existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how Maturidi builds his argument step by step.
The Arabic contains some unclear phrases due to OCR, but the main logic is clear.
2
Identify the key concepts
Identify the terms: 'need' (ḥāja), 'necessity' (ḍarūra), 'eternity' (qidam), 'self-sufficiency' (ghinā). Understand how they relate.
3
Trace the logical argument
Follow Maturidi's reasoning: every entity is needy → eternity requires self-sufficiency → needy entities cannot be eternal → therefore they are originated.
This is a reductio argument: if they were eternal, they would be self-sufficient, but they are not.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this argument matters for proving the existence of God. If all observable things are originated, they require a creator.
This argument is foundational in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتعلم الخيس [؟] ، وهو أن كل عين من الأعيان يحس مُحاطا بالضرورة مبنيا بالحاجة ، والقدم هو شرط الغنا ؛ لأنه يستغى بقدمه عن غيره ، والضرورة والحاجة يحوجانه إلى غيره ، فلزم به حدثه. وأيضا أن كل شيء جاهل يبدو اخاله [؟] عاجز عن إصلاح ما يفسد منه فى المال التو منه في المحال التي هو فيها موصوف بالكمال فى القوة والعلم إذا كان ذلك حيا ، ولو كان ميتا فسلطان الحى عليه جَارٍ أنه لم يكن واحد منها إلا بغيره ، وإذا ثبت الغير لزم الحدث ، إذ القدم يمنع الكون لغيره
And you know the [?] that every individual entity is perceived to be surrounded by necessity and built upon need, while eternity is a condition of self-sufficiency, because it is self-sufficient by its eternity from others, while necessity and need make it need others. So its origination follows necessarily. And also that every ignorant thing appears its [?] incapable of repairing what becomes corrupt from it in property [?] from it in the places in which it is described as complete in strength and knowledge if it were alive, and if it were dead, the authority of the living over it runs, that not one of them exists except with another, and when 'the other' is established, origination follows, because eternity prevents existence for the sake of another.
Source pages p. 610

Commentary
This passage presents two interrelated proofs: one from need and one from incapacity. The author first asserts that every observable entity is characterized by need (ḥāja) and necessity (ḍarūra). Eternity (qidam) requires self-sufficiency (ghinā), but since these entities need others, they cannot be eternal. Therefore, they are originated. The second proof states that everything that is ignorant or appears in states of incapacity cannot be eternal. Even a living being that appears complete in strength and knowledge is still subject to corruption and needs repair. If it is dead, it is under the power of the living. Moreover, no entity exists independently; each one exists only with another (i.e., is contingent). Since eternity cannot depend on another, these entities must be originated. The argument is structured as a reductio: if they were eternal, they would have to be self-sufficient and independent, but observation shows the opposite.

Key Terms
الحاجة
الضرورة
القدم
الغنى
الغير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Neediness is just a quality and does not affect the nature of an entity's existence.
Correction: In this argument, neediness is a logical indicator of contingency. If an entity needs others, it cannot be self-sufficient and hence cannot be eternal, because eternity entails absolute independence.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does neediness negate eternity?
A) Because eternal things are always healthy.
B) Because eternity requires self-sufficiency, and neediness implies dependence.
C) Because need is a temporary state.
D) Because only God is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi proves that all observable entities are originated because their neediness contradicts the self-sufficiency required for eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as though your existence depends entirely on Allah every moment, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient? What would change if you internalized that every need you have is a proof that you are not eternal and that you must depend on the Eternal One?
?
When you feel self-sufficient in any area of your life, how does Maturidi's argument expose that feeling as an illusion? What need of yours have you ignored that points directly to your dependence on Allah?
?
If neediness is a hallmark of creation, how should that reshape your understanding of prayer, humility, and reliance on Allah today?

The Proof from Incapacity
Maturidi argues that incapacity and dependence on others prove origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the observed incapacity and dependence of entities to argue for their temporal origination.

Core Idea
Incapacity to exist independently proves that entities are contingent.

Maturidi presents a second, complementary argument: every entity is either alive or dead, and in both cases it shows incapacity and dependence on others. Even a living being that seems perfect cannot repair its own corruption. No entity exists solely by itself, so each depends on another, which negates eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the continuation of the passage
Read the second part of the passage starting with 'وأيضا'. Note how Maturidi shifts from need to incapacity.
This part builds on the first but adds a new angle: incapacity to repair oneself.
2
Analyze the states of living and dead
Maturidi considers two cases: if an entity is alive, it is ignorant and incapable of repairing its own corruption; if it is dead, it is under the authority of the living. In both cases, it depends on something else.
The idea is that even a living being that appears complete is still subject to decay and needs external repair.
3
Understand the concept of 'the other'
Maturidi argues that no entity exists without another. The presence of 'the other' negates eternity, because an eternal being cannot depend on anything else.
This ties back to the first proof's notion of self-sufficiency.
4
Synthesize the two proofs
Consider how the proof from need and the proof from incapacity together form a strong argument for the contingency of all things.
Both proofs rely on the same principle: eternity requires independence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

So its origination follows necessarily. And also that every ignorant thing appears its [?] incapable of repairing what becomes corrupt from it in property [?] from it in the places in which it is described as complete in strength and knowledge if it were alive, and if it were dead, the authority of the living over it runs, that not one of them exists except with another, and when 'the other' is established, origination follows, because eternity prevents existence for the sake of another.

Commentary
This passage presents two interrelated proofs: one from need and one from incapacity. The author first asserts that every observable entity is characterized by need (ḥāja) and necessity (ḍarūra). Eternity (qidam) requires self-sufficiency (ghinā), but since these entities need others, they cannot be eternal. Therefore, they are originated. The second proof states that everything that is ignorant or appears in states of incapacity cannot be eternal. Moreover, no entity exists independently; each one exists only with another (i.e., is contingent). Since eternity cannot depend on another, these entities must be originated. The argument is structured as a reductio: if they were eternal, they would have to be self-sufficient and independent, but observation shows the opposite.

?
