The Body's Eternity Cannot Be Due to an Accident
Maturidi argues that body's eternity is not due to an accident, and answers an objection about color.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that an accident cannot be the cause of a body's eternity, and how he refutes an objection based on the analogy with color.

Core Idea
Accidents are contingent and cannot grant eternity.

Imam Maturidi presents a key argument that the eternity of a body cannot be attributed to an accident occurring in it. He then addresses a potential objection: just as a thing exists without a color, perhaps a body exists without an accident causing eternity. In this lesson, you will follow his reasoning and see why the analogy fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Let Maturidi's own argument come through.
Notice the structure: first a claim, then an objection and response.
2
Identify Maturidi's core claim
Find the sentence where Maturidi states that the cause of the body's eternity cannot be an accident. Ask yourself why he says this.
Key phrase: 'ولا يجوز أن يكون سبب قدم الجسم حدوث عرض فيه'
3
Understand the objection and refutation
An objector says a thing can be without color yet not be color—so why can't a body be eternal without an accident? Maturidi explains why this analogy fails. Focus on his reasoning about 'occurrence' (hadath) and the nature of color.
The refutation hinges on the definition of 'hadath' as coming into being after non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتابع البقاء فيه . ولا يجوز أن يكون سبب قدم الجسم حدوث عرض فيه فصار هو عديله وقرينه ؛ فلذلك لا يتقدمه ، والله الموفق .
وعارض بعض من يقول بهذا ؛ لما لا يوجد الشيء بلا لون ، ثم لا يجب أن يكون لونا . وذلك لا معنى له ؛ لما أن الحدث هو وصف الكون بعد أن يكن ، فإذا وجد غيره غير مفارقه - وفيه هذا الوصف - لزمه هذا الحكم ، وليس اللون بلون المعنى يُوجد في المتلون به ؛ لذلك اختلفا ، لكن جملة الجسم إذ لا يخلو من الألوان فلا يسيقها ولكن يسبق واحدا فواحدا ، وكذلك أمر الإحداث .
The continuity of existence is in it. It is not permissible that the cause of the eternity of the body be the occurrence of an accident in it, so it became its equal and its companion; therefore it (the accident) does not precede it, and Allah is the Grantor of success.
And some of those who say this object: because a thing is not found without a color, yet it is not necessary that it be a color. That is meaningless; because occurrence is the description of being (al-kawn) after it was not. So if other than it is found not separate from it—and in it is this description—this judgment necessarily applies to it. And color is not meaning that is found in the thing colored; therefore they differ. But the entirety of the body, since it is not free from colors, does not precede them but precedes them one by one, and likewise the matter of bringing into existence.
Source pages p. 614

Commentary
This passage contains a claim and an objection-and-answer. First, Maturidi states that the continuity of existence (baqa') in the body cannot be the cause of the body's eternity. If a body were eternal because of an accident occurring in it, that accident would be concurrent with the body, not prior. Then an objector argues: a thing can exist without having a color, yet it is not itself a color; similarly, a body might exist without an accident making it eternal. Maturidi replies that this analogy fails because 'occurrence' (hadath) means coming into being after non-existence. When something is found inseparable from that description (the accident of existence), the judgment of contingency applies. Color is different because it is a meaning (ma'na) in the colored thing, not the same as the accident of existence. The body as a whole is never free from colors, so it does not precede them in time, but each individual color is preceded by the body. This refutes the objection.

Key Terms
بقاء
قدم
جسم
عرض
حدث
كون
لون
معنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any accident can serve as cause for eternity.
Correction: Maturidi shows it is impossible for an accident to be the cause of the body's eternity because the accident itself is contingent and inseparable from the body.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi reject the objection that a thing can exist without a color as analogous to a body existing without an accident causing eternity?
A) Because color is an accident and existence is a substance
B) Because occurrence (hadath) is a description of being after non-existence, and the objector's analogy ignores this
C) Because bodies cannot exist without color
D) Because the objector is a heretic

Key Takeaway
The eternity of a body cannot be caused by an accident because accidents are contingent and inseparable from the body, and the analogy with color fails due to the nature of occurrence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every moment of your existence is an accident that could cease, what difference does it make in how you live today? What would it mean to truly believe that you are not self-sufficient?
?
The objector tried to find a false analogy to avoid acknowledging contingency. In what areas of your life do you rationalize to avoid admitting your complete dependence on Allah?
?
Since your body is never free from changing accidents, yet your existence is entirely from Allah, what concrete act of gratitude or submission will you perform today as a direct response to this teaching?

Maturidi's Analogical Proof for the World's Contingency
Maturidi proves the world's contingency through analogies with writing, separation, and composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses everyday analogies—writing, separation, and composition—to argue that the world is contingent and requires a Creator.

Core Idea
The world's composition points to a Creator just as writing points to a writer.

Maturidi presents a simple yet profound argument: just as we know that writing requires a writer, we can infer that the complex composition of the world requires an external agent. This lesson traces his reasoning step by step, showing how the argument gains strength from the world's greater wonder.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from premise to examples to conclusion.
The Arabic includes a stray '6' likely from pagination; ignore it.
2
Identify the analogies
Maturidi lists writing, separation, composition, rest, and motion. Each requires an agent. List them and note the pattern.
3
Apply to the world
The world as a whole is composed and separated. Therefore, like the individual examples, it also requires an external agent. Why does Maturidi say the inference is even stronger?
He mentions the 'wonder' of the world's composition is higher.
4
Reflect on the strength of the argument
Consider the common misunderstanding that analogies from human actions are too weak. Maturidi anticipates this by asserting the world's greater complexity makes the argument more, not less, compelling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً إنَّه إذا يكن أحد يُخبرنا أو كان أزليا من جوهر العالم فلا وجه للعلم به إلا بالاستدلال مشع العالم ; إذ هو عن قدمه
6
ثم لا تعلم كتابة بلا كاتب ، ولا تفرقا إلا بمفرق ، وكذلك الاجتماع وكذلك السكون والحركة ، فيلزم فى جملة العالم ذلك ; إذ هو مؤلف مفرق بل الأعجوبة في تأليف العالم أرفع ، فهو ألحق أن لا يتفرق ولا يجتمع إلا بغيره ، ثم كل ما فى الشاهد من التأليف والكتابة يكون أحدث من به كان ، فمثله جميع العالم في معنى ما ذكرت ، وبالله التوفيق.
And also, if there were no one to inform us, or if something were eternal from the substance of the world, there would be no way to know it except through inference from the world's [؟]; for it is from its eternity.
Then you do not know writing without a writer, nor separation without a separator, and likewise composition, rest, and motion. So that necessarily applies to the entirety of the world; for it is composed and separated, nay the wonder in the composition of the world is higher, so it is more deserving that it does not separate or come together except by other than it. And then everything in the witnessed realm of composition and writing is more recent than that by which it was. So the like of it is all the world in the meaning I mentioned, and with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 614

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the world's contingency using analogies from ordinary experience. Maturidi argues that if there were an eternal entity from the world's substance, we would only know it through inference from the world's signs. Then he lists familiar examples: writing requires a writer, separation requires a separator, composition, rest, and motion all require an agent. The world as a whole is composed and separated, so it too must require an external agent. Indeed, the world's composition is more wondrous than human artifacts, so it is even more necessary that it does not compose or separate by itself. Every example of composition and writing in the witnessed world is caused by something else, and the same applies to the entire world.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Analogies from human actions (e.g., writing) are too weak to prove God's existence because human actions are different from divine creation.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the world's composition is even more wondrous than human artifacts, so the analogy is actually stronger, not weaker. The principle that composition requires a composer applies universally.

Checkpoint
What is the main analogy Maturidi uses to argue for the world's contingency?
A) Writing requires a writer
B) Color requires a colored object
C) Intellect requires a rational soul
D) Hearing requires sound

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that just as writing implies a writer, the world's intricate composition implies a Creator, making the argument from contingency accessible through everyday experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the intricate order of the universe—your own body, the seasons, the stars—do you truly see them as signs pointing to a Creator, or have they become so familiar that you no longer pause to acknowledge the Hand behind them? What would it take for you to see each moment as a fresh evidence of His existence?
?
If you are convinced that the world's composition demands a Creator, then why do you sometimes live as if you are autonomous, independent of that Creator? Where in your daily life do you act as if you are the writer of your own story without reference to the One who composed you?
?
Maturidi argues that the world's wonder makes the proof even stronger. When you consider the vastness of galaxies or the complexity of a single cell, does it stir in you a sense of awe and accountability toward the One who brought it all into existence? If not, what is blocking that response, and what will you do about it today?

Every Part of the World Points to Contingency
Maturidi concludes that exhaustive proof is impossible but every part of the world displays contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that while exhaustive proof of contingency is impossible, every part of the world provides abundant and clear evidence of its createdness.

Core Idea
Every part of the world screams contingency — exhaustive proof impossible, signs everywhere.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi brings his discussion of contingent evidence to a close with a powerful observation. He acknowledges that no human can prove the temporality of every single thing exhaustively. Yet far from being a weakness, this limitation highlights the overwhelming abundance of signs: every part of the world, from its initial state to its need for repair, from its inability to preserve itself to the presence of lowly qualities, all clearly indicate that it is created and dependent on a Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage in Arabic and English
Read the Arabic passage aloud if possible, then the literal translation. Notice how Maturidi first states a limitation (exhaustive proof exceeds human capacity) and then pivots to a positive assertion (every part shows evidence).
Pay attention to the rhythm: the limitation actually strengthens the argument by showing the signs are too numerous to count.
2
Identify the five signs of contingency
Maturidi lists several specific signs. List them: initial state, need for repair, inability to create its like, inability to preserve itself or alter its substance, and the presence of base qualities requiring external management.
Each sign alone is sufficient; together they are overwhelming.
3
Reflect on the theological implication
Ask yourself: Why does Maturidi mention that exhaustive proof is impossible? How does that affect the argument for God's existence? The answer is that the argument does not rely on perfect enumeration but on the clear, universal evidence that is obvious to anyone who reflects.
This passage guards against the misconception that incomplete enumeration means the argument is weak.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو تكلف الاستقصاء فى مثل هذا ليخرج عن طوق البشر ; إذ ما من شيء مما يحس أو يُسمع به من أجزاء العالم إلا ودلالة حدثه ظاهرة : من جمله بابتداء حاله ، وباصلاح ما يفسد ، أو ينشئ مثله ، وعجزه عما به حفظ نفسه أو تقليبه عن جوهزه ، مع ما فيه الخبيث والقبيح والذليل والمهان الذي لولا تدبير غيره فيه ما احتمل أن يكون كذلك ، وبالله التوفيق
And if one were to undertake exhaustive investigation of such matters, it would exceed human capacity; for there is nothing among what is sensed or heard of the parts of the world except that the evidence of its temporality is apparent: from the totality by its initial state, and by the repair of what corrupts, or the creation of its like, and its inability regarding what preserves itself or its alteration from its [؟] substance, along with what is in it of the vile, ugly, lowly, and contemptible which, without the management of another in it, would not likely be as it is. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 614

Commentary
This passage is a concluding remark on the limitations of human demonstration. Maturidi states that trying to prove the contingency of every single thing exhaustively is beyond human ability. However, every part of the world that we sense or hear about shows clear signs of being created: its initial beginning, the need for repair when it corrupts, the inability to create its like, its dependence on external preservation, and the presence of base and lowly things that require an external manager. All these point to contingency.

Key Terms
الاستقصاء
طوق البشر
أجزاء العالم
دلالة
حدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If exhaustive proof is impossible, the argument for contingency is weak.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that the abundance of clear signs in every part of the world makes exhaustive proof unnecessary; the signs are sufficient and obvious.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about the capacity to prove contingency of every single thing?
A) It is easy and should be done
B) It exceeds human capacity, but enough signs exist
C) It is unnecessary because only a few signs are needed
D) It is possible only for prophets

Key Takeaway
Even though exhaustive proof is impossible, the abundance of clear signs in every part of the world is sufficient to establish contingency and point to a Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the flaws and weaknesses in your own life—your inability to control your health, your anger, your death—do you see them as signs that something else must manage you? Or do you ignore these signs and pretend you are self-sufficient? What would it take for you to stop pretending and admit that every part of you points to the need for a Creator?
?
You have seen countless signs today—from the rising sun to your own heartbeat. Yet how many of them have you actually taken as evidence of the Creator? Are you truly paying attention, or are you just coasting through life ignoring the testimony of every moment? What will you do tomorrow to not let another sign pass unnoticed?
?
Maturidi says that even the vile and ugly things in the world show contingency. Think of something in your life that you consider ugly or painful—a failure, a sin, a loss. Can you see how even that thing, in its weakness, points to the need for a Manager beyond yourself? Will you let that sign lead you to humility and dependence on Allah, or will you resent it and miss the message?

Proof for a Creator: Contingency and Dependence
Maturidi presents a concise proof for the world's creator from contingency and lack of self-organization.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's proof that the world's contingency and lack of self-organization necessitate a creator.

Core Idea
The world's inability to assemble itself proves it depends on a maker.

Maturidi presents an elegant proof combining logical contingency with everyday observation. He first reminds us that the world is contingent, then points to a simple fact: nothing in our experience brings itself together or separates on its own. This leads to the conclusion that the world's existence comes from another. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Notice the concise style and the flow from premise to conclusion.
The proof is very brief—every word matters.
2
Identify the two premises
The proof rests on two premises: (1) the world's contingency (huduth) is already established, and (2) nothing in the witnessed world (al-shahid) can gather or separate by itself.
The first premise is from earlier discussion; the second is empirical observation.
3
Understand the conclusion
From these two premises, Maturidi concludes that the world's existence and changes come from another (i.e., a creator). If the world were self-causing, it would have the power to organize itself, but it does not.
The conclusion is stated as "ثبت أن ذلك كان بغيره."
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this proof is foundational for tawhid. It shows that the existence of everything points to a necessary, self-sufficient creator who is not like His creation.
This proof is used by many kalam scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل على أن للعالم محدثاً. قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: ثم الدليل على أن للعالم محدثا أنه ثبت حدثه بما بينا، وبما لا يوجد شيء منه فى الشاهد يجتمع بنفسه ويفرق، ثبت أن ذلك كان بغيره، والله الموفق.
The proof that the world has a maker. Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Then the proof that the world has a maker is that its contingency (huduth) is established by what we have explained, and because nothing from it is found in the witnessed world that gathers by itself and separates, so it is established that that (existence) was by another. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 616

Commentary
This is a concise proof for the existence of a creator (muhdith) for the world. Maturidi builds on the earlier establishment of the world's contingency (huduth) — that it came into existence after not existing. He adds an empirical observation: in the witnessed world (al-shahid), nothing seen comes together or separates by itself. Therefore, the world's existence and its changing states must be caused by an external agent. The proof is presented as a logical conclusion: if the world were self-causing, it would have the power to assemble and disassemble itself, but it does not, so it is dependent on another.

Key Terms
دليل
محدث
حدث
شاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof is purely a priori and does not rely on observation.
Correction: Maturidi combines the a priori established contingency with an empirical observation (nothing self-organizes), making the proof both rational and empirical.

Checkpoint
What empirical observation does Maturidi use in this proof?
A) The world is eternal
B) Nothing in the witnessed world gathers and separates by itself
C) The world is composed of atoms
D) The world is in constant motion

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's proof combines the logically established contingency of the world with the empirical observation that nothing self-organizes, leading to the conclusion that the world must have a creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If nothing in your life comes together by itself, why do you sometimes act as if your successes are self-made?
?
When you look at the order in your daily life, are you truly attributing it to the Creator or are you tempted to attribute it to chance or your own effort?
?
How does the fact that everything depends on a Creator change how you approach your next major decision?

The World's Specific Determinations Prove a Creator
Maturidi presents a twofold argument from determination and evil for a creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the world's specific times, states, attributes, and the existence of evil to argue that the world is not self-subsisting but requires an external cause.

Core Idea
Specific determinations and evil show the world cannot be self-sufficient.

This passage presents one of Imam Maturidi's key philosophical arguments for the existence of God. It first argues that if the world were self-subsisting, it would have no reason to be in one specific time, state, or set of attributes rather than another. Since such specific determinations exist, an external cause is necessary. Then, it adds that if things were self-subsisting, they would all choose the best possible states, eliminating evil. The persistence of evil further confirms that the world is not self-subsisting but depends on another.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if...then...' and the conclusion.
The Arabic uses a concise logical style typical of kalam.
2
Identify the two conditional statements
Find the two 'if' clauses: (1) 'If the world were by itself...' and (2) 'And if it were permissible...' Note what each implies.
The second conditional builds on the first but adds the moral dimension of evil.
3
Understand the role of evil
Reflect on how Maturidi uses the existence of evil as evidence, not against God, but to prove that things cannot be self-sufficient.
This is a powerful response to the problem of evil.
4
Connect to the overall proof
See how both parts lead to the same conclusion: 'So the existence of that indicates that it is by another.' Recognize that 'that' refers to the specific determinations and the presence of evil.
The final phrase 'Allah is the Grantor of success' is a pious conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العالم لو كان بنفسه لم يكن وقتا أحق به من وقت، ولا حال أحق به من حال، ولا صفة أليق به من صفة، وإذا كان على أوقات وأحوال وصفات مختلفة ثبت أنه لم يكن به، ولو كان لجاز أن يكون كل شيء لنفسه أحوالاً هي أحسن الأحوال والصفات وخيرها، فيبطل به الشرور والقبائح، فدل وجود ذلك على كونه بغيره، والله الموفق.
If the world were by itself, no time would be more appropriate for it than any time, nor any state more appropriate than any state, nor any attribute more fitting than any attribute. And since it exists with different times, states, and attributes, it is established that it was not by itself. And if it were permissible, then it would be permissible for everything to have for itself the most beautiful states and attributes and the best of them, thereby nullifying evils and ugly things. So the existence of that indicates that it is by another. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 616

Commentary
This argument proceeds in two parts. First, if the world were self-subsisting, it would have no reason to be at a particular time rather than another, in a particular state rather than another, or with particular attributes. Since it has specific determinations, those must come from an external cause. Second, if things were self-subsisting, they would all choose the best possible states, which would eliminate evil and imperfection. The existence of evil and imperfection shows that things are not self-subsisting. Both points lead to the conclusion that the world is caused by another.

Key Terms
وقت
حال
صفة
استحقاق
شر
قبيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The existence of evil disproves God's existence.
Correction: Maturidi argues that evil actually proves the world is not self-subsisting, because a self-subsisting thing would choose the best states. Evil thus points to an external cause.

Checkpoint
According to this proof, what would follow if the world were self-subsisting?
A) All times would be equal for it
B) It would have only one attribute
C) Evils would not exist
D) Both A and C

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's proof from specific determinations and evil shows that the world cannot be self-sufficient, requiring an external cause – Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which area of your life do you most often act as if you are self-sufficient, ignoring the specific determinations (like your limited time, changing states, and imperfect attributes) that point to your need for a Creator? What one habit can you change today to consciously rely on Allah?
?
When you experience evil or suffering, do you let it push you away from God or, as Maturidi argues, use it to recognize that the world cannot stand on its own? What would it look like for you to let that realization truly sink into your heart right now?
?
If you truly believed that every specific moment, state, and quality of your existence is determined by Allah (not by chance or your own effort), how would your worry about the future change? What would you stop fearing and start trusting Allah for starting today?

Third Proof: Living and Dead Types Show Dependence
Maturidi argues from the incapacity of living beings to create themselves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the inability of living beings to create or repair themselves as evidence that they depend on an external creator, and how this applies even more to non-living things.

Core Idea
Living things cannot create themselves, so they must depend on a Creator.

Maturidi presents a concise but profound argument: everything in the world falls into either living or non-living. Living things, even at their peak, cannot produce their own kind or repair themselves. This shows they are dependent on something else. Non-living things are even more dependent, so the argument applies with greater force.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp Maturidi's concise argument.
Notice the logical flow: classification → observation → conclusion.
2
Identify the two types and their properties
Maturidi divides the world into living and dead. He notes that living things are ignorant of producing their like and repairing themselves at their peak.
The key words are 'إنشاء' (producing) and 'إصلاح' (repairing).
3
Understand the conclusion and its extension
Since living things cannot do these acts, their existence must be from another. Then Maturidi adds that the dead are even more deserving of this conclusion.
This is an a fortiori argument: if the powerful living depend on a creator, the powerless dead do even more.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أن العالم نوعان: حى وميت، وكل حي جاهل بابتدائه عن إنشاء مثله وإصلاح ما فسد منه وقت قوته وكماله، فثبت أنه كان بغيره، والميت أحق بذلك.
Also, the world is of two types: living and dead. Every living thing is at its beginning ignorant of producing its like and repairing what has decayed from it at the time of its strength and perfection. So it is established that it was by another. The dead is even more deserving of that.
Source pages p. 616

Commentary
This is a brief but powerful argument from the nature of living beings. Maturidi points out that living things, even at their peak of strength and perfection, are unable to produce their own kind or repair themselves. Since they lack this ability, their existence must be from an external creator. The dead are even more dependent, so the argument applies a fortiori. The conclusion is that all things in the world, whether living or dead, need a creator.

Key Terms
حي
ميت
إنشاء
إصلاح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that living things can reproduce or heal themselves, so they are self-sufficient.
Correction: Maturidi's point is that living things cannot create their own kind ex nihilo or repair themselves at will; their limited abilities still point to dependence on an external cause.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi mention that living things are 'ignorant' of producing their like?
A) To show they lack knowledge of their own species
B) To demonstrate their inability to create themselves or their kind
C) To argue against evolution
D) To prove that knowledge is unnecessary

Key Takeaway
The inability of living things to create or repair themselves proves they depend on an external Creator, and this logic applies even more to non-living things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are alive right now, but you did not choose to be born, and you cannot stop your body from aging or decay. How does the fact that you are 'ignorant of repairing what has decayed' from your own body humble your sense of self-sufficiency? What would change in your daily life if you truly lived knowing that every breath is a gift from the One who made you?
?
The passage says even at your peak strength and perfection, you are ignorant of producing your own kind. Every parent knows the awe and helplessness of bringing a child into the world—you can only facilitate, not create. In what areas of your life do you still pretend to be the creator when you are only a vessel?
?
Non-living things are even more deserving of dependence—your phone, your bed, the chair you sit on are all made. But you too are made. Do you treat your Creator as the Owner of your existence, or do you live as though you are self-made? What is one concrete way you can acknowledge your dependence on Him in the next hour?

The Mutual Dependence of Bodies and Accidents
Maturidi argues that bodies and accidents depend on each other, proving the world needs a creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mutual dependence of bodies and accidents shows that the world cannot be self-subsisting and thus requires an external creator.

Core Idea
Bodies and accidents need each other; neither is self-sufficient, so the world points to Allah.

Maturidi presents a powerful observation: everything in the world, whether a body or an accident, is locked in mutual dependence. Bodies always have accidents, and accidents never exist without bodies. This interdependence proves that neither can be the ultimate source of existence — they must depend on something beyond themselves.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Maturidi uses conditional language ('لو لا') but here it's declarative.
2
Identify the two premises
The argument has two key observations: first, every body is subject to accidents by necessity; second, accidents have no existence without a body. Find these in the text.
3
Understand the conclusion
From the mutual need, Maturidi concludes that bodies and accidents cannot be self-sufficient. Ask yourself: what does this imply about the world's origin?
The conclusion leads to affirming Allah as the necessary existent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أن العالم لا يخلو كل عين منه إلى ما يحتمله من الأعراض قهرا، وما اعترضه من الأعراض لا قيام لها ولا وجود دونه، فثبت بذلك دخول كل واحد منهما تحت حاجة الآخر، فيبطل أن يكون بنفسه محتاجا إلى غير به يوجد ويقوم، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Also, the world is not free, every individual of it, from what it undergoes of accidents by compulsion, and the accidents that befall it have no subsistence and no existence without it. So it is established by that that each of them falls under the need of the other, thereby nullifying that it is by itself while being in need of another for its existence and subsistence. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 616

Commentary
This proof highlights the interdependence of bodies and accidents. Bodies are always subject to accidents involuntarily, and accidents cannot exist independently of bodies. This mutual need shows that neither is self-sufficient. If the world were self-subsisting, it would not be in need of another, but the observed dependency disproves that. Therefore, the world must have an external cause that provides existence and subsistence to both.

Misconception: Bodies are self-subsisting and independent of accidents.
Correction: Maturidi demonstrates that bodies are always subject to accidents, and accidents cannot exist without bodies, so neither is independent. The mutual dependence refutes self-sufficiency.

Checkpoint

B) Accidents can exist without bodies

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

What would it take for you to genuinely acknowledge that every atom of your power originates from Allah? Will you pause right now and whisper a prayer of acknowledgment?

The Proof from Witnessed Contingency
Maturidi states the proof from witnessed contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the witness of ordinary experience (shahid) to prove that the world must have a maker.

Core Idea
Nothing in the world comes together or separates by itself.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi introduces a simple yet powerful argument. He begins by reminding us that the world's contingency (having a beginning) has already been established. Then he draws on everyday observation: we never see anything assembling or disassembling on its own. Everything that changes or combines requires an external agent. This leads to the conclusion that the world itself is not self-sufficient and therefore must have a creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Read the Arabic once without analysis, then the literal translation. Notice the concise style.
The phrase 'wa Allāhu al-muwaffiq' is a pious closure.
2
Identify the key claim
Maturidi claims that nothing in the witnessed world combines or separates by itself. Find that in the text.
3
Connect to contingency
The proof builds on the already established contingency of the world. Ask: Why is contingency necessary for this argument?
Contingency means the world had a beginning and is dependent.
4
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider why this empirical observation is convincing. Does it apply to the world as a whole?
The argument parallels everyday causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : ثم الدليل على أن للعالم محدثا أنه ثبت حدثه بما بينا ، وبما لا يوجد شيء منه فى الشاهد يجتمع بنفسه ويفرق ، ثبت أن ذلك كان بغيره ، والله الموفق . العالم لو كان بنفسه لم يكن وقتا أحق به من وقت ، ولا حــال والثاني أن العالم لو به من حال ، ولا صفة أليق به من صفة ، وإذا كان على أوقات وأحوال أولى به من صفات مختلفة / ثبت أنه لم يكن به ، ولو كان الجاز أن يكون كل شيء لنفسه أحوالاً (هي) أحسن الأحوال والصفات وخيرها ، فيبطل به الشرور والقبائح، فدل وجود ذلك على كونه بغيره ، والله الموفق .
Then the proof that the world has a maker is that its contingency has been established by what we have explained, and because nothing in the witnessed world exists that combines and separates by itself; so it is established that that was by another. And God is the granter of success. If the world were by itself, no time would be more appropriate for it than another time, nor any state and the second [point] is that if the world were by itself, no state would be more appropriate for it than another state, nor any quality more suitable than another quality. And since it occurs in various times and states and different qualities, it is established that it was not by itself. And if it were possible that everything by itself could have the best states and qualities, then evil and ugly things would be invalidated. So the existence of that indicates that it is by another. And God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 616

Commentary
This passage contains two proofs. The first proof: we have already established the world's contingency (that it had a beginning). Additionally, in our experience (shahid), nothing comes together or separates by itself; everything that combines or separates does so through an agent. Therefore, the world must have been originated by another. The second proof: if the world were self-existent, it would not have one time more suitable than another, nor one state or quality more suitable than another; but we observe variation, so it must be dependent. Moreover, if everything could independently choose the best qualities, evil would not exist. Since evil exists, the world cannot be self-sufficient. Thus, it is established that the world is by another (i.e., a creator).

Key Terms
محدث
حدث
شاهد
بنفسه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think natural processes are self-sufficient and need no external cause.
Correction: Maturidi argues that nothing in our experience combines or separates by itself, so the world's changes require a cause.

Checkpoint
What is the key observation Maturidi makes from the witnessed world?
A) Everything is eternal
B) Nothing combines or separates by itself
C) Things come together randomly
D) The world is self-sufficient

Key Takeaway
The world's observed dependence on external causes points to a necessary maker.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on countless things every day—your phone, your food, your health—without thinking about why they work. When you realize that nothing in this world holds itself together by its own power, how does that change the way you see your own existence? Are you truly independent, or are you constantly dependent on the One who holds everything together? What would it mean for you to live today with that awareness?
?
If nothing in the witnessed world combines or separates by itself, then every breath you take, every beat of your heart, is a gift from another. Does your heart feel the weight of that dependency? Or do you live as if you are self-sufficient? How can you cultivate a constant sense of need for Allah in your daily life?
?
The proof says that all changes in the world are caused by something else. When you sin, do you blame your circumstances or yourself? This argument shows that even your own actions are caused—but by your own will, which is also created. How can you take responsibility for your sins while acknowledging that your will itself is dependent on Allah? How does this push you toward sincere repentance?

The Proof from Variation and Evil
Maturidi presents the argument from temporal and qualitative variation and the existence of evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the world's variations and the existence of evil demonstrate its dependence on a creator.

Core Idea
If the world were self-sufficient, there would be no variation or evil.

Maturidi continues with a second argument. He reasons that if the world were a self-existent being, it would not have one time or quality more suitable than another. Yet we see constant change and diversity. Moreover, if everything could choose its own best state, evil would not exist. The presence of evil and variation thus proves that the world is not self-sufficient but relies on an external maker.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage once to get the overall argument, then study the literal translation.
Note the conditional structure: 'law kāna...'
2
Understand the first conditional
Maturidi says: if the world were by itself, no time, state, or quality would be more appropriate. Why would that be?
A self-sufficient being would not be constrained by external conditions.
3
Observe the actual situation
We see variation in times, states, and qualities. How does this disprove the hypothesis?
4
Understand the second conditional about evil
Maturidi argues that if everything were self-sufficient, it would choose the best, so evil would not exist. But evil exists, so things are not self-sufficient.
5
Draw the conclusion
Both lines of reasoning lead to the same conclusion: the world is by another.

Proof from Living Beings' Inability to Create or Repair
Maturidi argues that living beings' inability proves dependence on a Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the inability of living beings to create their own kind or repair themselves at the peak of their strength points to their dependence on an external Creator.

Core Idea
Living beings cannot create their like, proving they are not self-sufficient.

Maturidi presents a concise proof from the observable world. He divides the world into living and non-living, then argues that every living being, even at its peak strength, cannot produce its own species or fix its own damage. This indicates a need for an external agent, thus pointing to the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Let the author's own words shape your understanding before interpreting.
2
Understand the division
Notice that Maturidi divides the world into two types: living (حي) and dead (ميت). This classification is the starting point of the proof.
The Arabic word 'فيت' is likely a variant for 'ميت' (dead).
3
Grasp the argument about living beings
The passage states that every living being, at the time of its strength and perfection, is ignorant of creating its like and repairing its own corruption. This means living things cannot originate their own species or heal themselves autonomously.
The word 'جاهل' here implies inability, not just unawareness.
4
Extend to the dead
If living beings are dependent on another, then non-living (dead) things are even more obviously dependent. This strengthens the conclusion that the entire world exists by a Creator.

Source Text

And also that the world is of two types: living and dead. Every living being is initially ignorant of creating its like and repairing what has been corrupted from it at the time of its strength and perfection. So it is established that it was by another, and the dead is even more deserving of that.

Commentary
This is a proof from the inability of living beings. The world consists of living and non-living (dead). This indicates that the living being depends on an external agent. If the living is dependent, then the non-living is even more obviously dependent. Therefore, the entire world must be by another (a creator).

Proof from Interdependence of Substance and Accident
Maturidi argues that substances and accidents depend on each other, so the world cannot be self-sufficient.

Core Idea
Substances and accidents rely on each other, so the world cannot be independent.

Imam Maturidi presents a concise but profound argument for the existence of God based on the interconnection of substances and accidents. By examining the relationship between physical entities (a'yan) and their transient properties (a'rad), he shows that neither can exist without the other. This mutual dependence points to a creator who brings the whole into being.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the terms 'ayn and 'arad.
The Arabic may have unclear parts due to OCR; focus on the clear meaning.

Identify the two key claims

3
Draw the conclusion

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أن العالم لا يخلو كل عين منه إلى ما يحتمله من الأمراء اض قهرا ، وما اعترضه من الأعراض لا قيام لها ولا وجود دونه ، فثبت بذلك دخول كل واحد منهما تحت حاجة الآخر ، فيبطل أن يكون بنفسه محتاجا إلى غير به يوجد ويقوم ، ولا قوة إلا بالله

Since they mutually need each other, the whole cannot be self-sufficient. Therefore, the world must have been brought into existence by another (a creator).

Proof from Opposing Natures
Maturidi argues that opposing natures require an external unifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the coexistence of opposing natures in a single entity proves the existence of an external unifier, which is Allah.

Core Idea
Opposing natures cannot unite by themselves; they need a unifier.

Imam Maturidi presents a simple yet profound argument: anything that combines inherently repelling elements cannot bring itself together. This points to a power that overcomes their natural opposition. Let's explore this proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation for the first proof. Notice the key terms: opposition natures and unifier.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage says things with opposing natures cannot come together by themselves. Why? Because their natures are repellant.
3
Draw the conclusion
Since they can't unite on their own, they must have a unifier. This unifier is Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أن كل عين مما اجتمع فيه الطبائع المتضادة التي من طبعها التنافر لم يجر أن يكون بنفسه يجتمع ، ثبت أن له جامعا ، والله الموفق . وأيضا أن كل عين محتاج إلى آخر به يقوم ويبقى من الأغذية وغيرها مما لا يحتمل أن يبلغ علمه ما به بقاؤه ، أو كيف يستخرج ذلك ويكتسب ، فثبت أنه بعليم حكيم لا بنفسه ، وبالله النجاة والعصمة
And also that every individual in which opposing natures that are by nature repellant come together cannot come together by itself; it is established that it has a unifier. And God is the granter of success. And also that every individual is in need of another by which it subsists and continues from nourishment and other things, such that its knowledge cannot reach what its subsistence depends on, or how to extract and acquire it. So it is established that it is by an All-Knowing, Wise (One), not by itself. And with God is deliverance and protection.
Source pages p. 617

Commentary
This lesson contains two proofs. The first proof: every entity that has opposing natures (like hot and cold, wet and dry) cannot bring itself together because these natures naturally repel each other. Therefore, such an entity must have a unifier (jam'i') from outside. The second proof: every living being depends on external nourishment and other things for its survival. The being's own knowledge is insufficient to know what sustains it or how to procure it. Therefore, its existence must be due to an All-Knowing, Wise Creator, not itself.

Key Terms
طبائع متضادة (opposing natures)
جامع (unifier)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) That things can unite by themselves
B) That there must be an external unifier
C) That opposites do not exist
D) That the world is eternal

Key Takeaway
The coexistence of opposing natures in a single entity proves it must have a unifier, which is Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own body, which combines countless opposing forces (like heat and cold, growth and decay), do you truly feel the presence of the One who united them? How does this awareness challenge your reliance on your own strength?
?
If opposing natures cannot unite by themselves, what does that say about your ability to bring harmony to the conflicting desires within your soul? Are you relying on the same Unifier to bring peace to your inner world?
?
How often do you pause to consider that the very fact you are alive—a composition of opposing elements that would normally tear you apart—is itself a sign of a Unifier? What would change if you lived every moment with that certainty?

Proof from Need for Sustenance
Maturidi argues that dependence on external sustenance proves an All-Knowing, Wise Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how every being's dependence on external sustenance and its inability to secure it by itself points to an All-Knowing, Wise Creator.

Core Idea
Our own ignorance of what sustains us shows we are not self-sufficient.

The second proof focuses on our constant need for nourishment. We depend on things outside ourselves, yet we do not fully know what sustains us or how to obtain it. This points to a wise provider who designed this system.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation for the second proof. Note the terms: nourishment, subsist, knowledge insufficient.
2
Identify the dependency
Every being needs something external (food, etc.) to survive and continue. Understand that this need is fundamental.
3
Reflect on limited knowledge
The passage says our own knowledge cannot reach what our survival depends on or how to acquire it. This shows we are not self-sufficient.
4
Conclude the Creator
Since we depend on something beyond our knowledge, our existence must be due to an All-Knowing, Wise Being who arranged this sustenance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And God is the granter of success. And also that every individual is in need of another by which it subsists and continues from nourishment and other things, such that its knowledge cannot reach what its subsistence depends on, or how to extract and acquire it. So it is established that it is by an All-Knowing, Wise (One), not by itself. And with God is deliverance and protection.
Source pages p. 617

Commentary
This lesson contains two proofs. The first proof: every entity that has opposing natures (like hot and cold, wet and dry) cannot bring itself together because these natures naturally repel each other. Therefore, such an entity must have a unifier (jam'i') from outside. The second proof: every living being depends on external nourishment and other things for its survival. The being's own knowledge is insufficient to know what sustains it or how to procure it. Therefore, its existence must be due to an All-Knowing, Wise Creator, not itself.

Our dependence on external sustenance and our limited knowledge of it prove we are sustained by an All-Knowing, Wise Creator.

Proof from Uniformity: If Self-Existent, Would Remain Unchanged
Conditional argument for dependence from lack of uniformity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that if the world were self-existent, it would remain uniform, and since it changes, it must depend on a creator.

Core Idea
Change proves dependence; uniformity would be required if self-existent.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents a logical proof based on observable change. He argues that if the world were self-sufficient (existing by itself), it would remain perpetually in a single state. Since we witness constant change and variation, the world cannot be self-existent — it must be brought into being by another.

Guided Path
1
Read the conditional premise
The argument starts: if the world were self-existent, it would remain unchanged and uniform. Understand why a self-existent thing would not change.
2
Observe the conclusion from reality
The world is not uniform but changes. Therefore, it cannot be self-existent; it must be by another (a creator).
This is a modus tollens argument.
3
Reflect on the implication
This proof shows that change is not an argument against God, but rather evidence for His existence as the cause of change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أنه لو كان بنفسه لكان يبقى به ويكون على حد واحد ، فلما تم مع ما لو كان ملك دل على أنه كان بغيره . وأيضا لا يخلو كونه بنفسه من أن كان بعد الوجود ، فيبطل كونه به ؛ لما كان موجودا بغيره ، أو قبله ، وما هو قبله كيف يوجد نفسه ، به قبل الوجود لتوهم أن لا يوجد فيكون عديما فاعلا وذلك محال . ويشهد لما ذكرنا أمر البناء والكتابة والسفن أنه لا يجوز كونها إلا بفاعل موجود ، فمثله ما نحن فيه . وبالله التوفيق.
And also that if it were by itself, it would remain as it is and be on one single state. So when it has completed [unclear] with what it would have if it were a king, it indicates that it was by another. And also that its being by itself is not free from being after existence, so its being by itself is invalid because then it would have been existing by another, or before existence, and what is before existence — how can it bring itself into existence? Because before existence, it is imagined that it does not exist, so it would be a non-existent agent, and that is impossible. And what supports what we have mentioned is the matter of building, writing, and ships: they cannot come about except by an existing agent. The same applies to what we are discussing. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 617

Commentary
This lesson contains two proofs (seventh and eighth). The seventh proof argues that if the world were self-existent, it would remain unchanged and uniform. Since we observe change and variation, it must be dependent on another. The eighth proof is a logical argument: if the world were self-existent, it would have to be either after non-existence or before existence. Both are impossible. If it came into being after non-existence, then its existence is by another. If it were before existence, then it would have to bring itself into existence when it was non-existent, which is impossible (a non-existent cannot act). Therefore, the world cannot be self-existent. It also gives an analogy: buildings, writing, and ships require an existing maker; similarly, the world requires a creator.

Key Terms
حد واحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi argue would happen if the world were self-existent?
A) It would change constantly
B) It would remain uniform
C) It would cease to exist
D) It would be eternal

Key Takeaway
The observation of change in the world indicates that it cannot be self-sufficient; it must depend on a creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life do you act as if you are self-sufficient (بنفسك), forgetting that every change you experience proves your dependence on God? What would it look like to actively acknowledge your need for Him in those areas today?
?
When you face unexpected changes—a loss, a surprise, a disruption—do you instinctively resist or try to regain control, or do you see that change as a reminder that you are not self-existent? How can you train your heart to turn to the One upon whom you depend (بغيره) in that moment?
?
The argument says that if something were self-existent, it would remain uniform. You are far from uniform—your moods, health, circumstances all shift. Does that unsettle you, or does it drive you to humble yourself before the only One who is truly self-sufficient? Commit one concrete act of dependence today, such as a prayer of utter need.

Proof from Impossibility of Self-Existence Before or After Existence
Reductio ad absurdum argument against self-existence via dichotomy and analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that self-existence is logically impossible because it would require either coming into being after non-existence (contradicting self-existence) or bringing itself into existence from non-existence (impossible for a non-existent agent).

Core Idea
Self-existence is impossible: can't come after non-existence, and can't precede existence.

In this passage, Maturidi provides a second proof against self-existence, using a logical dichotomy. He argues that if something were self-existent, its existence must either be after non-existence or before existence. Both options lead to absurdity. The proof is then reinforced by an analogy from observable crafts.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dichotomy
If X exists by itself, then its being by itself must occur either after it already exists (which would mean its existence was by another) or before it exists (which would require it to create itself when non-existent, impossible).
2
Analyze each branch
After existence: invalid because then it would have been existing by another. Before existence: impossible because a non-existent cannot act.
The term 'before existence' refers to logical priority, not temporal.
3
Connect to the analogy
Just as buildings, writing, and ships require an existing maker, the world requires an existing creator.
This strengthens the argument by analogy.

Source Text

And also that if it were by itself, it would remain as it is and be on one single state. So when it has completed [unclear] with what it would have if it were a king, it indicates that it was by another. And also that its being by itself is not free from being after existence, so its being by itself is invalid because then it would have been existing by another, or before existence, and what is before existence — how can it bring itself into existence? Because before existence, it is imagined that it does not exist, so it would be a non-existent agent, and that is impossible. And with God is success.

Commentary
This lesson contains two proofs (seventh and eighth). The seventh proof argues that if the world were self-existent, it would remain unchanged and uniform. Since we observe change and variation, it must be dependent on another. The eighth proof is a logical argument: if the world were self-existent, it would have to be either after non-existence or before existence. Both are impossible. The Wisdom in Creation as Proof of a Wise Creator
Maturidi reflects on the wisdom in creation as a sign of the Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the profound wisdom embedded in every aspect of creation, which even the greatest sages cannot fully comprehend, serves as a clear indication of a wise Originator and Creator.

Core Idea
Creation's unfathomable wisdom points directly to a wise Maker.

Maturidi begins this passage by drawing attention to the astonishing wisdom present in every part of creation. He emphasizes that even the most learned sages are incapable of fully grasping how things come to be as they are. This very limitation becomes a powerful sign that the universe is not self-made but originates from a transcendent, wise Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on wisdom
Read the Arabic passage carefully and then its translation. Notice how Maturidi frames the discussion: he starts with the root principle that everything in creation contains amazing wisdom.
This is a reflective passage, not a logical proof—it appeals to the reader's sense of wonder.
2
Identify the main claim
Underline the phrase 'حكمة عجيبة ودلالة بديعة'. Maturidi claims that the wisdom in creation is so remarkable that even the wisest humans cannot fully understand it. This is not a flaw but a sign of divine origin.
Pay special attention to the word 'مائيته' (quiddity) – it refers to the essential nature of things.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why human inability to fully grasp the wisdom actually strengthens the case for a Creator. If a human could fully explain everything, that would imply it is finite; but the depth of wisdom points to an infinite intelligence.
This is a subtle but crucial point: limitation of human knowledge becomes evidence for transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : وأصل ذلك أنه لا يعانى منه شيء إلا فيه حكمة عجيبة ودلالة بديعة مما يعجز الحكماء عن إدراك مائيته وكيفية خروجه على ما خرج، وعلم كل أحد منهم بقصور على ما عنده من الحكمة والعلم عن إدراك كنه ذلك، فهذه الضرورة وغيرها دلالة حكمة مبدعها وخالقها، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وأيضا أنه لو جاز أن يكون العالم يبدأ من قبل نفسه بمرة لجائز أن يذهب كله بمرة ، فإذا لم يكن ، بل كان على الاختلاف حتى لم يكن تختلف عليه الأحوال إلا بالأغيار نحو حي يموت ، ومتفرق يجتمع ، وصغير يكبر ، وخبيث يطيب ، أبدا يتغير بأغيار تحدث ، فعلى ذلك جملته ، لا يحتمل أن يكون لا بغيره . ولو جاز ذا الجاز أن تتغير ألوان الثوب بنفسه لا بأصباغ ، أو السفينه تصير
Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: The root of that is that nothing in it is dealt with except that there is in it amazing wisdom and splendid indication, such that the sages are incapable of apprehending its quiddity and how it came out as it did. And every one of them, despite the wisdom and knowledge they possess, is deficient in perceiving the true nature of that. So this necessity and other things are indications of the wisdom of its Originator and Creator. And there is no power except with God. And also that if it were possible for the world to begin from itself at once, it would be possible for it to perish all at once. Since that is not the case, but rather it is in a state of change such that states do not change except through other things — like a living thing dies, a dispersed thing gathers, a small thing grows large, a foul thing becomes good — it changes perpetually through newly occurring other things. Thus, considering the whole, it cannot be without another. And if this were possible, it would be possible for the colors of a garment to change by itself without dyes, or for a ship to become [unclear].
Source pages p. 617

Commentary
This passage contains a concluding reflection and a final proof. Maturidi states that everything in the world contains amazing wisdom that even the wisest sages cannot fully comprehend. This is a sign of a wise Creator. Then he presents the ninth proof: if the world were self-originating, it would be able to annihilate itself instantly. Since it does not perish instantly but undergoes gradual changes through external causes (like death, gathering, growth, and purification), it must be dependent on an external agent. He draws an analogy: a garment's color cannot change without dye, and a ship cannot become something else by itself. Therefore, the world needs a creator.

Key Terms
حكمة (wisdom)
مبدع (Originator)
خالق (Creator)
مائية (quiddity)
ضرورة (necessity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if humans cannot fully understand the wisdom in creation, then there is no order or purpose.
Correction: The inability to grasp fully indicates that the wisdom is of a higher order, not that it is absent; this actually points to a transcendent intelligence.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the inability of sages to fully comprehend creation indicate?
A) That creation is random and without purpose
B) That the wisdom in creation points to a wise Creator
C) That humans are not meant to seek knowledge
D) That the sages are not truly wise

Key Takeaway
The depth of wisdom in creation, which even the wisest cannot fully grasp, is a powerful sign that the universe originates from a supremely wise Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the world around you, do you truly see the wisdom in every detail, or have you become numb to the signs? What one specific creation will you stop to contemplate today as a deliberate act of worship?
?

?
If every part of creation is infused with amazing wisdom, how does that change your responsibility toward the environment, your own body, and other people? What is one practical change you will make this week to honor that wisdom?

The Argument from Gradual Change Against Self-Origination
Maturidi presents a proof from gradual change against the world being self-originating.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that because the world undergoes gradual change only through external causes, it cannot be self-originating and must depend on an external Creator.

Core Idea
If the world were self-made, it could vanish instantly; its gradual change shows it depends on a Creator.

Maturidi now presents a concise proof based on the nature of change. He argues that if the world had come into existence by itself, it would be capable of perishing all at once. However, we observe that changes in the world are gradual and always involve external factors. This leads to the conclusion that the world is not self-sufficient but requires an external cause—the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the conditional statement: if the world began from itself, it could also perish at once (بمرة).
The phrase 'بمرة' means 'at once' or 'instantaneously'.
2
Understand the observed reality
Notice that the world does not perish instantly but changes gradually. Maturidi gives examples: a living thing dies, scattered parts gather, something small grows, something foul becomes good. All these changes happen through external agents (أغيار).
The list of examples emphasizes that change always involves 'other things' (أغيار).
3
Apply the analogy
Maturidi uses analogies: a garment cannot change color without dye, and a ship cannot become something else by itself. These illustrate that the world, like these objects, requires an external agent for any change.
The analogy strengthens the logical deduction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Evidence for the Oneness of the Creator
Maturidi proves oneness via scripture and reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main arguments Maturidi uses to prove that the creator of the world is one: the scriptural argument based on the meaning of 'one' and the rational argument about finitude.

Core Idea
Oneness is not numerical, but unique greatness.

This lesson introduces the foundational proof that the creator of the world is one. Maturidi presents three sources of evidence—scripture, reason, and the world's testimony—but focuses on unpacking the scriptural and rational arguments. The scriptural argument clarifies that 'one' in the context of the creator means uniqueness and supreme authority, not the first in a countable series. The rational argument then demonstrates that positing multiple creators leads to absurd consequences, such as an infinite number of originated things.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice how it begins by stating the claim and then lists three sources of evidence. Focus on the scriptural argument (starting with 'وأما السمع') and then the rational argument (starting with 'ثم ما من عدد').
The passage is dense; reread if needed.
2
Understand the meaning of 'one'
Identify how Maturidi explains the term 'one' (واحد). He says it can mean the beginning of number (ابتداء العدد) but also greatness, authority, and excellence. Relate this to the example 'فلان واحد الزمان' (so-and-so is the one of the time).
This is crucial to avoid numerical confusion.
3
Follow the rational argument
Trace the logical steps: if there were multiple creators, each would produce one thing, leading to infinite originated beings (since the number of creators would be infinite or undefined). Then note that every finite number can be increased or decreased, so no specific number is necessary. Therefore, only one creator is necessary.
The argument uses the impossibility of an infinite regress.
4
Reflect on the testimony of the world
Although not elaborated in this passage, note that Maturidi mentions 'testimony of the world by creation' (شهادة العالم بالخلقة) as a third source. Think about how creation itself points to a single creator.
This is a brief mention; further lessons may expand.
5
Check your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you grasp the key meaning of 'one' in this context.

مسألة
محدث العالم واحد ]
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : والدلالة أن مُحدث العالم واحد لا أكثر السمع والعقل وشهادة العالم بالخلقة . فأما السمع فهو اتفاق القول ، على اختلافهم على الواحد ، إذ من يقول بالأكثر يقول به على أن الواحد اسم لابتداء العدد واسم للعظمة والسلطان والرفعة والفضل ، كما يقال : فلان واحد الزمان ومنقطع القرين في الرفعة والفضل والجلال ، وما جاوز ذلك لا يحتمل غير العدد ، والأعداد لا من حيث العدد ، وفى تحقيق ما يُعد يخرج عن النهاية العدد ، فيجب أن يكون العالم غير متناه ؛ إذ لو كان من كل منهم شيء واحد ، فيخرج / الجملة عن التناهي بخروج المحدثين ، وذلك بعيد . ثم ما من عدد يشار إليه إلا نهاية لنا وأمكن من الدعوى أن يُزادَ عليه ويُنقص منه ، فلم يجب القول بشيء لا حقيقة لذلك بحق العدد - لا يشارك فيه غيره ؛ لذلك بطل القول به ، وبالله التوفيق.
Question: The Originator of the world is one. Abu Mansur – may Allah have mercy on him – said: The evidence that the Originator of the world is one, not more, is [from] scripture, reason, and the testimony of the world by creation. As for scripture, it is the agreement of speech, despite their differences, on the one; for whoever says 'more' says it based on the fact that 'one' is a name for the beginning of number and a name for greatness, authority, elevation, and excellence, just as it is said: 'So-and-so is the one of the time and unmatched in excellence, virtue, and majesty.' That which exceeds that does not admit of anything other than number, and numbers are not in terms of number [itself], and in the realization of that which is counted, it goes beyond numerical infinity. So the world would have to be infinite, because if each of them were one thing, the whole would exceed finitude due to the multitude of originators, and that is unlikely. Then, every number indicated has a limit for us, and it is possible to claim increase and decrease upon it, so there is no obligation to affirm something that has no reality in terms of number – nothing shares it; therefore, the statement [of plurality] is invalid. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 618

Commentary
This passage begins a new section titled 'The Originator of the world is one.' It presents three sources of evidence: scripture (al-samʿ), reason (al-ʿaql), and the testimony of the world through creation. The focus here is on the scriptural argument, which is explained in detail. The key point is that when people speak of 'one' in the context of the creator, they do not mean numerical oneness (which is the beginning of a countable series), but rather uniqueness, greatness, and excellence. For example, calling someone 'the one of the time' does not imply that others do not exist, but that this person is unparalleled. Similarly, the creator is one in the sense of being unique and supreme. The passage then provides a rational argument: if there were multiple creators, each would produce one thing, leading to an infinite number of originated things, which is absurd. Moreover, any finite number of creators could be increased or decreased, so there is no necessary number; only one is necessary. The argument concludes that the claim of multiple creators is invalid.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'one' means the number one, implying the creator is the first in a series of gods.
Correction: The passage explains that 'one' in this context denotes uniqueness, greatness, and authority, not numerical count.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the term 'one' mean when applied to the creator?
A) The number one from which all numbers begin.
B) Uniqueness, greatness, and authority.
C) A countable entity among many.
D) The first of many equal beings.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi proves the creator's oneness by showing that 'one' means unique greatness and that rational reasoning prevents multiple creators.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you declare 'Allah is one,' do you truly feel His unmatched greatness in your heart, or is it just a slogan? How would your life change if you lived every moment with the certainty that no power, person, or plan can rival His authority?
?
Maturidi argues that if there were multiple creators, the universe would be infinite and chaotic. But don't you sometimes act as if there are other 'creators' in your life—your job, your reputation, your own will—that you rely on alongside Allah? What would it take for you to utterly depend on Him alone as the sole Originator of every outcome?
?
The term 'one' here means unique, unmatched, unparalleled. Is there any area of your life where you still hold something else as 'one of a kind' in your heart—a relationship, a skill, a possession—that competes with Allah's uniqueness? What concrete step will you take today to restore His exclusive place in that area?
